THE VOICE OF THE SURF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
/ stood on the dark verandah,
And looked out ■ over the Bay.
The stars were silver above it,
A wizard’s ink-pool it lay.
I heard the creak of a cricket,
A curlew called like a ghost,
It made the silence stiller,
T heard the surf on the coast.
To-night it is low and distant,
Hut clearly I understand
7he message that conies to blind me
To beauty here at my hand.
The cruel voice of the breakers
a fl' rec]ced uiy heart on the beach,
Ana driven me mad with longing
For that that I may not reach.
The sullen roar of the breakers
a j aS r shattered the brooding peace,
Ana sleep has fled from my eyelids.
1 listen for it to cease.
1 % f ,? p m . y ec T s ’ hut I hear it.
The night is still as before,
But through the sound of an army
1 d hear the surf on the shore.
The voice of the distant breakers,
l rorn sleep it is calling me —
But was there ever a woman
H ho rose and followed the sea?
DOROTHEA MACKELLAR.
More Insect
The Life and Love of the Insect intro-
duced to English readers the life-work of the
most interesting of modern naturalists, the
nonagenarian ,T. H. Fabre; and at once it
was seen that here was a man who had
opened to us a new world : —the strange, in-
conceivable world of the insect. Unlike the
naturalists of the text-boobs, Fabre—this
patient, humble observer, whose life-long
obscurity is the prelude to an enduring fame
—took us down into that world and let us
see the insect almost as if through the many-
faceted insect’s eyes. It is announced that
the whole of Fabre’s entomological essays
are to be translated into English; they
would fill a library shelf. Meantime, here
is another large volume, entitled Social Life
m the Insect World (Unwin), crammed full
of original observation and informed with
the sympathetic spirit of the man who has
been called “The Insect’s Homer.”
Here, for instance, he demolishes the
ancient legend that the Cigale, the careless
singer of the summer, went destitute in
winter to the Ant, begging for food; and the
Ant coldly advised the beggar to go on
dancing. It is really the Ant that robs
the Cigale of her food, and on the Cigale’s
death he feasts on her body. Fabre goes on
to investigate the life-history of the Cigale,
and sums it up as: Four years of hard
labor underground, and a month of feasting
in the sun.
For four years, in the darkness, he has
worn a dirty parchment overall; for four
years he has mined the soil with his talons,
and now the mud-stained sapper is sud-
denly clad in the finest raiment, and pro-
vided with wings that rival the bird’s-
moreover, he is drunken with heat, and
Hooded with light, the supreme terrestrial
joy. His cymbals will never suffice to cele-
brate such felicity, so well earned although
so ephemeral.
Another popular belief shattered is that
which invests the Praying Mantis with the
sentiments of a Chapmalexander convert:—
, These attributes of prayer conceal' the
rfeost atrocious habits; these supplicating
arms are lethal weapons; these fingers tell
no rosaries, but help to exterminate the un-
foitunate passer-by.. . .The Mantis lives ex-
clusiveiy upon living prey. It is the tigef
ot the peaceful insect peoples; the ogre in
ambush which demands a tribute of livine
flesh. &
And, as usual, the female of the species is
the more dangerous. This particular female
eagerly devours her male lover immediately
after the honeymoon. The human female is
more leisurely. The Mantis husband pro-
vides the wedding breakfast. So does the
male Golden Scarabieus. The curious thing
is that the new-made bridegroom does not
try to escape.
Of the Philanthus aviporus, the insect that
hunts the, bee, the naturalist tells an
astounding tale. The Philanthus kills bees,
by stinging them at a precise spot in
the throat. By stabbing the cervical gang-
lions she gets a corpse, and not, as is the
custom of other insect murderers, a para-
lytic.
In what fencing-school did the slayer
learn that terrible upward thrust beneath
the chin? And as she has learned it, how is it
that her victim, so learned in matters of
architecture, so conversant with the politics
of Socialism, has so far learned nothing in
her own defence? As vigorous as the ag-
gressor, she also carries a rapier, which is
even more formidable and more painful in
its results —at all events, when my finger is
the victim! For centuries and centuries
Philanthus has stored her cellars with the
corpses of bees, yet the innocent victim sub-
mits, and the annual decimation of her race
has not - taught her how to deliver herself
from the scourge by a well-directed thrust.
I am afraid that I shall never succeed in
understanding how it is that, the assailant
has acquired her genius for sudden murder
while the assailed, better armed and no less
powerful, uses her dagger at random, and
so far without effect To this difficulty I
see only one reply: the one knows without
having learned and the other does not know,
being incapable of learning.
Then the victor squeezes the dead bee’s
abdomen and compresses the throat, with the
result that the honey in the stomach of the
bee ascends to its mouth. As it oozes forth
the bandit greedily licks it up. Why this
feast? Mere hunger? No. It is an example
of that most beautiful of feelings, the tender
maternal instinct. In emptying the stomach
of the bee, the mother is performing the
most praiseworthy of all duties; she is
guarding her future family against poison.
The naturalist found by experiment that
honey is poison to the Philanthus’s grubs.
The Philanthus mothdr, though she likes
honey, has found that out, too. How?
The bees must therefore be emptied of
honey before they are fed to the grubs. The
process .must be effected without wounding
the victim, for the larva must receive the
latter fresh and moist; and this would be
impracticable if the insect were paralysed.
The bee must therefore be killed outright,
otherwise the honey could not be removed.
Instantaneous death can be assured only by
a lesion of the primordial centre of life. The
sting must therefore pierce the cervical
ganglions.... Here the sting is inserted in a
breach of the armor no larger than a pin’s
head. Suppress a single link in this closely-
,mt„ch; lin ’,. and the Philanthus reared upon
the flesh of bees becomes an impossibility.
The fact that a female Great Peacock, or
Emperor, Moth emerged from her cocoon on
Fabre’s laboratory table, and was immedi-
ately caged in by a cover of wire gauze, led
to extraordinary happenings, and to a per-
sistent search by the naturalist for the ex-
planation. That night the house was full
of giant butterflies—lovers brought by some
mysterious instinct to the new-born female.
There was 40 of these would-be husbands.
Fabre, delighted, set to work to discover
what lure and what organ of sense could
have brought these great moths through
darkness and distance to the cage of the
butterfly woman. He removed antennae and
other likely sense organs, but found no solu-
tion of the miracle.
. With a view to mating, the sole end of
its life, the great moth is endowed with a
marvellous prerogative. It has the power to
discover the object of its desire in spite of
distance, in spite of obstacles. A few hours,
for two or three nights, are given to its
search, its nuptial flights. If it cannot pro-
nt by them, all is ended; the compass fails,
the lamp expires. The Great Peacock exists
as a butterfly only to perpetuate itself. It
knows nothing of food. While so many others,
joyous banqueters, fly from flower to flower,
unrolling their spiral trunks to plunge them
into _ honeyed blossoms, this incomparable
ascetic, completely freed from the servitude
ot the stomach, has no means of restoring
its strength. Not a sip of honey can ever
enter its stomach. The Great Peacock re-
nounces the joys of the palate; but with
them it surrenders long life. Two or three
nights—just long enough to allow the couple
to meet and mate —and all is over; the great
butterfly is dead.
So Fabre investigates the means by which
the female lures the male. Sound? Does
she emit vibrations of such delicacy or rapid-
ity that only the most sensitive microphone
could apppreciate them? But the warning
reaches the males at distances of some thou-
sands of yards. Smell? Does the female
emit effluvia analogous to odor, imperceptible
to us, yet capable of stimulating a far more
sensitive sense organ than ours? Fabre
saturated his room with a choice assortment
of vile odors, in the hope of masking the
odor presumably emitted by the moth. The
males penetrated those concerted odors
of a gasworks, a perfumery, a petroleum
well and a chemical factory, straight to the
female. Then he tried hermetically sealing
the female under glass. Not a male arrived.
He hid her in drawers and in cupboards.
The males beat with their wings at the
doors. And the visitors arrived against the
strong wind then blowing. Four years of
varying experiments left the problem un-
solved.
Then the naturalist turned to another
moth, and. discovered the love-philtre—an
invisible emanation with which the female
impregnated any surface on which she
rested. To this surface the male moths
went straight, disregarding the female whom
Fabre had removed to another easily accessi-
ble spot. So it was smell, an inconceivably
subtle sense of smell. But Fabre cannot
believe that it is an odor such as we per-
ceive and understand. For the odor emitted
by the female moth must saturate with its
molecules a hemisphere of air some miles
in diameter—and yet it is an odor that a
conglomeration of pungent odors cannot
swamp. Fabre hopelessly suggests that, like
light, odor has uts X-rays.
This is but one example among many in
Social Life in the Insect World of the great
naturalist’s inexhaustible patience and in-
genuity. What are our own naturalists
doing on these lines with the unique insect
life of Australia? Is there a mute, inglori-
ous !^ re hidden in the “Abo.” page? What
has Moorabinda” to say about the nuptials
of the bull-ant? And, anyway, what are
“A. William Cann” and his cobber, “Jimmie
Pannikin,” going to do about it?
Two Strange Plays
The first eugenics drama comes from Percy
Mack-aye, author of a number of interesting
plays, including Sappho and Phaon, The
Scarecrow, and Anti-Matrimony, a satire
on Nietzsche, Ibsen and Sudermann.
To-Morrow (Stokes, New York) is Mendel-
ism and biology on the stage, the cult of the
better breeding of humanity. The scene is
set in a garden where another Luther Bur-
bank is occupied in evolving the thornless
cactus and the blue poppy. His daughter,
Mana, he has educated on scientific prin-
ciples, with eugenics as her god, in order
that she might mate with his assistant, an
equally perfect physical being. But into
the garden blows the seed of a weed: the girl
falls in love with a man tainted with disease,
whose illegitimate child has been from that
cause born blind. The lover dares not con-
fess his paternity to the girl, nor, though
ordered by the girl’s father to explain to her
the taint that is in him, and that will in all
probability affect any other children he may
have, can he bring himself to obey. Mark,
the gardener’s assistant, determines for the
sake of the girl to “pull up this weed” that
threatens to ruin her life. He throws the
lover over a cliff, and discovers that his
motives were concerned less for the race
than for his desire for the girl. The final
scene is smoothed over by the averting of the
expected death of the lover, leaving Mark
and the girl to wed.
The drama is too obviously a piece of
special pleading, though the theme is de-
veloped with many charming allegories. The
garden atmosphere is delightfully preserved,
with Peter, the Burbank of the play, at
work, happy with his genial philosophy:—
Mana : Dear flowers !
Peter: There they are, toeing the line —
stubborn little upstarts, growing up smart,
all bowing “Hello!” to their step-dad, Peter
Dale.
Mana : They don’t look afraid of him.
Petee: Oh, at first, they all wanted to go
their own ways; but they had to mind their
pistils and quin tines, I tell you. When they
were babies, I’ve bottled ’ejn, and weaned
’em, and boxed ’em, and put ’em to
bed, and tucked up their tendrils.
And when they grew bigger and put
on their petals, I’ve picked out their
wardrobes and done their courting and wed-
ding for ’em. You might say, I’m kind of
nurse, priest and select-man combined.
MaNa : You’re their dear old Father
Nature—-that’s what you are.
Petee : No, I’m a plain gardener, learn-
ing how, and trying .to teach my family.
Only here I’ve raised millions of step-flowers,
but just one flower: that’s Mana.
Mana ( hissing him ) : Dear Dad!
Petee : Just so; just your old, useless Dad.
For you see, I can garden Mother Nature’s
childer, but not my own.
Mana : You’ve brought us all up together.
Have I turned out such a naughty flower-
girl?
Petee : Well, kind of a “sport,” I’m glad
to say. But ’tain’t what you are; it’s what
I can’t do for you, that bothers me—when I
think of them. Look here!
Mana : Why, it’s the blue poppy—blos-
somed.
Peter : Yes, it’s blue. I made it so, and
I can keep it so —for a hundred generations:
true blue... .To keep it true, I can give this
poppy environment and selection. But you
- —I can give you only the first.
Mana: You mean, with selection, it’s I
that
Peter: I mean that when my flower-girl
comes to the blossoming, she becomes a
woman; and worn'an is herself garden and
gardener, and the flowers of God are in her
keeping. . . .Poppies need a lot of reminding.
It’s in their sap to hark ’way back to the
old times: to dream —and forget to-morrow.
And as for girls—-There! I’m forgetting
my fruit trees!
The Terrible Meek, “a oue-act play for
three voices, to be played in darkness,” is
the last word in theatrical, as opposed to
dramatic, effectiveness. Charles Rann Ken-
nedy, author of The Servant in the House,
gets his thrill by conventional methods of
lighting, but it certainly is a legitimate
thrill. The curtain rises upon inky dark-
ness, and the voice of an unseen woman
weeping. Follows a conversation between
the educated voice of an army captain and
the Cockney tones of a soldier. From the
talk the audience gathers that a malefactor
has been executed that afternoon, before the
coming of the storm that had enveloped the
hill in this black fog.
Soldier: Well, we done for 'im good and
proper, any’ah.
Captain : My God, yes. We builders of
empire know how to do our business.
However, the soldier has got something
out of jthe execution. He “an’ the boys”
tossed for the criminal’s clothes, and the
soldier got the poor devil’s boots.
It turns out that neither the captain nor
the soldier know exactly for what crime he
had been condemned to death.
Soldier: It’s difficult to know wot’s wot
in these outlandish places. It’s not like at
’Ome, sir, where there’s Law an’ Order an’
Patriotism an’ Gawd’s Own True Religion.
These, blarsted ’eathens got no gratitude.
’Ere’s the Empire sweatin’ ’er guts aht,
tryin’ ter knock some sense inter their dam
silly ’eds; an’ wot do you get aht of it, orl
said an’ done? Nuthin’! Nuthin’ but a lot
of ingratitude, ’ard words, insurrections, an’
every nah an’ then a bloody example like
this ’ere to-day! Oh, these foreigners mek
me sick; they do reely!
The captain suggests that perhaps they
are not foreigners: they might be —brothers.
“Brothers!” exclaims the soldier. “Why,
that’s exactly wot ’e used ter say —’im up
there.” He goes on to wonder what it was
the criminal had “reely done.” “If you arsk
me, sir, it was them —well, them long-faced
old jossers dahn there as begun the ’ole
beastly business... .Like a lot of old washer-
women. Alius jawin’. They was alius
naggin’ the pore fellow. Couldn’t leave ’im
alone.”
It was for something that the executed
man had said : “A bit of langwidge, that’s
orl.” The long-faced gents handed him over
to the magistrate, only this time it was on a
charge of rioting and stirring people up
against the Government. The accusers did
their duty to the public in delivering him to
the magistrate, their witnesses did their
duty, the magistrate had to do his, and
handed him over to the captain, who did his
duty in ordering his soldiers to execute him.
As the soldier exclaims: “I on’y obeyed my
orders. When my superior orficer says:
‘Kill a man,’ why, I just kill ’im, that’s orl.
....Wot’s a soldier f0r?.... Blood an’ ’ell!
I’d kid ’im as soon as look at ’im, yes, I
would, if ’e was Gawd aht of ’Eaven ’lmself!
Not as I ’ave anythin’ personal agen this
pore devil. On’y I do know my duty.”
“So you think he is dead, do you?” the
captain earnestly asks.
“Well, wot do you think? A man don’t
live forever, ’ung up as ’igh as we got ’im
yonder. Besides, we did a bit of business
with ’is vital parts, after we’d got ’im up
there.”
The soldier departs, glad to get out of
this creepy place; and in the grey, misty
light are now seen the face and hands of the
wailing woman, her faintly limned features
“one pale agony.” In her grief she recalls
her son.
“He wasn’t particular, my son. He would
go with anybody. He loved them so. There
wasn’t a drunken bibber in the place, not a
lozel, not a thief, not a loose woman on the
streets, but called him brother. He would
eat with them, drink with them, go to their
parties. He would go with grand folk, too;
gentlemen. He wasn’t particular; he would
go with anybody.... And I tried to hinder
him..'. .1 was afraid of what the people
might think. Like I was blind I never
told him as I undei’stood. And now it’s too
late. He’s dead.” 1
And the captain tells the stricken woman
that her dead son has won the greatness pro-
mised for him. “The earth is his.... The
meek, the terrible meek, the fierce, agonising
meek, are about to enter into their inheri-
tance.” The captain refuses to obey his
general’s orders. He has sworn duty to
another general, and accepts the conse-
quences. The darkness melts away.
“An unearthly splendor fills the place. It
is_ seen_ to be the top of a bleak, stony hill
with little grass to it. The woman is
dressed in Eastern garments ; the captain is
a Roman centurion; the soldier, a Roman
legionary. Above them rise three gaunt
crosses bearing three dead men gibbeted like
thieves. At the foot of the crosses a flock
of sheep nibble peacefuly at the grass. The
air is filled with the sound of their little
bells.” , '
So —-a theatrical trick! But a very effec-
tive trick ; and the composition of the brief
drama is almost perfect in its naive and
telling simplicity. Without the darkness it
would have its appeal.
1 "■ ♦ ———-
A new profession is suggested by The
Bookman (New York). It is well known
that the literary critic —the sort of bloke,
for instance, who conducts this page—has
no say at all in determining the sale or
popularity of a novel. What makes a book
a best-seller is mere unliterary people talk-
ing about it at a dinner-table or in a tram.
Well, why should a publisher not engage
important-looking people to pervade dinner-
tables and trams, and casually mention the
books of that publisher? That is the way
that motor-cars are now sold. Why not
books?
For an excellent detective story—one of
the best in recent years, The Mystery of the
Boule Cabinet, by Burton E. Stevenson
(Dodd, Mead, per Sydney Book Club), can
be enthusiastically recommended. The author
has bettered his teacher, Conan Doyle.
In the collection of essays now published
under the title, Essays in Radical Empiri*
cism (Longmans, Green, per Lockley
Library), the late William James puts forth
his philosophy of that name. This doctrine,
we are told, James regarded as more im-
portant than his own Pragmatism, the estab-
lishment of which he held to be an important
step toward making radical empiricism pre-
vail. The volume has the vivacious style
that has done so much in making his other
works popular even outside the ranks of
scientific readers.

“Montere”: The report of the Commission on the
cost of living in Maoriland goes to show that most
things you buy from a grocers shop are about where
they were 15 years ago—-some are, in fact, much
lower in price—but one item in the list has soared
like an aeroplane. Soap has almost doubled in price,
and in view of the fact that Europe doesn’t wash to
any extent, and prefers its bath dry during the
greater part of the year, the. breast of the Maori-
lander may well swell with cleanly patriotic pride
in studying the report. Some of the fatty product is,
of course, used for helping hot springs to turn them-
selves into geysers, and some goes to the cleansing of
the domestic dog, but these uses for the article Were
known more than 15 years ago. It may be that the
cause of the increase is the larger quantity of gold
in circulation, the growth of national extravagance,
the domestic waste, or any of the score Of probabili-
ties mentioned in the report; but the most simple,
plain, honest, faithful and straightforward reason
that could be given is that the demand is greater,
and that Maoriland is now washing itself in a fashion
utterly unknown to its old father, Who came Out
from England.

What Anthony had given £270 for, in 1839, in 1872

Proem always lived in Melbourne. The first Mrs.
ordern (she died at the Darling Point house of
Anthony .No. 2), was 79 years of age at her passing
out. This would make her 29 on first landing in
Sydney. Ten years ago, there were in Sydney four
distinct firms of Horderns. First there were Edward
Hordern and Sons, who advertised that they were the
original firm of the name, established in 1823. (This
would be correct, hs Anthony and Lebbeus left the
parent .counter in King-street to open on the spot
.where Edward Hordern was in business in 1900.)
Then there was Anthony Hordern and Sons, Hay-
market, where Anthony No. 2 had set up his counter
on the dissolution of partnership with? his brother
Lebbeus. After that there was Percy Hordern,
draping busily on the corner of Swan-street, next to
Edward. The last was Cecil Hordern, who had been
in business in York-street, but, in 1900, was ill the
Strand, the arcade which tunned off George-street;
iust past King-street. There are but two firms of the
name now in business—the ‘Universal Providers’, and
Hordern Brothers, of Pitt-street. The genesis of the
last-named firm Was in the year. 1868, wilt* the lat'e

“Montalex”: A Sydney pharmacist has recently
been kicking against the custom Some doctors have
of giving nrescriptiong ih cipher, and thus compelling
the patient to, have bis poison made up. by a particu-
lar chemist who is, “in the know.” The ".dodge isn’t
a new one. A good, many years ago, in Melbourne, I
received a prescription couched in apparently mean-
ingless words and sign?. I obiected, on the" ground
that I aiwavs liked to know what I was putting into
mv stomach. “Pooh!” said the medico, “you evi-
dently don’t understand Latin.” This gratuitous as-
sumption moved me to sarcasm. “Oh. yes,’’ I said,
“1 understand even doctors’ Latin. But this isn’t
Latin, and these are not the recognised quantity-
signs, Kindly re-write the thing in the ordinarv
wav.” He did. I don’t know what he thought, hut
he looked hvdrocvanic acid.
THE WORM TURNS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Upon the appearance of two ■creditors, who Were
accompanied by a bailiff and a judicial officer, the
infuriated man attacked the party, killing both
the creditors and severely wounding the Others.
“■Act of debtor, chronicled by cable.
How oft in dreams my soul hath Craved this thing,
Vainly desirous, when I heard the ring
Of duns at doorbell, and was forced to “part,”
Coin in my hand-, and murder in my heart!
And now a glorious leader points the Way;
Bohemia’s haunts are shrill with joy to-day.
The •Dun is mortal. Yea, he can be slain!
(Fill up the bedroom-jug with beer again!)
Let the Collector tremble in his lair;
Let the weird whiskered Bailiff well beware;
Let the Time-payment fiend howl words profane.
And run and hide him in the nearest drain l
Brethren, the Revolution is at handl
Up, up, to smite our foe throughput life land!
Fall, knife in hand, on all the breed of Duns,
And rid the world of these Accursed ’Ones).
Blokes, to your axes'! Let the blades bfe keen!
Strike, white the glorious memory green
Of one Anon.—our Valia'ht eWiel, our caliph—
Who Ik’Med Ptoo 'diitrtb, Hrtk mangled half a Bailijff.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Spring Song
The winds of Spring are brisker
Where warm, and soft, and thin
They stir the wide green whisker
Upon the mountain’s chin ;
Through grass and fern they flutter
Where scented blossoms strew
The fields where cows make butter
And I make love to you.
Excuse my baldly stating
The patent fact in rhyme,
The little birds are mating
And it is loving-time. _
In words sincere and simple,
And with a tongue that trips,
I sinv the laughing dimple
One inch below your lips.
We walk in shining weather,
The glad sun goes to bed,
Nor stops to ask us whether
We like the West done red;
Before his light is hid, he
Flings roses down the way
Where oldest maids grow giddy,
And youngest girls are gay.
He knows that still he misses
The best part of the fun,
The warmest kind of kisses
From night’s first hour are won :
The yellow lustre lingers,
One star begins to blink,
And I have ten slim fingers
To hold, whose hue is pink.
The moon shall follow" after—
A heedless queen is she—
Tossing her yellow laughter
Around our kindest tree;
The planets gaily swinging
Their soft, white way, shall take,
Nor mark the fitful singing
Our happy pulses make
Then as the airs grow crisper
And hurry by with haste,
My circling arm shall whisper
Soft to your waist;
And where the lights are gleaming
We two on loving feet
Shall wander fact-ward dreaming
Of something good to eat.
Excuse my baldly stating
The simple truth in rhyme,
That e’en when birds are mating
There is a supper time:
Love hath her little fishes
With frizzled tails upcurled,
And Spring in pleasant dishes
Makes gladness through the world.
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.
The Climbers.
“What a climber she is !” said a fat, heavy
dame,
Referring to smart Mrs. Gilder.
“The society papers are full of her name,
And her father, you know, was a builder.
Just look at her now, with those men hang-
ing round,
As though she were Someone —the ninnies!
Men mob her because she is prettily gowned,
And her furs cost at least fifty guineas.
“Gave a dinner last week, and the Sheep-
leighs were there,
But nobody else worth a mention.
They visit her often —they don’t seem to
care — i
And pay her a lot of attention ; I
Yet they mix with the best. It’s a wonder
to me
That any undoubted old-timers,
Whose people are all at the top of the tree,
Should patronise parvenu climbers.
“I suppose she’ll be marrying into our set,
And then we shall all have to know her.
She’s decent, I’m told, though a vulgar
coquette—
The sort that the men call a “goer.”
Ah! society changes in morals and ways:
With the stream, so to speak, we have
drifted.
No climbing was known in my poor mother s
days:
If we took people up they were lifted.
All sorts and conditions of people you meet,
Who seem much alike as you pass ’em;
And unless you belong to the social 61ite
You cannot be bothered to class ’em.
But some are nonentities stylishly drest,
Looking down on mere common lor-blimers,
And some, like the fat, heavy dame, are the
Best,
And the ones she dislikes are the climbers.
VICTORIA.
E. F.
The Week-Ender.
A toy horse feeds on a distant hill, and a
far-off cow is lowing,
And sweet and cool through the rustling
grass a southerly breeze is blowing;
The blue hills wait, and a white cloud
broods, and the Autumn day commences
With cheery rattle of milking pails, and
low, clear chirps on the fences.
Hill and valley and hill again, ’neath a gar-
ment of autumn grasses;
Each far-flung fence is a regiment that over
the landscape passes;
And here and there is a chocolate square
where ploughs, in their work unending
Have cut a piece from the cloak and left some
work for a Springtime mending.
A grey crane stands by the brimming tank
where sheep are quietly grazing,
While dazed and awed by the country peace,
I say to myself “Amazing!”
But Monday morning sees me back —(and
glad to be there!)—on the station ;
For smoke’s, my diet, and noise my drink,
and hurry my sole vocation.
N.S.W.
FOE.

THE BRICK CONTUMELIOUS.
It is estimated that the holding of the Federal capital will increase the demand for bricks to the extent of 90 millions.
If the victim one day awakes to a full appreciation of the ludicrous insult it is doubtful if 999 million bricks will meet the sudden demand.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
tlie Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

SYDNEY, SEPTEMBER 26, 1912.
HIGH RENTS ON THE ROCKS.
The well-intentioned folk who lately re-
lated to Premier McGowen their tale of
overcrowding and high rents in the harbor-
side areas of Sydney propounded a very real
grievance, but made little progress at show-
ing a remedy. The tale has been told very
often, and has ended very regularly up
against the same old brick wall. In fact,
it has become like the ancient witticism
about the immovable obstruction which was
struck by an irresistible force, in conse-
quence of which something happened which
nobody has ever been able to explain to the
satisfaction of anybody. It is admitted on
all hands that Sydney is becoming, in places,
a congested city. That is to say, it hasn’t
begun to be congested as the term is under-
stood in the worst localities of England and
Scotland, but it is becoming more afflicted
in that direction tin a any really civilised
place ought to be. (The worst localities of
England and Scotland have never been
really civilised, so they don’t come into the
argument.) Stories are told of two or three
families living where one used to live. There
are also narratives about four families living
temporarily in four rooms and a kitchen,
which is an unspeakable business, and the
fact that, in the east end of Glasgow, two
families have been known to live perman-
ently in one room, which was bedroom,
kitchen and everything else, is no apology
for this sort of horror. Many of the houses in
Sydney are old, and out of date; and though
there are inhabited structures in England
and Scotland which are 200 years older and
400 years more out of date, that makes the
situation no better. For very many years
Sydney had an antiquated system of muni-
cipal government, and the purposeless mayor
and futile corporation had no power to con-
demn broken-down structures ; so these sort
of tenements accumulated. And the mean
tenements mostly open off mean streets, be-
cause Sydney was built on a haphazard plan
and mean streets are >numerous. The worst
of them are better than the mean streets
which are to be found in some British cities,
but that doesn’t make their meanness any
less unpleasant. Nobody has devised a com-
prehensive plan for the reconstruction of
Sydney, still a good deal of improvement is
being done in a fragmentary sort of way.
Every improvement involves the pulling
down of crowded buildings, and the ejection
of the inhabitants, who, while the rebuilding
is in progress, have to crowd into some other
slum and make it worse than it was before.
And when the rebuilding is finished, with
wider streets and better accommodation, the
improved area naturally holds fewer people
than the unimproved area. Therefore, it is
alleged with great bitterness that the im-
provements are steadily making things
worse.
Amid all this excitement rents are going
up. That is to say, the landlord takes what
he can get for his house, just as the farmer
takes all he can get for his wheat, and the
wharf-laborer takes as much as he can get
for his labor, and the trades union accepts
as much interest as comes its way on its in-
vestments, and the policy-holder in the
A.M.P. Society annexes whatever bonus is
offering. It is not absolutely certain that
rents are much higher even now than they
were before the great slump of 1892, 1898
and adjacent years, yet the rise in recent
times has been very conspicuous. Even the
Labor Government, which is a big landlord
on the Rocks area of Sydney, has been
accused of putting up some of its charges
about 100 per cent., and there are said to be
places where a Government tenant lets out
beds at Is. a night, and puts eight beds in
a room. Also, there are frenzied Govern-
paent tenants who are under notice to leave
because their dilapidated hovels are con-
demned, and who say they can’t find even a
dog-kennel which is close enough to the
scene of the house-father’s labors to suit
their case. So in that comparatively small
part of the city of Sydney which is used for
residential purposes—the part which isn’t
yet eaten up by shops and warehouses —life
is a strenuous business. And in the near
suburbs it isn’t much better.
The price of land is going up. That fact
is realised with satisfaction by the worker
who possesses his own cottage and allot-
ment, bought before the present boom set in,
and by the one who owns a few building
society shares. The cost of building is going
up because wages have increased all round.
Interest is going up bv reason of the cash
scarcity, and the cash scarcity is caused by
the ridiculously large export of gold which
goes out to pay for the ridiculously vast in-
flux of cheap labor goods which climb with
ridiculous ease over the ridiculously low
tariff wall. A very large proportion of
houses carry a mortgage, and as the rent
has to pay interest on the mortgage any
great increase of interest rates is a disturb-
ing influence. Then the policy of dragging
all the trade of N.S.W. to Sydney has
greatly increased the amount of space re-
quired in the city for shops, offices and
warehouses, and greatly reduced the area
available for residential purposes. And at
the same time the wonderful temporary ex-
pansion of commerce ; —caused partly by the
low tariff and partly by the policy of "drag-
ging everything to Sydney—has greatly in-
creased the number of persons—wharf
laborers, seafarers and the like —who want
either to dwell right alongside their jobs, or
to have very swift and cheap means of
reaching their jobs from outlying suburbs.
For these folk very little has been done. The
railway which might bring them in from the
southern suburbs still stops a mile or more
from Circular Quay, just as it has done
ever since N.S.W. began to have railways.
Places like Paddington, Waverley, Bondi,
Surry Hills. Balmain, Pyrmont, etc., are
just as well supplied with lines as they were
in the days of King Billy. The North
Shore bridge is where it was in the
time of Parkes or Exodus. The
tube railways are where they were in
the •timer of Abraham. The streets
are just about as wide as they were in the
time of that cheese-paring cavalier, Gov-
ernor Belmore, save that they are more
crowded, and it is more difficult to get along
them. And, in one sense at least, travelling
doesn’t grow cheaper. The engine-driver,
for instance, wants about twice the pay he
received 50 years ago, and no one can sho\v
that he shouldn’t get it. Thanks to all
these circumstances, the struggle for such
houses as are available in the business sec-
tion of Sydney has become intense,' and
rents have gone up—they have gone up to
exactly the figure that the person who wants
the house is willing to give rather than do
without the house. If rents are compul-
sorily reduced, as is proposed, then the
landlord will have less rent but more tenants
to choose from. And as he will doubtless
give the preference to the one who looks like
doing the premises least damage, and will
consider an elderly, childless philosopher as
more desirable at the price than a wharf
laborer with a large family—well, a com-
pulsory reduction of rents may not greatly
improve the situation for the present in-
habitants. In fact, it may lead to general
ejection of the present inhabitants.
* * * *
One method of dealing with the case is by
building tenement houses in narrow streets
—by putting families in one- and two-
roomed cells, and piling four or five fami-
lies on top of each other, and packing the
beehives close together. That is the Eng-
lish and Scotch method. It solves the diffi-
culty of the man who wants to live close to
his work by making him not so much a man
as one of the lower animals —except, of
course, when he is a cheap attic tenant, and
then he is one of the higher or sky-towering
animals. (In a geographical sense the lower
animal lives in the cellar.) By the east-
end-of-Glasgow methods fully 100,000 people
could be accommodated on the Rocks area of
Sydney. It is a simple device, but it isn’t
desirable. The other method is to make
transit so amazingly cheap and swift and
abundant that the worker could live twice
or three times as far from his job as at pre-
sent, without being ruined by loss of time
and railway fares. If railways could be
built at about half or one-third the present
cost, and coal was ss. a ton, and engine-
drivers could be hired at 4s. or ss. per day.
and other railway servants were paid in pro-
portion, this proposition would be worth
considering. As these things are impossible
as well as undesirable the proposition
doesn’t amount to much. To put the mat-
ter shortly, until man learns how to fly in a
rapid, reliable fashion at about one-eightb
of a penny per mile, there is no hope of
creating a big industrial and commercial
city in which the people can live under
cheap and healthy and civilised conditions.
A big city of the sort would cover so much
ground that the man on the fringe of things
would be outside the industrial hunt. The
struggle for room near the centre and along-
side the job is bound to produce unwhole-
some congestion and rack-renting under pre-
sent conditions. If the State takes over the
areas where the trouble is worst and decides
to prevent crowding and rack-renting, then
the coveted central position alongside the
Job will presumably go by favor instead of
by auction —perhaps by priestly favor, or
perhaps, by political favor, or possibly by
the accident of relationship to some Barn-
acle or Stiltstalking in office. That will
be little better for the person who is in and
quite as bad for the one who is out.
A proposition which has been mentioned
once or twice, and which might be alluded
to again, is that the State —-pending the
time when the ideal flying machine will
enable the worst-paid workman to reach his
job at. nominal cost from a distance of 10
miles inside a quarter of an hour —should
lay itself out to grow half a dozen or more
moderate sized commercial and industrial
cities instead of one bloated monster and a
wilderness. In six or a dozen cities of
-00,000 people apiece, it is possible for
every family to have a decent dwelling and
at least a scrap of garden, and yet the most
remote citizen can get to his place of toil in
reasonable time and without selling his fur-
niture to pay for travelling expenses. There-
fore, four families are not driven to dwell
in a five-roomed cottage in the middle of
the crush, nor does anybody offer an insane
rent for a mere kennel or shed or coal-
scuttle. But when there is only one city
and it contains 1,200,000 or 2,400,000
people things begin to happen—congestion,
rack-renting, disease and other troubles.
\\ hy the McGowen Government should
have such a boundless desire to drag all
commerce and industry to the private-land-
lord-owned city of Sydney, and thus create
over-crowding, dirt, ill-health, breathless-
ness, high rents and misery, when it could
relieve the pressure, and make a great
revenue, and do honor to its alleged prin-
ciples, by creating half a dozen State-owned
cities at other ports, no one has been able to
find out. Certainly no one can find out by
asking the McGowen Government. For ail
the answer that can be extracted from that
dreary conglomeration of Tory stodge one
might as well ask the dead.
THE CANONISATION OF THE
TRUST.
On Friday last, the High Court (consisting
of C. J. Griffith and Justice Barton, Jus-
tice O’Connor being away ill) delivered
judgment in the Vend Appeal. The deci-
sion amounts in effect, to a charter of lib-
erty of the fullest description to Trusts.
They may all come now, and do practically
what they please, and no one may say them
nay. The portentous judgment delivered
by Justice Isaacs was declared, last week,
to be bad from end to end, and from top to
bottom, and likewise sideways. The Coal
Vend and the Shipping Combine were pro-
nounced to be “laudable” institutions. The
High Court found that though these Trusts
had combined to raise prices as against the
public, though they had,, at times, supplied
inferior coal, and though they had brought
financial and every other sort of ruin on
those who would not join with them in their
scheme to keep up the cost of coal, yet there
was nothing “obviously unreasonable” in
their actions. Justice Isaacs, in inferring
that the prices fixed by the Vend were exces-
sive, was wrong. Who can decide, in a
trade mattter of this sort —containing, as it
does, thousands of ramifications and side-
lights and blind alleys and other strange
commercial mysteries—that any Vend is act-
ing unjustly because it elects to put its own
price on the goods it has to offer? Justice
Isaacs, in finding that would-be consumers
of coal had been so starved of the
commodity that the public suffered,
was in error. Nothing of the sort had
occurred in the opinion of the High Court.
In the words of the Sydney Telegraph
report:—•
There was no evidence that any actual detriment
to the public followed from a short delivery of coal
or non-supply of a particular kind of coal desired by
a customer.
Justice Isaacs’ strictures on boycotting
were unjust, not to say absurd. There was
no real harm in the boycott. On the con-
trary. The shipping combine did well from
practically every standpoint to drive Scott,
Fell and Co. into the Bankruptcy Court,
they (the insolvents) being fractious people
who insisted fatuously on standing alone in
a trade manner of speaking, and spurning
the virtuous Shipping Trust. Such persons
deserved to go undeT, and it was in the pub-
lic interest that they should be boycotted by
the Vend, since failing such treatment they
might be enabled to defy the Vend, which
would, consequently, languish, and so bring
more or less of a blight upon the land —
Vends being essentially “laudable” con-
cerns.
* V * * *
Of course, these amazing conclusions were
not stated in the bald terms that are used
above. They were toned down by a vast
and elaborate mass of argument and
rhetoric. Thus, as regards the exclu-
sion by the /end of outside competi-
tion (or, in’ plain terms, the boycotting and
subsequent destruction of such concerns as
defied the Combine), it was contended that,
after all, the public did not stand to be pre-
judiced by such practises. In fact, the pub-
lic could only be gainers. The exclusion of
outside competition meant, in reality, the
lowering of prices. And as for ruined
shipping cos. —well, they brought their down-
fall on their own heads, by their lack of en-
terprise, and so on. Perpend :
It was manifest that the only way in which they
(the vend) could exclude competition was by selling
at such prices that' 1 it would not pay anyone else to
sell at a lower rate. It was absurd to ask the Court
to believe that sufficient steam tonnage could not be
readily obtained in Australia to carry the coal of all
possible competitors.
In theory this is unassailable almost; in
practise every business man knows it
to be unadulterated flapdoodle and bosh.
Take the case of a trader who wanted 4000
or 5000 tons of coal, and ..didn’t feel dis-
posed to buy it from the Trust. In the first
place, he could only get it from a non-Vend
mine. There are very few mines of this
class in Australia, and they might be unable
to supply the sort of coal that was needed.
Assuming the coal was available, he could,
no doubt, “readily obtain steam tonnage” to
bear it to its destination. But it would
bring him face to face with two alternatives ;
viz. (1), he might charter a steamer ex-
pressly for the trip, and go bankrupt that
way; or (2), he might start in the shipping
business, on his own account, be mercilessly
undercut, as Scott, Fell and Co. were by
the affluent Vend, and so reach the Insol-
vency Court by a different route. Theoreti-
cally, anyone may compete successfully with
any Trust. The writer, for example, might
go over to America to-morrow, buy a few oil-
wells, purchase a strip of land, furrow it
with pipe lines and butt in against the
wealth and might of the Standard Oil,
people. The only thing that would hamper
him in such an enterprise would be the un-
willingness of th'e Standard Oil Co. to per-
mit him to survive. Prices might fall in the
neighborhood of his venture for a week, or a
month ; the public would benefit for a frac-
tional space of time ; and then he would go
under, and prices would leap to their old
mark, and possibly a bit over, and there
would be nought to the general good but a
financially exploded citizen.
There is no profit in considering
in detail the various arguments by
which the High Court seeks to estab-
lish its views that Vends, Trusts,
Monopolies (call them what you will),
far from being curses and social sores,
and extortionate Brigands, are benevolent, in
fact, almost sainted, affairs. The world
knows better. Let the Australian consumer
decide as to which of the two legal horses —-
Griffith or ISAACS—it is going to pay
him to back in this connection by an
examination of their form. The gist of the
Vend case was : Did the Combine do certain
things “with intent to restrain trade to the
detriment of the public”? Isaacs held —
and 999 normal common-sense Australians
out of every 1000 were with him at the time
—that where the public was mulcted of huge
sums by way of extravagantly-rinsed
coal charges; where the supplies of coal
were irregular ; where the public were habi-
tually unable to get the sort of coal they
wanted ; and where rival traders were cal-
lously ruined, it was a fair thing to infer
that trade had been restrained in a manner
detrimental to the public. And since those
disabilities were plainly the inevitable re-
sults of the agreement entered into by the
parties to the Vend, lie inferred further that
they were intended —just as the veriest
hedge-lawyer might deduce that the man who
loads a fowling-piece with No. 4 shot, and
stalks and aims at and slays a duck was in-
spired by a deliberate intent to compass the
death of the fowl. Griffith, C.J., speaking
for the High Court, scouts that view. He
admits the raising of prices by the Vend
(though he questions the “unreasonableness”
of the same, and inclines to the view,, any-
how, that no one is competent to say exactly
what is and is not reasonable in this
connection) ; he admits the remorseless wip-
ing-out of extra-Vend concerns; and he
admits that there were cases of (a) short
delivery of coal, and (b) non-delivery of the
particular kind of coal desired by customers.
But what of it, lie inquires blandly?' You
cannot infer an intent to injure the public
from any one or all of these happenings. The
intent of the parties was merely to make
money. So long as the dividends panned out
n ll right, the Vend, being, as aforementioned,
a most laudable institution, would not have
cared had the public gone completely un-
scathed. All they were concerned about was
to benefit themselves, and that being so, and
evil intent being absent, though various
lamentable results followed, they were all
irrelevant; and no one has anv cause to feel
annoyed about them.
Bv this latest decision Australia stands,
as has already been remarked, at the mercy
of every combine, local or foreign, which
cares to exploit it. As amended the other
day, the Australian Industries Preservation
Act laid the burden on a Vend of proving
that it did not intend to act to the detri-
ment of the public. As Griffiths now in-
terprets the Act, the defendants can dis-
charge that burden by the childishly simple
process of showing that what they were
doing was for their own benefit. Since the
U.S. Beef Trust, the Standard Oil,
the Sugar Trust, and every other old preda-
tory Combine that the world has ever
known, is palpablv out, first and foremost,
to earn money, the Commonwealth may
shortly expect the arrival of such a herd of
Trusts as the world has not seen hitherto.
The only possible way in which Parliament
can deal with an absolutely demented situa-
tion is for it to starkly declare that, what-
ever their intentions may be, all combina-
tions in restraint of trade are bad. A fatal
objection to that step lies in the fact that it
would make partnerships illegal, also sales
of goodwill, agreements not to compete, etc.
—all of which are forms of restraint of
trade. As a matter of fact, the scheme was
tried in the U.S. It worked so badly that
the Supreme Court reversed its decision and
declared that only “unreasonable” restraints
were bad. And who is going to agree as to
“unreasonableness” when experts like Grif-
fith and Isaacs differ?
There is but one way out. If the people
of Australia want Trusts, they can have
them, full measure and brimming over, by
merely sitting down and waiting a few
months. If, on the other hand, they decide
that there are no points about Trusts, and
that the Court which rises up and calls them


blessed, takes a distorted view of the situa-
tion, then they will be given a chance, pre-
sently, to act in accordance with their con-
victions. The Fisher Government’s Refer-
endum questions will contain a clause
authorising the Australian Parliament to
identify an injurious Trust on sight, and
compass its destruction. If there isn’t an
all but unanimous acquiescence in that
clause, at least, the people of this vast
land will be unworthy of the blessings of
free education and the franchise —in fact,
they will be unworthy of one flat, stale old
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Gas Goose and the Real Estate
Gander.
A business-hike little deputation, which
waited recently on N.S.W. Premier
McGowen, must have left a somewhat sur-
prised politician behind it. It came to
propound the old grievance about advancing
rents and scarcity of dwellings, and though
it had no adequate remedy for the latter, it
had some ideas about the former. Possibly
it hardly realised how extensive its own
views were, or how logical they were, or
how r far they were likely to lead, or how
much interest they were calculated to
arouse. The scheme which the deputation
carried about with it was based apparently
on the McGowen Government’s gas mea-
sure. It proposed that a certain return
should be allowed on the capital originally
invested in buildings and land —possibly a
rather larger one than it was proposed
to allow the gas shareholders, but, like
it, calculated strictly on the original
investment. In ' other words, if land
sr gas shares had passed from hand
to hand, of from one speculator to
another, at an advance on the original cost,
no notice was to be taken of the advance in
either case, and the interest was to be
reckoned on the first investment only. The
deputation doubtless considered itself logical,
for a landowner is, within his limits, just as
much a monopolist as a gas co.—in fact, if
the gas co. is opposed by a municipal electric
lighting business, the landlord may fill the
bill better, in his small way, than the co.
does. But Premier McGowen, who is
certainly a landlord, and probably not a
gas shareholder, seemed a surprised man.
The analogy had probably not come to his
notice before. Of course, if the average
Sydney landlord is only permitted to collect,
say, 10 per cent, on the original capital, he
will, in most cases, collect on the value of
the building only. Most of Sydney land repre-
sents no original capital whatsoever, and
the rest represents hext to none. The early
rulers handed out abundant grants free of
charge—Redfern to one man, and Balmain
to another, and so on. The present values
are almost wholly—in many localities they
are wholly—the increased prices paid by
speculators, who thought they saw a good
thing ahead. Just as people have been
known to pay £lB for a share in a gas
monopoly which represented an original in-
vestment of £6, because they fancied tliev
would thereby make money, so other people
have been known to give £lO,OOO for a
scrap of land monopoly which represented
an original investment of perhaps 12/b m
fees and parchment, because of the same
reason. It was a staggering proposition,
and calculated to obliterate £20,000,000 or
£40,000,000 of Sydney land values in one act;
still it was based on a plausible par-
allel. But the rich Labor landlord-Premier
refused to see the point. He spoke the
deputation with fair, soft words, and sym-
pathised with its troubles, and assured
it that its case would receive all Pos-
sible consideration, and that the Gov-
ernment had its eye on the matter,
and had had its eye in that same
direction for a long time. The problem
passed away in a cloud of words, and the
parallel of the gas goose and the real estate
gander wasn’t argued out to the end.
A Seaport Where They Can’t Build
Ships.
The other day, one J. S. White, a rejected
politician of the Cabbage Garden, whom a
forgotten Government appointed as one ot
its representatives on the Melbourne Harbor
Trust, roused the ire of the city on the
Yarra by telling the officials of the IN.S.VV.
State Dockyard at Cockatoo Island that the
aforesaid Trust would presently be wanting
a tugboat, and, “as it can’t be built m Mel-
bourne, the Trust would be pleased it it
were built in Sydney or anywhere else, lhe
said Trust of which Stinking Fishite White
is a member, has all along persistently dis-
couraged Victorian shipbuilding of all
classes, by refraining, on various grounds,
from accepting local tenders. If it could
get a tug or a dredge built in Scotland or
Holland, or any old land, it would do so; if
it couldn’t, it would go to S’Africa to buy
one, or Maoriland, or Sydney—anywhere
rather than its own city. That is the policy
of which White has ever been an exponent.
Dredges, tugs, steamships, have all been con-
structed on the Yarra —the Platypus, by
Forman and Co., in ’7B; the Commissioner,
by Robison Bros., in ’7B; the Pelican,
Petrel and Penguin, by Johnson and Co., in
’BO, ’B4 and ’S9 respectively; the ij a Trobe,
by the Melbourne Steamship Co., in
’9l; the Lady Loch in ’B7; the
Nimmo, by Forman and Co., in ’S6, and the
Queenscliffe, Sprightly, Paynesville, Ad-
vance, Surprise. Vulcan. Sterling, etc., etc.
The boats have been built all right; and they
can still be built. And yet White, befoul-
ing his own nest, accidentally struck a great
truth. They aren’t built to-day by private
firms. The new State shipbuilding yards at
Williamstown are approaching completion,
and already the management is rushed with
orders —two hopper barges and two dredges
being contracted for, while inquiries are
being made for tugs and small steamers. But
the private firms are idle, and their Work-
shops rotting and their machinery rusting to
decay. All the above-mentioned vessels were
built prior to Federation, under the old Vic-
torian tariff. There was no duty on finished
vessels, but there were Protective duties on
many other items of manufactures of iron,
and the policy of the then province was Pro-
tective. The men trained to build the ves-
sels, and the plant and plans needed were all
at hand and in prime working order, and so
the articles could be turned out at less cost
than they can when the machinery has got
to be furbished up, the mechanics gathered
together, and the plans drawn up
Also, there were no Wages Boards in those
old days, and there wasn’t so vast a dittei-
ence between the labor cost here and tnat
in other countries as there is to-day, iNi< )W,
the plants and the staffs have gone to the
dogs—almost anything on earth will go to
ruin in 12 years, if nothing is done to it
and the sole reason for that is the complete
lack of protection afforded the engineering
and iron-manufacturing industries. H acl
the tariff that Charles Kingston wanted to
introduce been placed untouched on tne
Statute Book, the intervening years would
have seen, not one, but a dozen shipyards
on the Yarra, or at Westernport or 1 ort-
land ; and, instead of making a fuss over one
paltry tug, the shipbuilders would have
been turning out tugs and dredges before
breakfast, and building inter-State passenger
steamers during the day. The vessels /mue
been built right enough, but they aren t built
now. Nor are they likely. to be while the
manufacturer who is willing to risk his
money in expensive plant and the skilled
draughtsmen and mechanics are kept away
from the Commonwealth by a mongrel tariix.
fit only for a people that are hewers of wood
and drawers of water.
The Reform which Gets There in Weeks,
and the One which Fails to Get
There in Years.
It is instructive to contrast the terrific
speed with which the N. S. Wales Govern-
ment rushes through the sort of measure
that really appeals to it, with the porten-
tous gradualness it invariably displays n?
regard to the other variety of measure, lo
cite a case in point, the Bill by which mem-
bers’ salaries have just been raised from
£3OO to £5OO per annum was hurried
through all its stages in a matter of weeks.
This paper has no brick to level at the
broad principle of that measure. Still, see-
ing that the hoisting of members remunera-
tion by some 70 per cent, is no part ot the
Labor platform; and seeing, also, that the
community has not been given any oppor-
tunity of saying whether or no it is yearn-
ing to pay the higher wage; and taking into
consideration the possibility of the electors
firmly turning down any such proposition,
in the event of its being submitted to them
in the form of a referendum —in view ot
these and other things it is safe to say that
it would have appeared more seemly had the
salary raising business been suffered to wait
upon various matters of a greater public
urgency. One of them was mentioned last
week in the Assembly when —
Cochrane, M.L.A., inquired of the Premier if it
was the Government’s intention to take steps for the
abolition of the office of State Governor.
Mr. McGowen replied that both the member for
Darling Harbor and himself knew the planks of the
platform unon which they were elected, and which the
Government would carry out when circumstances per-
mitted.
The desirability «of doing away with the
superfluous and absurdly costly figurehead
at Cranbrook is far more apparent than the
necessity of well-nigh doubling the salaries
of M’s.L.A. This reform is in the forefront
of the State Labor platform. It has the
warm support of practically every genuine
democrat outside Parliament and of a
goodly crowd of conservatives to boot. At a
bleak, cheerless time when cash is none too
plentiful and the State’s loan account has
just passed the One Hundred Million mark,
it would mean the saving of money in place
of the squandering of it. Meanwhile, the
best that the Government can do is to hint
guardedly that it will do something in the
way of abolishing the office of State Gover-
nor “when circumstances permit.” When,
in private life, a man owes another money,
and when he lets years go past without mak-
ing the slightest effort to discharge the
debt, and when, incidentally, he is discov-
ered to be paying other accounts in regard to
which his moral obligations are infinitely
less, the creditor has a tendency to grow
restive. It begins to dawn on him that the
debtor never intends to settle up, if the un-
pleasantness can possibly be avoided. There
is, a powerful analogy between that situation
and the position of the McGowen Govern-
ment as regards the State Governor. Had
the Ministry been really serious in this
matter it could have put it through as easily
and as swiftly as it did the Raised Salary
business. As a matter of fact,, its every
action indicates that it is not serious. The
ousting of Governor-General Denman from
Federal Government House has given keen
pleasure to those who understand the fine
points of the State Rights game (Geebung
incorrigibles of the Joseph Carruthers
stamp, for example) inasmuch as they know
that the next move will be the instalment
of the State Governor there. And when a
Labor Cabinet has perpetrated, or paved the
way for the perpetration of this infamy,
what is the ensuing Liberal Administration
likely to be that it should object? This scan-
dal of the seemingly imperishable office of
State Governor affords yet another solemn
warning to the taxpayers of N. S. Wales in
regard to the wild folly of returning mili-
tant State Righters to the Assembly.
The Crooked Question that Turneth
Away Truth.
Melbourne Age, discussing the ancient cry
for Scripture lessons in Victorian State
schools, remarks that the very men and
women who forever rail against the typical
Jesuit of sectarian agitation are themselves
trying to gain their end by “Jesuitical”
means. The latest proposal of the Bible-
class “pushers” is a Referendum vote on the
question—-
Are you in favor of unsectarian lessons being im-
parted to children whose parents do not object, by
teachers who have no conscientious objection to im-
parting the instruction?
The fraudulencv of which drag-net question
is almost frank in its artfulness. The
average parent who believes in the principle
of secular education at State schools, and
has even a profound disrespect for any re-
ligious teaching of the young in church
prayers, or “grace before meat,” or any other
set form, is not unfavorable to the idea of
other people’s children being taught any-
thing that their parents don’t object to, by
teachers who are willing to impart the vague,
unspecified instruction. Every thoughtlessly
liberal-minded voter would mark “Yes” to a
question which practically forbids “No” for
an answer, and the majority vote of Believ-
ers and Unbelievers would count as a tri-
umph for Bible-pushery, whereas a straight
question as to the maintenance or destruc-
tion of the present secular system of educa-
tion would result in a crushing defeat of
its enemies. The plan of attack on the sys-
tem is masked by the “Jesuitical” wording
of it. If it were to get Parliamentary
sanction Protestant dogmatic instruction,
followed, no doubt, by Catholic dogmatic in-
struction, would creep into State schools
disguised as a thing of no importance, and
the defenders of the neutral ground between
Church and State would be blown out be-
cause they did not know the gun was
loaded. This is what is hoped for by the
righteous regular church-goers who denounce
as abominable the Jesuitry of their Orange-
tinted imagination, whilst commending the
Jesuitry which is under their nose for its
fair, impartial reasoning. Seemingly the
Bible-pushers will fail in their attempt to
get a Referendum vote on account of their
persistent opposition to any non-Jesuitical
sort of voting paper. The policy of the
secular education system is plain enough to-
day (whatever the purpose of its authors
may have been), but a plain proposal to re-
verse it is not to the taste of the little
crowd that shrieks and snuffles for a Refer-
endum. A crooked design cannot be exe-
cuted on straight lines. Where a direct
negative is positively essential, no qualified
affirmative, or combination of qualified affir-
matives, will fill the bill, or vice versa. It
is as though the Government were to ask:
“Are you averse to the exclusion from Sun-
day school of unconcerned children if their
parents don’t wish them to be deeply in-
structed in the relations between Jonah and
the whale?”

A Superfluous Convention.
Dear Bulletin, —The Greater Sydney Con-
vention Bill aims, at the best, at achieving
an immediate need in the most leisurely
possible manner. With the examples of Glas-
gow, London, Berlin, etc., at its disposal, the
Legislative Ass. of N.S.W. had no neces-
sity whatever to fall back on the guidance
of a number of citizens at £lOO a time, some
of whom may have given the question of city
unification careful thought, but the majority
of whom will, almost certainly, be quite
ignorant of the matter in hand. The Con-
vention sittings will be used, doubtless, by
many of the members for the purpose, of
acquiring some smattering of civic lore, just
as George Reid learned the elements of his
Anti-Socialistic creed long after his plat-
form war against the low-comedy Menace
had started. And in the process much valu-
able time will be lost. It is even more
likely—since most of the Convention mem-
bers will, in the nature of things, be aider-
men, and suburban aldermen at that —that,
after months of blither and acres of flap-
doodle, every scheme for the institutmn of
a Greater Sydney that is broached will be
turned down. Now this is one of those
matters which simply cannot wait, unless the
biggest city in Australasia is prepared to
face plain chaos in the near future. The
congestion trouble is swelling in a
manner that is discernible with the
naked eye. Last week a deputation, which
claimed to represent many . thousands of
workers in the metropolis, waited on Premier
McGowen, and supplied him with harrow-
ing details on the subject of the housing pro-
blem. The latter mentioned feebly that—
The Government was as much in sympathy with
the movement as the members of the deputation.
The Government would welcome suggestions. But
they would recognise the difficulties of the Govern-
ment, and this problem of housing was not the least
of them.
A point which he omitted to comment on
was that the only feasible solution of the
housing trouble is bound up with the im-
mediate institution of a Greater Sydney. A
little good may be done by the provision of
cheap bricks: a few more workers may be
accommodated at Daceyville in time: and
there are other ways in which about a ha’-
porth of present benefit may be wrought at
the cost of some ss. worth of effort. But
these are mere sops. The problem is already
immeasurably too vast for them to affect it,
and it is growing daily. The needed reforms
are the, construction of a North Shore Bridge
which, with the aid of a railway, will open
up more or less cheap residential areas adja-
cent to Middle Harbor, and the building of
underground railways which will link the
city with the outlying suburbs. Various Gov-
ernments —Progressive, Liberal and Labor —
have been trying to summon up the nerve to
do one or other of these things for the past
30 years or more. They have all refrained
for no better reason- than that they feared
to enrage the proprietors of the country
vote, and thereby lose their jobs. A Greater
Sydney Council will be in no such predica-
ment. On the contrary. One of its first
moves will surely be to embark on the job
of bringing Sydney’s traffic system up to
date. Which is merely another way of say-
ing, amongst other things, that it will attack
the housing problem or curse in its most
vital spot. But it has to get born first. With
weird perversity, the N. S. Wales Govern-
ment, by its Convention measure, is taking an
effective step to obviate, or at any rate
delay, the joyful event. —Yours in an atmo-
sphere of fury, Henri Singemoine.

A Wages Board for parsons would seem to
be one of the possibilities of the future. The
man who keeps on telling people that this
world is a passing flicker naturallv looks for
his payment in the golden paradise which
has been made out of the unclaimed salaries
of saints; but he needs enough dross here to
keep him alive and sufficiently well-dressed
to make rfternoon calls and attend funerals,
christenings, bazaars, sales of work, synods,
mothers’ meetings and the rest of the curious
medley of clerical dissipations. Too often
the meek soul-guide is the most miserably
paid worker in the parish. He does his up-
lifting and his Wowsing at sweating rates,
and begs desperately on behalf of remote,
happy heathen who mostly hate him, and
local" hard-ups who don’t thank him, while
he himself scuffles about and practises all
sorts of cramping private economies, know-
ing all the time that the supreme posts of
his holy profession are invariably given to
imported saints, and that his woeful flat hat
will always be in the rear ranks. The
lamentable narrow-mindedness and bitterness
which so often spoil the parson must be in
some measure attributable to the way he is
fleeced and snubbed by his employers. The
Sydney clergyman who was recently dis-
covered to be doing the Lord’s work for a
miserable £BO per year is a tragic instance
of religious serfdom. That sort of dole must
mean endless anxiety and cruel cheesepar-
ing ; and when cheeseparing and financial
worry are added to nervous strain, the re-
sult is hysteria and incredible stories about
35 drunken infants reeling out of a brightly-
lighted pub. A Wages Board for parsons
would do away with that sort of dangerous
blither and make haloes more popular than
they are.

Melbourne Argus, forgetting its allegiance
to Private Enterprise, has unconsciously
lapsed into Socialistic sentiment. The
schemes for exploiting the alleged brown coal
resources of Morwell and Altona Bay to
supply power for the electrification of the
Melbourne railway system has aroused the
Argus to a new sense of the State’s com-
mercial duties to its public shareholders: —
The natural expectation would be that the electric
current would be State-owned and controlled. .. .
The proposal, if carried out, will place the control
of an essential part of the system in outside hands —
which is certainly undesirable, and could only be
justified by absolute proof that thereby large
economies will be secured which the State could not
effect through its own agencies. .. .Under no cir-
cumstances should any exclusive right to such a con-
cession be made to any company, as it would create
a most improper monopoly.... Are all the brown
coal deposits in the hands of syndicates, and, if not,
why should not the State act independently of any
syndicate, and supply its own requirements?
This from the old organ ot Private Enter-
prise which snorted, not so long ago, at the
action of the Vic. Government in opening
its own coal mine at Wonthaggi to compete
with private producers of railway locomotive
force. The Argus forgets for the moment
that it has hitherto approved, without re-
servation, of an essential part of the railway
system being in outside hands. Having
turned Socialistic as regards brown coal, the
traitor to Private Enterprise may presently
extend its sympathies to the older mineral
that has become black.
“Curse o’ Moses” : The proposal to remove
the Maoriland capital from Wellington to
some inland position, where a model city
could be established on State lands, beyond
the range of an invader’s guns, has very
much to recommend it, and has gained so
largely in the matter of support that it is
now well within the range of practical poli-
tics. George Lauren son, in bringing the
suggestion forward, pointed out that the
cost of the undertaking would be paid from
the start through the rents to be derived from
the present public buildings in Wellington,
and those paid by civil servants, for their
State-owned homes in the new city. The
Maoriland capital problem is not on all fours
with that of Australia, but there. are definite
reasons for a removal from Wellington, and,
if the thing can really be achieved at no
initial cost, and with a decent prospect of
profit to the Dominion in the future, it
ought to be put in hand at once. Welling-
ton is a congested place, heavily handicapped
by the boots of Private Enterprise, and
full of a certain commercial . odor, or
emanation, or influence, or something, which
gets into the political nostrils and spoils
promising men. It has also an infernal cli-
mate, and must be approached through the
dangerous Cook Straits, while it is so ex-
posed that a few ships lying oft Island Bay
could batter the city to pieces without risk-
ing an encounter with the guns or mines
which are supposed to protect the harbor, but
probably don’t. Laurenson’s scheme looks
about the best means yet suggested for grap-
pling with the question of the national debt.
There is very little doubt that a city, State-
owned and controlled, would. be about the
best money-making venture into which a
Government could well go.. The city landlord
knows well enough the solid value of bricks
and mortar when the population is growing
and expanding. It is in the Maoriland
Government’s power not only to build the
city, but to practically make certain of its
holding a teeming population. The thing is,
of course, plain Socialism, but it is Socialism
in about as practicable a form, as it well
could be presented. Also, the thing could be
done without going to Cohen for a single
penny.
The last idea of sanguine Premier Watt,
of Victoria, concerning the Greater Mel-
bourne Council, is to push his Bill as far as
the second reading stage, and then hang it
up till next year. He reckons he cant get
it passed much before December, 1913, and
it will be the latter end of T 4 before Greater
Melbourne gets going. In the interim he
proposes to stall off the loud demands tor
new tram lines by creating a new and im-
proved Tramways Trust, which will attend
to immediate requirements, and will subse-
quently merge itself into the big Council.
That shows the weakness, of a rusher like
Watt. A seasoned politician would have
made these proposals last him a decade; in
fact, some veteran battlers would nave lived
on the Greater Melbourne Council idea for
a political lifetime—and then left it for
someone else. It is a pity Matt is so
sudden. There will be nothing left for him
to do presently.

The Victorian Government recently closed
down on Private Enterprise in the matter
of cutting piles and poles in the State forests
about Heathcote and Bendigo. The contract
timber-getters were chased off the reserves,
and men were employed by the Forestry De-
partment to cut piles and poles for both
Federal and State purposes. The persons
who had been hunted away then began to
make a row, and the noise has been increas-
ing ever since, until the tumult has become
like that of the Goths descending on Rome,
armed with bagpipes and kerosene tins., lhe
move on the part of Watt and Co. is sup-
posed to be driving trees and other capital
out of the country at a great rate, and before
long it may be bursting up the
marriage tie or doing any horrible
old thing. Still, the Government is
paving higher wages than Private. En-
terprise did, and .getting its timber
at a lower rate than it used to do. If the
