Private Enterprise Gets a jLeg-rup.
As might have been expected, the Private
Enterprise of Victoria has communed with
the Watt Ministry, and, perceiving that
that conglomeration has a new milch cow
“coming in,” in a manner of speaking, has
arranged to skim off for itself the cream of
the beast’s yield. In this case the milch cow
is the supply of power for the about-to-be-
electrified suburban railway system. Watt
and Co. have just decided to build a power
station at Yarraville, capable of supplying
barely 25 per cent, of the electric force re-
quired, and to obtain the other 75 per cent,
from Private Enterprise—in this instance
the Victorian Minerals Development Co.,
which proposes to work the brown coal de-
posits at Morwell, and the Altona brown coal
collieries, at Altona Bay. Not only are
Watt and Co. thus —er —kind to their Pri-
vate Enterprise friends, but they propose to
take their regular daily supplies from them,
and to use the State’s power-house product
only at busy times of the day, and when an
abnormal rush of traffic occurs. As the
Age nicely puts it :
This arrangement ensures that for normal traffic
practically the whole of the power will be drawn
from the private stations ; the outside firms will thus
be enabled to sell power at an exceptionally cheap
rate, as with a steady output they are freed from the
usual expense of plant to meet fluctuating demand.
It is a charming piece of special pleading,
and the grocery firm director who abandoned
to his competitors his staple lines—tea,
coffee, sugar, etc.—and stuck solely to side
lines like biscuits, arrowroot and so forth,
might talk just as seductively to his share-
holders ; but the chances are they would
heave him out notwithstanding. As to the
price to be paid for the brown coal product,
Watt says nothing, but as, under the condi-
tions stated, the spasmodic State-produced
power cannot hope to be as cheap as that of
the regularly employed companies, the latter
can charge a comparatively high price, make
a good profit, and still strut around as. a
gaudy example of the superiority of Private
Enterprise over State undertakings. An-
other matter that Watt has carefully re-
frained from explaining is why he, though
acting under the Immediate guidance of ex-
pert Merz, yet rejected Mekz’s proposal for
generating all the power required at Yarra-
ville, and selected the necessarily costly plan
of obtaining the article from three different
sources. There is no earthly reason why
the State shouldn’t build a power-house at
Yarraville, large enough to supply, not only
the suburban railway requirements, but pri-
vate consumers as well. Merz recommended
that, and what was the use of paying Merz
for his advice, and then passing it into the
disused tank in the back lane?
“Bows” lodges a strenuous indictment: —
I see in the S. M. Herald (10/9/’l2) that Com-
mander Brewis, R.N.R., an imported lighthouse ex-
pert, and Captain Hacking, Superintendent of the
N.S.W. Navigation Department, have returned from
their inspection of the: lighthouses of N.S.W., and are
able to report that “everything is satisfactory.” 1
presume that verdict may be taken to include Port
Kembla < where the eastern breakwater (lacking so
much as one ordinary two-candle-power warning
lamp) was responsible the other night for the wreck
of the Camboyne. The experts who could find no-
thing to kick against in connection with the un-
lighted ■ eastern and northern breakwaters of Port
Kembla would naturally approve of the Wollongong
light, surely one of the most ridiculous burlesques
of a coastwise light by the side of all the great
waters. It may be, of course, that the glimmer is
intended only to indicate the end of the breakwater
to vessels entering Wollongong harbor. But in that
case a hurricane lamp hung on a clothes pole would
be equally serviceable. From Smith Head Light to
Perpendicular Head Light the distance is 78 miles,
and if we except the miserable pale-green fixed apol-
ogy that looks like the eye of a cat at Kiama, there
is not in all that vast expanse so much as a glow-
worm to keep a vessel off the rocks. Neither Per-
pendicular Head Light nor South Head Light show
an unbroken arc along the coast, both being parti-
ally obscured by headlands over a large area that is
constantly used for navigation. Do Commander
Brewis and his friend consider this satisfactory ?
What is badly wanted, in view of the increasing
Kembla trade, is an efficient group-flash light of not
less than 20 miles’ range, placed ch the outer of the
Five Islands off Red Point. For the rest, half our
coastwise lights are fixed lights of low power illumi-
nants which would be considered obsolete in anv
other part of the world.
Incidentally, Montague I-land Light is still uncon-
nected per telegraph with the mainland. South Head
Light, through the late change in power, has lost half
its efficiency as a long-range beacon. Crowdy Head
Light, as ever, throws a danger light over clear
water between Crowdy and Diamond Heads —a chan-
nel of the greatest value to coasters working north
and south in strong, westerly winds —instead of
merely marking a safe distance off the Mermaid
Shoal. And yet every tiring is satisfactory! If re-
assurance in this connection hadn’t come direct from
an imported expert, I should have said that there
was foolishness, not to say idiocy, behind that cheer-
fully optimistic report,
“J.D.H.” gives him a send-off: —
Died in Sydney (11/9/T2) “Billy” Whitten
(M.L.C., Papua), senior partner of AVhitten Bros.,
storekeepers, traders, recruiters, planters, etc., all
along the Papuan coast. He was a Cockney, and
came originally to New Guinea (no one spoke of
Papua then). in the train of the first Governor of the
“Possession,” Major-General Sir Peter Scratchlev.
R.E. “Billy” was steward or chef, or stipendiary
bottle-washer or something on the old Governor Black-
all, which was Scratehley’s royal vaclit or barge, or
gilded wheelbarrow of the sea, so to speak. The old
warrior presently died iof malaria, and Whitten’s
story of how they brought the remains to Cooktown
was one of the most realistic chapters of the unpub-
lishable: history of New Guinea. The deceased poten-
tate was laid reverentially in an improvised coffin on
the table of the after-cabin, and the sad rite being
performed, all hands hastened to get as far for’ard
as they could. There was a strong wind blowing
astern. Even then the old Governor B. couldn’t
kick out more than five knots, and it was many a
long, oppressive day before the signal hill at Cook-
town , hove up.
Whitten made liis first big rise on the Yodda and
Gira fields, in the Northern Division, as storekeeper,
etc., and gradually added other irons to the fire
At one time he practically recruited all the labor
for the northern fields, and mighty was the mana of
Hi hi Awiteni among brown brudder. He died, aged
61, after a toilsome, life.

A Vision of
“Leg.”
But, of course, the play at Sydney Palace
didn’t really run off the well-worn melo-
dramatic rails in that reckless fashion. It
trundled straight along the safe main line,
stopping only at wayside stations when some
superfluous character had to be murdered.
Miss Madge Hope, who Appears as Edith
Coventry, the Squire’s daughter, is entitled
to honorable mention. The rest of the cast
may be allowed to escape the hateful ordeal
of having their names paraded before the
public. The plot (its age protects it) will
not be torn to pieces by this irreverent pen.
which will stop here. As the curiously sedate
villain might have remarked to the wronged
and moaning gardener: “By Fenven. sir. if
you were a younger man, I would run for my
life!”
* x * *
The bustling story of “Get-Rich-Quick
Wallingford” is now in its eighth week at
Sydney Criterion, and it is still necessary to
book early. With a lot of people "Walling-
ford” is developing into a habit. A bright,
natural show, full of finished characterisation
and movement, it deserves all it gets. And
it is getting full houses.
Miss Emma Litchfield pleads guilty to the
latest outbreak at the Adelphi—“The Queen
of the Redskins” ; and she is hereby advised
to give it up and get married, or, in default,
is bound over to keep the peace for 12
months. The new productioil is a compound
of the ordinary bellowdrama and Tivoli. The
customary melo. theme runs through it, with
this exception, that Indians (look at the pro-
gramme if you doubt it) are, for the most
part, substituted for the more common
whites. The Tivoli element is introduced by
an alleged nigger, who blithers at great
length after the style of the ordinary murky
corner man. “The Queen of the Deadskins”
covers no new ground. All bellowdramas
are supposed to contain a moral, however
vague, and the moral in this one is particu-
larly obscure. The most likely one, seeing
that .the villain gets in first and marries the
heroine, is “The Early Bird Catches the
Worm,” though it is a gross injustice to call
the heroine a worm —she doesn’t -speak like
a worm, anyway. It wouldn’t be an injus-
tice to call the villain anything—except a
villain. George Cross, as William Lynd-
hurst, alias Broncho Bill, is all that a cow-
boy hero could be, except when he upsets
things in the third act, just when they are
getting interesting—-from the villain’s point
of view. Nellie Fergusson makes her re-
appearance as the villainess, and Nellie
Bramby makes a splendid Wahneetali, the
Poor wronged Indian girl, also the heroine.
The scenery, especially in the final act, and
the costumes are all very picturesque.
***:{:
Sydney Royal is finishing up a fill-in week
of “Camille,” as this issue of The Bulletin
gets on to the bookstalls. Although the
present co., headed by Hilda Spong and Ear-
court Beatty, gives the dreary old thing a
decent run to the cold and silent tomb, the
public has stayed away in battalions, and
gone to the Firm’s other productions, which
are both bright and joyous, and don’t possess
one cough between them. The partially-
furnished auditorium may have something to
do with it, but the present version of the
great five-act coughdrama lacks fire. How-
ever, all the blame cannot be dumped on the
paying side of the footlights. Beatty’s force-
fur style is too hard for the lovesick and tire-
some Armand, and all the artistry of Hilda
Spong cannot hide her agreeable plumpness.
In the emaciated role of Camille, the Flesh
is the Devil. While this Camille is faint-
ing, and gasping, and dabbing her ripe lips
with a handkerchief, the Flesh is signalling
cheerily, “Only acting, you-know! Don’t
let yourself be depressed and carried away!
Look at us! We’re as right as the -bank !”
And it is quite impossible to disregard it. Of
the other people, Emma Temple makes a
quite credible Madame Prudence; A. J. Pat-
rick is somewhat stiff as Count de Varville;
and Herbert Leigh is fairly successful as the
elder Duval, who rescues his precious Ar-
mand. Rene Tanswell (Mam’selle Olympe),
Dorothy Gumming (Nichette), and' Enid
Bpnnett (Nanine)- fill in the feminine part
of the picture gracefully. On Saturday night,
the co. will stage “The Woman,” a modern
play which has a good deal to say on the
popular subject of graft, and is, therefore,
calculated to draw a lot better than the
dispiriting and artificial “Camille.” As a
brine pump, “Camille” appears to have had
its day. At any rate, it cannot compete
with two cheerful shows.
*x x x
“Rather brief, but very dainty,” is the
verdict when the Three Sisters Macarte have
made their final bow after their Japanese
turn at Sydney Tivoli. As a matter of fact,
only the opening of the turn is Jap. The
rest is graceful wire-walking with a Jap.
background. When the scene opens, the
stage is in blue limelight. The three sisters
are seated near the footlights, and the lan-
terns in the background' complete as pretty
a stage picture as has been seen for some
time. To-night, Chinko, the youthful jug-
gler will put away his silk hat, his stick
and gloves, gather up his teddy bears and
depart. So, also, will Millie Kaufman, the
pretty trick cyclist. Other turns which are
keeping the box office from being dead are
Whistler Borneo Gardiner, La Freya’s pic-
ture show, and the Rallis and Wilson Trio
On Tuesday night, when this was being
delivered into the hands of the printer, the
De Serris models began a farewell season of
six nights. All who missed this troupe on
its last visit are advised not to fail to see
it now.

On Monday night, the show at J. D
Williams’ Empress Theatre (Sydney) was
brought to a full stop by a fire which broke
out in a paint and oil shop next door. How-
ever, although only a wall divided the two
places and the paint shop became a furnace
the Empress was undamaged, and before
tlie fire was out the police allowed the .per-
formance to continue. Writer witnessed the
conflagration, and decided that even if the
Empress were dropped into Sheol it wouldn’t
burn. At the Crystal Palace a good pic-
ure entitled “Earl William” a drama of
the loth century, _ when knights were bold
washed occasionally, occupied most of
the attention. “Williams’s Weekly,” at the
same theatre, contained the usual budget of
local happenings. At the Lyric and Col-
onial llieatres the programmes were well up
to the standard.

By the time these lines appear, the large
and resplendent Miss Beaumont Collins will
have returned to the National Amphitheatre
(Sydney) with her one-act marrow-freeze,
or potted shudder, “The House of Terror.”
This turn is the one pool of blood, so to
speak, on a smiling landscape. The other
Amph. stars—-Lawrence Barclay, the Do,
Re, Mi Trio, Hamlin and Mack, and Hux-
ham’s serenaders—are all notably cheery
folks.

The long Anderson season of melodrama
is nearing its end at Adelaide Royal. Cyril
Mackay makes a fine, manly John Storm in
yet. another version of “The Christian,”
which cuts out the hospital and the monas-
tery, but shows the music-hall, and con-
denses the story into four acts, with a happy
ending. Miss Eugenie Duggan is an excel-
lent Glory—except in the first act —and the
support is admirable. The King’s has a
strong bill, headed by the Bartz three, who
do some reckless diving from the wire, and
Wong Toy Sun, an alleged Chinaman whose
conjuring apparatus is of great completeness
and effect. (But a conjurer who does not
talk leaves something lacking.) Blanche
Carelli is still singing, and Budd and Clare
have just come in. At the Tivoli, Nella
Webb talks her unusual American songs
with a charm all her own, and the Stavor-
dale quintette imitate the human voice re-
markably with their banjoes. Those comical
cusses, Rich and Rich, have made an imme-
diate success, while Vivian and Alton, and
the Alginas, supply the athletic element.
Wondergraph is starring “Fra Diavolo”
plus much of its music, and all the picture
houses have got in early with the football
final.. Other’s shows include boxing at the
Stadium, a tug-of-war entertainment at the
Exhibition, a Knox organ recital, a Reeves
elocution, William Robyns and co. reviving
the Omar song-cycle, “A Persian Garden,”
and sundry Scottish pipers’ evenings.
“WHAT WOMEN SUFFER.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Squire was quite an idiot, and very old
and mean;
His nephew, whom he trusted, had a record
most unclean;
He had designs upon the Will, and smoked
cigars and swore;
And when he hired a murderer he did it in
a roar —
Upon the victim’s doormat, in a confidential
roar.
The hero was a sailor brave—a bluff and
honest chap,
In short and sticky whiskers and a lovely
naval cap ;
His wife the old Squire’s daughter was ; his
infant son so gay
Was just a pretty little girl. (That is the
usual way ;
Small boys have too much self-respect to
figure in a play).
The old Squire choked the angel child, and
thrashed his dear Mammar,
Then suffocated Dad with one insanely cheap
cigar.
The villain, shocked by these events,' to Holy
Orders took,
And brought the boresome “funny” wretch
with threats of Hell to book.
The old and loafing gardener he also seized
and shook.
The stage police were “at a loss,” and also
“in the dark” ;
The old Squire met the lady who was “Lost”
down in the park ;
He called her “Kid,” and pinched her ear,
and took her out to dine ;
Then bullied sundry waiters when they lin-
gered at the wine.
Oh! it was auite original! The critic
thought it fine!

The official announcement at Melbourne
Princess’s last Saturday was “Good News
for the Irish,” signifying that Allen Doone
and co. had opened a five-weeks’ season wid
“The Wearing of the Green.” This innocu-
ous play of stage-Irish sintiment begins and
ends in Dublin, or thereabouts. In the
garden of the Shamrock Inn the characters
meet to explain themselves. Their costumes,
likewise the fact that somebody was hanged
last week for whistling “The Wearin’ of the
Green,” suggest a bygone period, yet the
outfit of Norah’s alleged studio in the next
act might have been borrowed from the
dining-room of an East Melbourne hashery.
Two old gentlemen have conspired to marry
Allen Doone to an heiress, who narrowly
escapes elopement with a loud-tongued vil-
lain ; but the gentle Doone adores, and is
adored by, Norah acushla, and an amiable
British baronet is accessory to their mutual
esteem. Moreover, the vociferous villain
favors a union between Doone and the im-
pecunious heroine, instead of himself pur-
suing her with base intentions. The only
obstacles in the path of true love are the
two fine old gentlemen, who say they’ll be
damned if they will when they are begged
to relent. Nothing remains for Doone but
to earn his living as a vocalist, to which
end he steps into the shoes of Miles O’Regan,
the local Caruso, and faces a crowded pic-
torial audience at the Dublin Hall of liar'’
mony. The silent, inhuman audience is
understood to have been clamoring wildly
for O’Regan, instead of Doone, the evening
long. The debut of the novice is a terrible
ordeal. He has backed himself to either
“knock the house” or break Norah’s heart- —
which is the swatest and tinderest heart in
all Oireland —so, as a last resort, he strikes
up “The Wearing of the Green,” and scores
an inartistic triumph. The old gentlemen,
having withdrawn their opposition to his
marriage, Doone takes his bride to America,
leaving the noisy villain to be fined 40 “bob”
for inciting the Hall of Harmony to cry
“Booh !”
* * * *
Allen Doone and his dramas have so little
vice in them that they invariably drive the
present reporter to temperance drinks be-
tween the acts. The dialogues are of the
florid stodgy, or poetic fustian, order, and
the plots excite one like a long-sleever of
milk and soda. Notable features of “Wear-
ing of the Green” are Doone’s emerald-tinted
garments and four tuneful warblings. His
warble to Norah McNamara is very effective,
and his rendering of Ireland’s national bal-
lad could only be surpassed in spirited
declamation by a tame curate singing “The
Death of Nelson” to an aged aunt whose
health was so delicate that the least shock
would kill her. Apart from his musical
turns, the easy, natural, unobtrusive thea-
tricalism of Doone is delightful to such as
enjoy his dramas, and soothing to such as
don’t. Miss Jgdna KeelCy acts prettily on
orthodox Norah lines, and most of the mem-
bers of the' co. understand their business
thoroughly. Some of them may be expected
to understand it better in the hereafter.

Gregan McMahon’s Repertory Co. com-
mence another season at Melbourne Athen-
aeum Hall to-night (Thursday) with Gals-
worthy’s “The Pigeon,” which may, or may
not, have been suggested by “Passers-by.”
The comedietta “Makeshifts” comes as after-
piece to “The Pigeon.” McMahon will
strengthen the cßst of both performances by
having a personal say in them, A revival
of “John Gabriel Borkman” is the bill for
Saturday, and “The Return of the Prodi-
gal,” by St. John Hankin, closes the 6th
chapter of Repertory drama on Tuesday
next.
According to the advt., “On Our Selection,”
at Melb. King’s, is attracting “the squatter
from his homestead and the shearer from his
hut.” When this paper last looked in at
the King’s, it did not notice either of the
persons mentioned, but it observed Billo, of
Collingwood, with his little bit of orright in
the gallery, and Arthur, of South Yarra, sit-
ting alongside Muriel in the stalls; and there
wasn’t room for any more Billo in the one
place, and, if an attempt had been made to
squeeze some more Muriel or a further quan-
tity of Arthur into the other, the theatre
would probably have burst, with a loud re-
port like a rent Marriage Tie. “On Our
Selection” appeals to the great heart of Mel-
bourne, as any decent drama with genuine
local color is liable to do. As Dad, Bert
Bailey wins laughter by legitimate means,
whereas Ned Duggan, as an utterly super-
fluous Hibernian, gets most of his humor out
of the toe of his right boot, and is the only
really non-Australian character in the play.
The verdancy of Ned’s Hibernian belongs to
the green-room, and not to John Bull’s Other
Island. Last week this paper, with child-
like trust, accepted the assurance of the pro-
gramme that Dave, of the expansive grin,
was played by Arthur Bertram; but the
document was unveracious. The credit for
this excellent bit of character acting is hereby
transferred to Fred McDonald. Arthur Ber-
tram is entitled to a bouquet of rather
smaller dimensions, for his performance as
Joe, but any sort of bouquet must be accented
as a mark of high appreciation in these days
of costly vegetables.
At West’s Palace, Melbourne, on .Satur-
day, Joachim Murat, N. Bonaparte’s mar-
shal and slap-dash King, had his life threat-
ened twice by one Perugini, but escaped
without damage to his person or composure.
A Dutch drama, with Dutch interiors and
dreadful pants and hoofwear pervading those
interiors, was somewhat baldly described as
“The Lock”; while a Yankee play with a
nice girl, a medico and an influential kid
was presented to the world as “Lulu’s Doc-
tor.” A shaky subject was “Beetles,” a
trick comedy which might have been devised
by the Religious Editor during a temporary
desertion of the water bottle.
* * * *
Of several new turns that eventuated at
Melbourne Opera House on Saturday, the
best was provided by the Schenk Brothers.
The head and hand balancing of the two is
good enough to justify the applause with
which it is received. Margo, .“the Mystic
Lady,” pleases this paper, without astounding
it. The audience is requested by her to
name its poison, and she supplies anything
within reason—beer, milk, whisky, wine or
liqueur —pouring the moisture out of a jug
of water into apparently empty wine glasses.
She afterwards has the drinks handed round
among the boys, and the boys inform her that
they are. genuine. Maybe the brightness of
the lady’s smile deceives the palate. Other
new arrivals* are Burns and Bentley, foot
shufflers, whose turn is described as “the
acme of simultaneous movement.” Any
motion that Burns cares to move is carried

At Melbourne Royal, a Jew-nosed com-
missioner named Sanballat, continues to
taunt the haughty Romans into laying 6 to 1
against Ben Hur for the Metropolitan
Chariot Stakes, and the wrath of those rash
speculators when they find they might have
invested their talents _ at evens burns as
fiercely as ever. Seemingly, they will never
get used to being taken down by Sanballat.
If they had the least commercial instinct,
they would go and reduce their liabilities by
backing Ben at the longest procurable odds
with some of the other Romans, but all they
think about is their indignation, when they
ought to be trying to make the best of a
horribly bad bet. Which goes to explain
why their Empire declined and fell.

Last Saturday the P’ran Lyric (Yic.) put
up “The Binding Link,” a large and sloppy
narration with heaps of kid element to give
it discursiveness. In the film a baby takes
on the job of reconciling its parents who
have been parted over her papa’s inability
to discontinue his pasts. A good picture
was “The Bank President’s Son,” which,
however, had a habit of stopping abruptly
and bisecting a long and shivery kiss. “On
El Monte Ranch” gave the usual U.S. story,
made up of cattle-duffing, poker-play, love
and coarse language. And a fairly bright
fiction was “A Romance of the Ice Fields,”
though a disenchanting factor was that the
hero was Dan, and not much romance can
be expected from a person called Dan.
It looks as though the same old influences
are going to defeat Melbourne’s struggle for
a decent traffic system, yet again. The posi-
tion is that ‘the Vic. Government has been
negotiating with the Tram Co. to buy it
out before the expiry of its lease in 1916, as
a preliminary to passing on the concern to
a municipal tramway trust which is to be
created this Parliamentary session. But the
City Council, being hostile to a Greater
Melbourne Council (a body which will be
elected on a one-ratepayer-one-vote basis) is
dead against the purchase, or any other pro-
position that will bring the dreaded, though
inevitable, change nearer. Also, the dozen
municipal councils into whose hands the
trams will fall in 1916 do not want to be
saddled with them until they have built
“wild cat” tramways to all points of the
compass. Their idea is to borrow money to
put down new trams, and let the old ones
go to blazes. But the Country Party which
has a big pull with the Government, goes
stark fanti every time the possibility of a
State loan for city improvement purposes is
mentioned in its hearing. The backblocks
are with the C.P. to a man in this respect,
and the bucolic feeling will herein be faith-
fully reflected by the votes of the country
members. It is also about 450 to one
(roughly speaking) that the “country influ-

ences” will prevent the Cabinet Pit-
ting through a Bill to authorise the electrifi-
cation of the suburban railways. In fact,
the chances are that nothing will be done,
and that Melbourne’s traffic system will con-
tinue to be Early Victorian, not to say Mid-
Elizabethan, as of yore; and that the only
beneficial outcome of the present movement
in the direction of improving matters will be
a further demonstration of the imbecility of
the Party Government system. Which will
be useful enough in its way, but not nearly
worth it at the price.
“Henry Horsecollar” writes :
Why throw scoffs at Albert Dorrington for his
yarn in the Contemporary Review about the horrors
of Australia? The Review presumably wanted good
reading matter. If Dorrington had described his
peaceful cottage and building allotment at Waitara
as an ordinary suburban spot on the railway line, a
few miles from Australia’s biggest city—a favorite
residence for business gentlemen-—a red-roofed suburb
with gas and fine pavements, and a good water
supply, and a tennis club, and golf links, and so on
—if he had done that sort of thing, would the editor
of the Contemporary Review have paid hyn a copper
for the information? Not much ! He..would as soon
have paid that enterprising Englishman, A. Dorring-
ton, for a truthful 10-page description of a window-
blind in Paddington or a flower-pot at Peckham Rye.
So the Contemporary Review man got the stuff he
wanted, written by a nameless eitv bushinan of the
worst omnibus description. The wild dogs at Waitara
are just as absurd as wolves at Battersea, but the
blessed editor never thought of looking up a map to
see the location of the place, nor did he consider the
obvious discrepancies of the narrative. The great
drawback about editors is the lack'of the journalistic
sense which induces the good pressman to rush at
the atlas, and make sure that the -Wild elephant story
that some humorist is trying to pass on him doesn’t
plant the impenetrable jungle in the middle of a great
city.-
AT POVERTY POINT.
The gallery crowd at the re-opening night of Mel-
bourne Royal looked strangely easy in mind and
body. They were sitting in comparatively open order,
in accordance with the new Board of Health regula-
tion, which insists on a reasonable breathing space
for each person. Nevertheless, there have been com-
plaints in the press that the Royal gallery had a
“packer” on the job, wljo compelled seven gods to
cram themselves within the spaces chalked off for
six immortals, and who stated that the Board of
Health boundaries didn’t count on first nights.
Which encourages the suspicion that prior to the
introduction of chalk lines the packer used to squeeze
not less than nine devout theatre-goers into the space
now dedicated to six. But the suspicion is beyond
proof. A number of girls who could have given valu-
able evidence, are said to be no more. There was
terrible mortality among Royal goddesses this last
winter, owing to tight-lacing it was thought. The
high death-rate is now believed to have been due to
tight packing.
Looking a gift horse in the mouth at Yorketown
(S.A.), a moving picture show gave a performance
for the benefit of the young men’s club. The propor-
tion of the receipts handed over was smaller than
was expected, and so, on a subsequent night, a crowd
gathered and pelted the manager with eggs. All this
is reminiscent of the fate of Ananias, “who kept
back part of the price,” getting no credit for the
sum he did give. In fact, he gave up part of the
money as a free gift, and was struck dead for the
balance.
Martin Harvey, who is booked for an Australian
tour, resembles the late Barry Sullivan in two ways.
He is idolised in the provinces, but a poor draw in
London. For years past he has been mainly identi-
fied with a character, which is eminently suited to
his style, viz., Sidney Carton, in “The Only Way,”
one of the many dramatisations of Dickens’ “Tale
of Two Cities.” No doubt it is in this role that he
will make his Australian debut.
“Kohu”: A number of fiercely idiotic and intern -
perately patient people formed a queue at midnight
in Auckland (Maoriland) to get a chance of seeing
the curtain rise on the Oscar Asche-Lily Brayton
performance of “Kismet.” People who do this sort
of thing probably deserve to do it, but present writer
wouldn’t wait 19 hours in a standing posture to see
the curtain rise on the Day of Judgment, even if the
piece were staged by William Shakspeare and had
special effects provided by Dante, John Milton, the-
Chapzander mission, Beelzebub and the ghost of
Elijah Dowie.
“Stargazer”: Two or three of the characters in
“On Our Selection” are particularly well done, and I
a,m inclined to think the truest bit of work is Sarah,
by Miss Laura Roberts. It is a
bright picture of a characteristic
bush girl, full of humor, and
without a trace of the exaggera-
tion with which several other
pros, get their effects. . There is
some excess of caricature in Ber-
tram’s Dave, but this,' too, is a
clever effort. The actor has been
assisted by Norm. Lindsay, whose
conception of David i$ faithfully
reproduced, yet Bertram spread.s
a sound bush type to great ad-
vantage by virtue of his own
abilities as a comedian. The chief
defect of “On Our Selection,” as
a picture of Australian manners
Outback, is the excess of eccen-
trics. All Steele Rudd’s curiosi-
ties of the bush are assembled
together, and the effect is too
suggestive of capers in a retreat
for the partially cracked.
Phil Rallis, the cheerful person who tumbles about
the Sydney Tivoli stage in order to make fun for the
folk in front, is said to have learned his business
with a gang of Arabs. The governor of the mob
of acrobatic undesirables was called Achmit Ibrahim,
and he was a distinctly difficult proposition. Rallis
was apprenticed to him at the age of eight years, and
saw not a piastre until he was 14, when he began to
get ss. a week. Four years later his screw rose to
10s. He left Achmit at last, and took to the variety
stage, where the gold and applause and other things
were better and more plentiful. During his Arab
slavery Rallis had all the important bones of his body
broken at one time or another. He tells hair-raising
tales of the way in which Achmit, who could tumble
like a dervish himself, treated the boys under his
control. Broken backs were not uncommon, and
those who won through the ordeal made rare per-
formers. Also, those who didn’t, made unusual
cripples. Rallis says there will be no more tumblers,
as English law forbids apprenticeship under the age
of 14, and no boy can learn after that age.
“Private Boxer”: Our old acquaintance, the
“actress-evangelist,” has once more turned up in
London, and to judge by the lady’s “card,” which
I have just come across on the front page of the
theatrical journal, The Stage, the fair Ada is intent
on carrying out her threat of returning to the boards.
Her advertisement runs:—
MISS ADA WARD,
Returned from Australian Tour. Great success
as Leah and Lady Isabel.
Quite in the language of the disengaged pro., you
observe. (By the way the mummeress, by some strange
oversight, has not printed her Australian press no-
tices!) The poor, unsophisticated theatrical man-
agers in England may be led to believe by the artless
Ada that she has been on a grand starring tour of
the playhouses in these parts, when, as a fact, the ex-
Salvationist only appeared in scenes from “East
Lynne” and “Leah” for one consecutive night. All
of which goes to prove that a converted actress can
never be converted from the base uses of advertise-
ment.
“Anglo-Saxon”: Why Nella Webb, the lady who
opened at Sydney Tivoli on Saturday, should call
herself a disease, passes all understanding. Anyhow,
why does a music-hall performer want to use a
manufactured French name at all? The Macarte
sisters pronounce their name McCarty, and are as
Irish as the shamrock itself. But disease. Nella
isn’t one. A diseuse is a talkeress, and the Webb
lady is a singeress. Why doesn’t she just say so in
any old language that may come handy ? If this
Frenchiness continues we shall have Huge Deal
Mclntosh spelling his name H. D. MakintQt or some-
thing.
“Harrison O.”: “Floater” alleges (B. 12/9/’l2)
that Gregan McMahon contemplates producing “Man
and Superman,” provided “he can find stage room
for Shaw’s Hell.” The Hell scene has invariably been
deleted from “Man,” etc., in London and elsewhere.
It is superfluous and boring. Shaw himself once
described it as an “attempt to write a new Book of
Genesis for the Bible of the Evolutionists,” and in
it he gives his, at times, intolerable prolixity free
rein. McMahon would be well advised to follow
precedent, and not attempt to present this dull and
unnecessary Sheol. Personally, I doubt if he ever
intended to do so.
“Ival”: A Bulletin writer pointed out recently
that the hero-crook of “Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford,”
though inexpressibly alluring to the matinee girl
type, would not go down for a second with business
men, however bucolic. Niblo’s youth, fluency, wide
hypnotic smile and gleaming teeth and clothes would,
as your correspondent remarked, inspire the utmost
distrust amongst the money-changers, etc., of real
life. The above is a reproach which cannot be
levelled at Randolph Bedford’s “The Unseen Eye,”
which is to be produced at Sydney Palace Theatre
at the end of next month. Roderick Speed, a' swind-
ling promoter and the central figure; is no' dazzling
lad, but the hard-headed, cynical, middle-aged man,
who is the real pirate of civilisation. George Bry-
ant will probably play the part. By the way, this
will be the first Australian city drama to find the
stage, here or elsewhere.
“Vulcan”: A large initial nail has just been
banged into the coffin of the conventional panto,
“principal boy”—who is a plump girl, mostly dis-
guised in a large hat and nothing much else. Drury
Lane (London), of all places, announces that this
year’s panto, “boy” will be Wilfred Douthitt —the
first male in the part within the history of the
Lane —and that the innovation will probably become
a habit of the future.

ART AND THE BEER-MONEY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We do not desire to be antagonistic to any union
of actors duly formed, but we are quite at one on the
subject of the impossibility of laying down any
minimum wage in connection with Art. —The honor-
ary general secretary of the Ac-tors’ Association.
Long time the beauteous actor-bloke
Gave tongue among the beer —he spoke
On Art, and artists’ recompense,
And thence,
He came eftsoons to speak on his
Relations to the acting-biz.
Shaking his high-browed, lovely head,
“I toil for Art’s sweet sake,” he said.
“I toil for her, my mistress dear,”
He burbled, in between his beer,
And, while his eyes with rapture shone,
Went on,
To speak on Art and what it means.
Apart from boodle, gonce or beans.
To one whose Art is It. “To me
My Art is everything,” said he.
In earnest tones, serenely glad,
He told of triumphs he had had
In days gone by, and mentioned how,
Right now,
As something big the boss had billed
His nibs. His soul was plainly filled
With splendid scorn of those who could
Ask oof for simply making good.
Yet, when more beer came on the board,
And Horace fumbled for his hoard,
No sound was heard of clashing splosh.
“Be gosh!”
He groaned—and as he fumbled so
He glumly murmured, soft and low,
In hurried tones, bereft of joy:
“Oh, you pay for this beer, ol’ boy!”

FRANK HENTY.
HOWLS AT THE GATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Captain H. L. A. Frankford (Legion of Frontiers-
men) wrote: “I suggest that after the resolution has
been put and carried, the whole of the meeting ad-
journ to Government House, and sing ‘God Save the
King.’ This will, to my mind, give the disloyalists
some discernment, that they can .go so far and no
farther. I feel a demonstration of this kind to be
absolutely necessary.” —Fearful outburst induced by
fuss over G.-G. Denman’s “eviction” from Sydney
Government House.
The viceroy gnawed alone,
One dark and wintry night,
His larder’s final bone.
There were no more in sight.
The seaside castle rent
Had not been paid, and so
Some knaves of ill intent
Ha‘d told him he must go.
The ragged A.D.C.’s,
Before the few poor logs,
Sprawled round about his knees
Like old and faithful dogs. ,
Their master hoped for aid
From loyal folk and stout;
But he was sore afraid
There was no help about.
His heart was full of fear.
What loyalist could help?
An aide put Up one ear
And gave a nervous yelp.
“Lie down!” the.master kind
In gentle tones remarked,
For he was quite resigned.
The aide rose up and barked!
That roused the other aides ;
The pack call sounded clear,
As when, through social glades,
They chased the fatted deer.
He thrashed them with his hat
Until their clamor ceased;
When they were crouching flat,
The door-latch he released.
Beyond the gateway light,
He heard the hungry growl
Of some beasts of the night,
Then one horrific Howl!
He gave one startled bound
And madly rushed within;
, His cowed aides gathered round
And trembled at the din.
Behind the bars they kept
A vigil fierce and strict;
And if the viceroy slept,
His hands the good aides licked.
They did no recognise 1
“Gorsave’l —that loyal roar , *
This was their rash surmise r 4
The wolves are at the door!'!

WHEN THE TIME
IS RIPE.
A significant sentence in the report of the Young People's Union stated that compulsory training had
interfered largely with the boys’ missionary band, and in some parishes the efforts to carry on the
branches had had to be abandoned. —Pious announcement in Sydney paper.
Peace-at-any-Price-Person : “Hit them with this whilst I make all speed for the
Great Dividing Range.”
fashioned way. The man, seemingly, is so
intent on his diabolical experiments that he
cannot see he is trying to do away with
what is at once a reason for living and a re-
ligion. Abolish the stomach, and myriads
of people would not know what to do with
themselves. Oratory would die out if human
innards ceased to be taken to banquets and
heavyweight functions; waiters, sailors,
farmers, vignerons, and others representing
Agriculture and Industry would go insol-
vent or mad, or both ; and the neglected part
of the human machine, the old-established
furnace, would develop some new and dan-
gerous complaint like appendicitis the
trouble that attacks the little blind, disused
alley that nobody knows the use of. Even
the greediest absorber of shocks would not
be able to develop a prosperous paunch on
electricity, and the sun would rise and set
on a race of servile flat men—wretched
beings with no splendid front curves. The
proud, confident fat man —the unshaken man
of resistless bulk —the man who is fit and
ready to shove against the world —would
nevermore be seen. A certain type of fighter
—the bar*room gladiator who holds his an-
tagonist with a baleful eye until he gets
near enough to shoot out his balloon vest
with the force of a battering ram, and fire
him through the swing doors —would become
merely a wonderful legend of retired arch-
drunks of the past age of beer. The absurdly
fat actor, who makes a splendid living by
singing and joking about his stomach, would
also vanish, and all the fat humor would
lose its meaning. The world will not stand
any scientific interference with its stomach
—the splendid contraption that outlasts iron
and steel boilers, defies kicks and bad cook-
ing, and acts as a buffer against the shocks
of adversity.
* * * *
“Lex” holds forth :
The proposed re-admission of R. D. Meagher to
practise as a solicitor of the Ma State has demons-
trated, amongst other things, the tremendous capacity
of the local press, also “Pro Bono, etc.,” “Father
of Twelve” and other thoroughly British institutions,
to be stampeded, on the frailest grounds, into an out-
burst of snuffle and self-righteous cant. What is it
Meagher asks? The right simply to do that which
the Supreme Court of N. S. Wales explicitly promised
he should be allowed to do years ago, and which
a Chief Justice and divers other good Judges have
repeatedly agreed he should be permitted to do.
To hear and read the views of the rival solicitors
and their anonymous henchmen who are filling the
State with excitement re Meagher, one would
think that he was applying for a bishopric, or a cure
of souls, or something. The notorious truth is that
if Meagher had committed all the misdeeds he is
accused of, in addition to those which have been
officially scored up against him, he would still be
entirely worthy to associate with quite a number
of Sydney Gentlemen One. With nauseating self-
sufficiency they have been using the Meagher case for
years and years as a peg whereon to hang masses of
praise anent the “honor” and “high-mindedness” and
so forth of their branch of the profession. Speaking
from harsh experience I can state that there are at-
torneys on the roll of local solicitors, at the time of
writing, who would stand to be “pilled” at a Black-
mailer’s Club (if such there be) as over the odds in
the way of villainy, and whose robberies, swindles
and other delicts would fill volumes of small print.
For reasons which are perfectly well known to
those in the inner running, Meagher has always been
relentlessly pursued by the Law Institute. Folks
who are his enemies, not on public but on purely
private grounds, use this body as a lever.
The State has nothing to gain, and everything to
lose by making a man an outcast for all time; and
the abler and more forceful the man, the more dan-
gerous it is to render him desperate. An amazing
judicial decision laid it down, the other day, that
any scurrilous person, who felt so disposed, was at
liberty to roam about the country, publicly denounc-
ing Meagher as a “criminal,” or worse. ”It practi-
cally leaves him no redress against malicious persons,
sensation-mongers, blackmailers and the like, save
the revolver and shot-gun which provide the answer
to such remarks in those parts of the world where
Law and Order amount to so many dead letters. In
fact, the decision is absurd and indefensible. Equally
so is the proposition that this man, who erred about
20 years ago, should never again be suffered to
practise his profession. It amounts to the putting
of a premium on all those evil courses which the
community spends thousands annually trying to
cheek.
HER LIKENESS.
THAT BOY (with one of auntie s studies): “Is
this like you, auntie? ”
About a week after it advertised a vacant
infant for adoption, or something of the
kind, under the heading, “Dogs, Poultry,
etc.,” the S. M. Herald burst into tears in
its leading columns over the iniquity of a
shameless Government which had offered Jfo
towards the usual expenses to the lady who
had just acquired a baby in the usual way.
In its advertising columns, the Herald
usually refers to the young of man as an
“encumbrance.” How familiar to the Her-
ald reader is the advertisement, “Married
couple wanted for station. No encum-
brance”! And then the Personal column:
“Earl Blither, who is paying a visit to Aus-
tralia, arrived here yesterday by the R.M.S.
Dashit. He is accompanied by Viscount
Vacant, his eldest male encumbrance.” But,
no! On second thoughts, that news item has
yet to be published. Blither’s expensive
heir is often an encumbrance of the most
tiresome variety; but, somehow, the honest
fact always escapes mention. The need-
lessly unhappy Herald, with its one ser-
viceable eye firmly fixed on the bassinette
containing Mrs. Bellevue Toorak’s most re-
cent treasure, shed absolute pints of brine
on behalf of the shocked mother whose dig-
nity and tenderest feelings the Government
proposes to wound with its well-meant fiver.
Vague and dreary references were made to
such things as “the sacred mystery of birth”
—the intensely commonplace mystery which
has to be paid for in poor households with
unforgettable anxiety and hard unmysterious
cash, and which results, if all goes well,
in the production of a healthy little Austra-
lian—the White Man’s Burden that handi-
caps the working parents so damnably in
the eyes of some Herald advertisers. The
whole tenor of the wealthy old sheet’s out-
burst was that the dreadful subject should not
have been mentioned at all, and that to talk
of a money reward in connection with such r
thing as birth was just shameful. One way
and another, the Herald is a diverting old
wet. blanket. One can almost imagine it
reading its Bible in the twilight and saying
tenderly: “What a loving and beautiful
command that is! ‘Suffer little encum-
brances to come unto Me.’ ” And, again,
“Like as a Father pitieth his encum-
brances,” etc.

“F.C.”: What, you ask me helplessly, is
the cause of “the waning influence of the
Church”? Wherefore, you clamor, are the
parsons and the bishops, and their fair mil-
linery and picturesque practices being en-
thusiastically turned down by the masses?
Well, one reason seems to me to be con-
nected with the Church’s snarling intoler-
ance and Chronic petty-mindedness and lack
of anything approximating to a sense of pro-
portion. To cite an instance, at the forth-
coming Melbourne Anglican Sydnod, Dr.
Deeper, one of the leaders of the denomina-
tion, proposes to move that the Synod “con-
demn the too prevalent practice of applying
the word ‘catholic’ exclusively to members
n i the Homan Catholic denomination, etc.,
etc.” A worthy issue for presumably edu-
cated men to waste time over, forsooth,
when such questions as housing of the Door,
pure food, control and treatment of infec-
tious and contagious diseases, and so forth,
are fairly yelling for attention.

A gruesomely humorous mental picture
can be drawn from the cable reports of the
wrecking of the Chester-Liverpool (Eng-
land) express train at Ditton Junction: —
Immediately after the disaster the wood-work of
the carriages caught fire. Crowds of would-be res-
cuers stood helplessly by, owing to the fierce heat,
while imprisoned passengers screamed with agony
as they were slowly roasted to death. A clergyman,
who was summoned from Ditton Junction, gave con-
solation to the dying, who were imprisoned in the
wreckage, and could not be released.

BEERCHEWER NO. 2: “ The Wowsers haven't

One would suppose that the “Precentor
of Norwich Cathedral,” England, is a very
solemn, staid and dignified old boy. But even
a man who has to intone psalms, sing an-
thems, and keep a frivolously-disposed choir
within the bounds of order and decency
must indulge himself in a few giddy moments
from time to time. This particular precen-
tor has just written and composed a song for
the Boy Scouts. Here is one specimen
verse:—
We are the boys who without any noise
Have to help the old world on its road.
There’s no need to look blue if a scout is in view,
He is certain to lighten the load.
We are made of the stuff that is quite up to snuff,
Though we don’t care to brag of our skill ;
If you don’t think we’re worth a small place on the
earth, - /
Then we hope that you jolly soon will.
THE NEW FIRM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some people deny the existence of a personal
Devil. My reply is that, if there is no Devil, I
would like to .know who carries on his business. —-
The Rev. 11. B. Hewett, vicar of St. Philip’s, Coll-
incjwood.
I stood beside the Gate of Dread in Sheol’s gloomy
wall ;
I saw a placard, flaming red, inscribed with light-
ning scrawl,
And, coming closer still to quiz, through rain of
joyful tears,
Read: “Mr. Satan—Out on Biz. Return, five billion
years.”
“ ’Tis closed!” I mused, and turned to go ; but many
a groan and yell
From out that precinct rang, to show that II —11
was still a h—11;
And, as I hearkened to the moans, close rooted to the
place,
There peered above the wall’s high stones a stern and
whiskered face.
“Whiskers,” I cried, “this shame avow! How comes
this grievous ill?
Who carries on this business now? W r ho tortures
humans still ?
“Who runs the biz since round the coke Nick’s imps
no longer buzz?”
And Mr. Whiskers up and spoke, and said, “We
Wowsers does!
“We runs the whole thing up-to-date; we don’t be-
lieve in coals;
We don’t toast bodies on a grate—we simply tortures
souls.
“The purest soul, the sweetest life, ne’er ’scapes our
fierce attack;
With many a prayer and precept rife we stretch ’em
on the rack ;
“With savage prods and twisty twirls we keeps ’em
on the grids ;
We loves to torture fallen girls and frighten little
kids !
“We blights your joys; we grabs your toys and
brands ’em all as sin ;
The brightest mind with shame we cloys and pours
our poisons in.
“We sears your loves” “Enough!” I cried, and
fled as one accurst;
And yet I feel a kind of pride, for now I know the
worst.
To those who ask, “Oh, tell me true if II —11 doth
flourish still?”
I make reply, “Dear friend, it do —they call it
Wowserville;
“And there where once by grid, and rack stalked
Nick in robes of flame,
A new chief sneaks about in black—and STIGGINS
is his name!”

“Tamuinu”: I recently encountered a man
who had been enjoying or enduring a 12
months seclusion cure on Rotoroa, the island
in the Bauraki Gulf (M.L.), owned by the
Salv’army, and utilised as a “Home” for the
reformatory treatment of confirmed alco-
holics. When an inebriate has broken his
prohibition order four or five times within the
year, the S.M. commits him to Rotoroa for
a period of one or two years. Two years is
the maximum, and, according to my acquaint-
ance, it is about a year and nine months too
long. Most of the cases sent to the island
are physical wrecks at the date of landing;
but within three months, what with regular
hours, fresh air and total abstinence, the
worst soaker has regained his health so far
as he is eyer likely to do. He is as fit as he
ever will be to earn a crust and a bob or two
towards the price of his coffin. But for the
next nine months, or year and nine months,
as the case may be, he has to toil gratis at
scrub-whacking, road-making, fishing; in
fact, any sort of old job the Salv’army official
in charge puts him on. Whether he works
willingly or not, whether he makes a good or
bad job of his task, or whether he does any
blamed thing at all, the remuneration he re-
ceives never varies. The results of his labor
merely go to increase the value of the Army’s
property. The joke is that, during the whole
period of his stay on the island, the Govern-
ment is paying the Salv’army authorities 10s.
a week for his keep ! My ex-patient im-
pressed on me that the last nine months he
spent on Rotoroa were unadulterated Hades.
He had just one thing to look forward to —
the day of his release. When the sup-
posedly cured inebriate is discharged, he is
dumped on the Auckland wharf, with a Gov-
ernment gratuity, in the shape of a fl note
in his pocket, and freedom to do what he
likes with it. In many cases, night finds him
once more in the cells, sleeping off the
effects of his purchases, Awaiting returned
patients from Rotoroa, and leading them
to inebriate Elysiums, was at one time
quite a promising industry with the beer-
chewers of Auckland. This fact coming to
Magistrate Kettle’s ears, he announced from
the bench that anyone found tempting the
cured alcoholic would be presented with 12
months imprisonment. Since then the in-
dustry has wilted away, and returned Roto-
roans have to find their own way to the
Demon.
* * * *
The stern parent who bellows so impres-
sively on the stage rarely appears in real
life; and when he does he isn’t a bit im-
pressive. During the hearing of a case at
Prahran (Vic.), last week, a father of the
stern, old-fashioned type (by some evil
chance he was being sued for maintenance
by his wife) told of the heroic efforts he
had made to make his son behave like a
gentleman. “He had flogged him black and
blue, and on several occasions knocked him
down and kicked him, but all to no pur-
pose.” One here pictures the splendid old
aristocrat throwing down his cudgel in de-
spair, and retiring to his study to take off
his boots and die slowly of heartbreak with
his bruised feet in a bucket of hot water.

“Jay Kay” talks Heresy:—
This is a plea, before I go too mad, for the aboli-
tion of Universities. The other day, wiui the sordid
ob'ect of getting a rise in salarv, I tackled, the cold-
canned intellectual goods of orthodox learning. Mine
is the Arts course, and I defy any practical-minded
investigator to tear half a pound of usable informa-
tion from the whole dreary waste of it. Sometimes
it loses itself in the murky past in search of fossilised
nonentities. At others it debates closely whether A
is A, and whether B would be damned if it was
found drinking with Z. Anon, it requires one to
ONE OF THE OLDEST CASES OF UN-
SIGNED JOURNALISM ON RECORD.
The Venetian 'shoving his letter of denuncia-
tion into the Lion's Mouth.
tackle the muddled Anglo-Saxon declensions of the
rude forefathers of the hamlet. And none of these
practices promise me the least fragment of direct
benefit. Next year, if I survive, the Latin-man is to
get me. He has got himself into a state where he
would be safer drunk on the Seven Hills of Rome
than sober on a modern sidewalk, and a false quan-
tity jars him more than a split infinitive does a
third-rate sub-editor.
Now, I may get past the lot. Indeed, I hope to.
But that is not the question. What I want to know
is: Shall I ever be able to forget the awful stuff
which is soaking into me, or does it all amount, as
a gloomy intimate avers, to committing intellectual
hari-kari ? Time will show, but the future is not
auspicious. Meanwhile, for the sake of a generation
yet unborn, I adumbrate a couple of reforms, viz.: —-
(1) Professors of the standard type to be segre-
gated from the normal community, and fed up with
material from their pet study. All escapees to be
hanged.
(2) Professional schools to be established in place
of Universities. These to really inculcate a live
knowledge of and capacity for his future job, on the
part of the student, and to avoid anv sort of allusion
to what was baked on the bricks of Babylon.
At present there is mostly only one sort of profes-
sional school, and nearly all who tackle the higher
education go to it. At it, the professors bring on a
few, who are able to stand it, and turn them also into
Professors. The many take up dog-fighting, or cro-
quet, or Christian Endeavor, or smoking niggerhead.
They stomach the Prof, half a year, and get it all
back on capping day. Meantime, the Commonwealth
searches in vain for its necessary complement of
statesmen, editors, pedagogues, captains of enter-
prise, and divines. All it gets is a selection of glum-
looking youths, with their mental machinery held
up by the barb-.wire entanglements of the Dead
tongues.

“Life Buoy” indicates a danger spot:—
Apropos the Royal Commission now sitting in
Melbourne (Vic.) on the bay steamers and their life-
saving equipment. Allow me to suggest the appoint-
ment of a similar body to consider the position of
the ocean-going excursion steamers that run from
Sydney to Cowan, Hawkesbury and Cronulla. The
Port Phillip’s Bay boats frequent practically smooth
water, and mostly there is a handy mudbank avail-
able in the event of desperate measures being called
for. The Sydney excursion vessels which specialise
in ocean trips hardly ever encounter calm seas, and
the only refuges open to them are a rocky lee shore
or a surf-pounded beach. They are allowed by the
Marine Act to take 1000 passengers. Nothing in the
way of extra boat accommodation is attempted, the
Law pinioning its faith to bulkheads and lifebelts—
the former being for the steamer, the latter for the
men, women and kids. But bitter experience goes
to show that these are but busted reeds to float upon
when, the gaunt hand of Death reaches up out of the
main and starts dragging down victims. After the
Titanic horror, the Government which allows a
steamer to go to sea with 1000 people on board and
boats for 300 takes a hideous risk of gaining sudden
unpopularity, the sort of unpopularity that keeps it in
opposition for decades.
“N. or M,.” who is The Bulletin’s
office whaler, sends an item: —
Either suicide is becoming fashionable among
whales, or the ambulatory oil_ reservoirs are consider-
ably more numerous than they were. The pivot of
these remarks is a young cachelot that has blundered
ashore on a Tasmanian beach, near my humpy, and
is at present forming a centre of attraction. (“Poor
little thing,” the women say, as they look at the
18ft. infant.) A practical humanitarian has taken
an axe and put an end to the dying brute’s struggles.
Ever seen a whale killed? It is a more gory affair
than a popular
melodrama or Nor-
wegian saga, and
“doth all the mul-
titudinous seas in-
carnadine, making
the green one red.”
These cataceans are
built on an ama-

and outside infor-

ears are mere pin-
holes, and the

little slits near the

that the owners
can only see

frequent disasters.
According to an

sea-sendings belongs

now p 1 e n tly o f

lian waters to turn
the industry back
to the roaring days
of its prosperity, if
conditions were tho
same; but, whilst

and mineral oils
has caused a slump
in prices, and

as high as £110 a

to about £30. The
“right” whale —
the fellow that
carries the whale-
bone —is the aris-
tocrat of his kind, as the sick ambergris factory is
the millionaire. Australian whalers did not drop
to the value of the smellful commodity as soon as did
the Americans, and many a fortune must have
floated to the sharks for want of a little knowledge.
The sensational haul at Recherche, that glutted the
ambergris market, and made the fortunes of two
fishermen, is still talked of, though it happened about
25 years ago. McGregor’s old Derwent Hunter had
taken a whale, and towed it in shore for trying-out.
Having hastily stripped the blubber, it left for fresh
fields and cataceans new, passing the bones and
wreckage over to a fisherman known as “Black”
Harry, and a negro named Louis. The last of the
oil having been extracted, the negro crawled inside
the defunct, on an exploring expedition, and brought
out something that he thought might be ambergris.
It was! and the partners were offered £12,000 for
it on the spot. They refused. The Com-
mercial Bank took charge of the stuff for
them, and —in view of the fact that lOoz.
will supply a large scent factory for a year—
sold it out by degrees, realising a sum of £24,000
or £25,000. A thousand more or less doesn’t matter
much: when it comes to a lump of ambergris the size
of a bag of wheat. Ambergris, I believe, is reallv
the pain the killer whale gets in its stomach through
eating too many octopuses, the indigestible beak of
«ie fish accumulating in the larsre animal’s innards.
I give this item for what it isn’t worth.
* * * »
Onehunga, the little Maoriland port with
the Zoo, which lives on the proceeds of
handling any chance goods which may have
the luck to get over Manukau bar, is the
latest place to cover itself with the blushing
shame of owning a Carnegie library. Maori-
land has now a lot of these memorials to the
man who posted guns in his own black city
of Pittsburg to shoot down the strikers who
clamored for somewhat less indecent condi-
tions than those which enabled Andrew to
gather in dollars by the ton. It is a pretty
bad business to accept foreign doles of this
kind from any source. Onehunga ought to
be quite capable of building its own library
as well as maintaining it, as it must do in
any case. The rate which the astute An-
drew insists on having struck to maintain
his keg of books after he has built it is, in
nine cases out of ten, sufficient to supply
the whole lock, stock and barrel of an insti-
tution for a town twice the size. In addi-
tion to the loss of respect which the Car-
negie transaction involves, and the contempt
which it earns from all decent citizens, the
whole affair is downright bad finance. Thus
a town which really requires a £IOOO library
costing £3OO a year to maintain, cadges
£4OOO for its building, and pledges itself to
go on raising till the Day of Judgment a
yearly sum for the upkeep which is out of
all proportion to the needs of the place. In
fact, it sacrifices its self-respect for the sake
of a white elephant.

Lieutenant Thingabob, of the army aerial corps,
was making a flight, when the what-you-may-call-’em
of his machine went wrong, and he was instantly

“Let me like a soldier fail .”
Melbourne’s dreadfully incompetent Har-
bor Trust, which Bent threatened to wipe
out and Watt promises to abolish or reform,
has left that port so ill-provided with, berth-
ing accommodation that recently two loads
of immigrants had to lie out in the bay till
there was room for them at crowded piers
or docks. On September 14 the Gothic, with
752 settlers, found an odd corner unoccupied
in the West Melbourne dock. On Septem-
ber 16 the Norseman and the Medic wan-
dered in. One had to wait for the Gothic
to clear out, the other for a mail steamer to
leave Port Melbourne pier, on the outward
track. It doesn’t speak well for Melbourne,
but perhaps the Trust is wise. The big
steamers that are coming will pass by Mel-
bourne’s shallow inlet, even though the
Trust dredges a channel from the Heads to
Spencer-street ferry, and it isn’t any use
building wharves for steamers that will
unanimously ignore their existence.

THE NOISE OF SPRING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Let us take a ramble through the paddocks now,
when the young grass is so lusty that the surging
of the sap can almost be heard.
—Melbourne Herald’s tame spring paragraphisl.
I love the merry noise of Spring;
It makes me rear right up and sing
And dance about like anything!
Yea, I adore its cheerful sound ;
It laps my senses all around.
The plants are springing from the ground.
(They hiss like rockets as they jump)
And bombs’ of grass, in many a clump,
Come bursting upwards with a bump.
Virginian creepers shout and bawl;
And far abaft the echoing wall
0 hark! the sweet potatoes call!
But stay! across these hollow thuds
This thunder of the bursting buds,
These plaintive wailings from the spuds,
What novel note ? Ah, winsome souls!
Like some strange brand of burrowing moles
Spring bards come crawling from their holes,
While, sweeter than the cooing dove,
With many a pert coquettish shove
We hear the insects making love!
My cup of joy o’erflows the brim.
Oh, in the woods all weird and dim
To wander hand-in-hand with him ! —
AVith him, with him, where we two list,
’Mid Spring’s lush riot, roar and mist—-
Me, and the Herald Botanist. !
VICTORIA.
GWENDOLYN O’GRACIOUS.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
WHEN THE CAT’S AWAY.
The Man on the 1ST F LOOR: “Look out!
Here's the boss.”
“A. Nehoc” : “Reka’s” par. (B. 22/8/T2)
about the octopus, makes me wonder what his special
brand is, or what he has been having lately to eat.
The beak of the octopus, which is much the same as
that of a parrot, but does not protrude in the same
way, is placed exactly in the centre of the nucleus
of the tentacles, and is always underneath when the
animal climbs out of the water for a terrestrial
crawl. Perhaps “Rika’s” animal was standing on its
head for a wager, or overcome with surprise at meet-
ing a real aboriginality in the flesh. The beast’s
eyes are small compared with those of most marine
things, and very much resemble the headlights or
cow-catcher of a flatliead. I never saw the eight-
armed curse looking heartbroken, but some state-
ments made by bushmen from Waverley, and by
pirates and bushrangers holding Government clerk-
ships, would break the heart of an oyster.
“Pete”: Just liow far back cannibalism,
as a regular thing, ceased to exist amongst the Maoris
is not quite certain. There was a revival of it during
the savage days of the Hau-Plau war in Taranaki ;
but it is thought that the Taranaki consumption of
“long pig” was more an act of defiance than any-
thing else. However, those who are in a position to
speak with authority, declare that the Maoris ate the
flesh of their conquered enemies far more recently
than is generally thought. This idea is borne out,
to some extent, by an incident that cropped up at a
meeting of the Taranaki Native Land Court about
two years ago. A young Maori put in a claim for a
piece of land, saying that it was his because his
father had been the last man to own it, and was
buried there. The Court ascertained that the young
man was right with regard to the last occupancy of
the section, and decided to give him the benefit of
the doubt as far as the resting-place of his father’s
bones was concerned. A certificate of title was
about to be issued, when a voice at the back of the
court-room cried “Taihoa!” (wait a bit), and an old
tattooed warrior pushed his way forward. Smiting
the table with his fist, he shouted to the astonished
Court: “When this young man says his father’s grave
is on the piece of ground he desires, he does not
speak truth! Hear it, then, from me! Here”
(smiting his breast) “is the grave of his father!
He was my enemy, and I slew him in battle, and
ate him. The land is mine by right of conquest, and
must be given me!” Of course the Court was hor-
rified, and told the old warrior what it thought of
him. But the piece of land still remains State
property.
“Kodak”: When, the masked burglar scare
was on, I felt strongly inclined to break the rule of
a lifetime and buy a dog. People who said they
knew all about it told me there was nothing better
as a burglar alarm than a small dog. All you had
to do at night was to lock the small dog in, give it
the run of the house and go to bed. The trouble
was that I could not bring to share a house
with a dog. One of us would have had to take to
the kennel; and, as I hated the idea of a burglar
slaying me with poisoned meat while the dog was in
bed, I decided to do without the animal, and keep
the house for myself. lam glad I did so, because
the reliability of the canine burglar alarm seems
open to. doubt. There are hosts of dog-proprietors
living round my district, and, if their animals can
be believed, the whole place is simply crawling with
burglars every night. Of course, the yelping brutes
are merely holding long-distance conversations,
throwing out challenges and indulging in dreary
hysterical soliloquies. The average house-dog seems
to be a fearfully nervy beast. His low-grade brain
is filled with vague, nightmarish recollections of his
old forest days, and the minute the sun goes down,
or he is left alone, he sees and smells ghosts, and
howls his fears from the basement of his contempt-
ible, flea-bitten stomach. There is a particularly
desolate brand of beast, which is much affected by
people who go out a lot, and think their house is
quite safe so long as he is there. That dog’s de-
serted yell is simply an advertisement for burglars.
Sooner than be startled and maddened by having an
obscene false-alarmist running over my house, I’d
just submit to be robbed. In these days of masked
combines, there is nothing particularly objectionable
about a straight-out, occasional brigand.

“Wal” : The remarks on the tide gymnas-
tics of Torres Strait, by “F.A.T.” (B. 20/6/’l2),
bring to my mind the antics played around the China
Strait, near Samarai, Papua. The eastern end of
New Guinea juts out into a myriad of islands, big
and small, and through the resulting innumerable
passages and channels the tide runs at the rate of
a baby hurricane. Was aboard a steamer once which
tried for three hours to enter a passage near Sam-
arai, known as Rocky Pass, but although the vessel
could steam 10 knots in a heavy sea, it was not able
to push its way in. I have seen vessels lying along-
side the Samarai wharf break every line and swing
out into the stream when the tide has been a little
faster than ordinary; and if the worried master had
not known the place well he might not have had his
anchor down to hang on to when the lines went. A
funny trick played one day was the spectacle of a
small launch being hurried down the passage towards
ICwato Island by a punt that the launch had taken in
tow —a sort of case of the tower being the towed.
When the tide has been at its full strength I have
seen bottles do the measured mile in a little under
seven minutes, which would show a rate of about
nine miles an hour. With a tide of this velocity, the
place is far from pleasant to navigate, and many and
strange have been the pranks that vessels have per-
formed when coming into port. In one passage the
current would be dead ahead, but, as soon as the
skipper entered the harbor the tide would be with
him, and then one would see a scared sailorman doing-
fancy steering to keep clear of the other boats, his
efforts being accompanied by much “langwidge” from
the crews of the anchored craft. Crossing the strait to
the mainland, I have seen as many as four different
currents, the first going south, the second north, the
third almost slack water, and the fourth and last
dashing madly to the south. The resulting “rips” are
sometimes startling propositions. Several boats have
been turned clean over by them, and several lives
have gone into them that never came back to pay
for their drinks. The worst tides are at full moon
and first quarter. One man who tried to study the
currents and make a tide-table is now in the lunatic
asylum. And the tides still chase each other round
the strait.
“Wonga” : Forest restoration is a small
kind of thing in the Ma State, but if the average
cocky had his way there wouldn’t be enough of it
left to find with a powerful microscope. During the
recent drought, a timber reserve up Dubbo way ear-
lied some coarse feed, and surrounding cockies who
had been too lazy or careless to save, a bit of feed on
their own places, let alone grow some as a standby,
cast an eye of envy over the fence. Applications
were made to put stock on the reserve, and, when
these were refused, there was a wail like that of a
stricken city bumped by the horror of Asia. Every
cocky put in several solid days of hard graft cursing
the authorities who had no bowels of compassion for
his hungry stock. But the reserve in question con-
tained young trees which the sheep would have eaten
out in 24 hours, and the loss of the future forest
growth was an infinitely more important matter than

“Te Ruruku” : The Maori Tohunga—priest,
medicine-man, wizard, what you will—is really a
capable person, with all the instincts of a Beef Trust
or a grey nurse shark. The Maoriland Government
grants the old age pension to the ancients of the
hapu, but, in spite of many safeguards, the old
people get very little of it. The Tohunga’s way of
going to work is simplicity itself. He merely lies
in ambush by the post-office where the pensions are
paid, and frightens the cash out of his victims by
displaying his occult knowledge. “Friend Hoani,
look at the sky. Do you not see a cloud shaped like
a coffin? No? Well, I do, and I also see a man in
it very much like you.” Tremblingly the pension is
handed over to buy off the evil spirits. One Tohunga
got six months lately for such practices, and it would
be good business for the aged Maori generally if all
Tohungas could be given 60 years.
“Vagabond” : I want to know if any sort
of soup, stew, sea-pie or other dish can be made
from the river or mud turtles that crawl round the
banks and braes of the Murrumbidgee. When we
first ran across a covey of these shelly reptiles with
the delirium tremens countenances, vague memories
of aldermanic feasts of turtle soup and calipash and
calipee and turtle steaks floated across our minds,
and we carefully stalked a bunch and made prison -
ers of them. They didn’t look
anything inviting as the basis of
a Sunday dinner, but we dumped
them in our boat and pondered
over their possibilities in the
edible line for a day and a night.
By that time we had firmly estab-
lished the fact that turtles have
well —an odor, and this removed
them another long step from the
soup tureen. Finally it came to
the point of whether we should
leave the boat or the turtles
should, and the turtles lost—or
p’raps they’d say won, as we gave
them our blessing and a boost
back to their native smellement.
But there are plenty more here,
and if anyone tells us they can
be turned into a savory dish and
how, we’ll round up another herd
and experiment. Also, how do
you kill the deathless and im-
mortal turtle. (The person who
replies under this heading might
also explain how you kill devils.)
The infrequent inhabitants along
the river can’t help us, and firmly
turn up their noses at the notion of turtle figuring
on the bill of fare.

“Gouger”: Re that slow curse with the
quick fore-and-aft action, the death-adder. I re-
member, about two years ago, when I was prospect-
ing in the Stanthorpe district (S. Q’land), our party
of four had an experience. We had left our kapok
mattress behind at the last pub, and as a substitute
had to spread a big tarpaulin on the ground. In the
morning we found that our joint weight had rolled
a death-adder as flat as a blotting-pad. On another
occasion a mate of mine managed to roll up one of
the reptiles in his swag. He had left the blue
arrangement unstrapped for a few minutes in the
morning, and the poison-injector had crawled in.
Three days afterwards the swag was unrolled for the
first time—the hospitality of the pubs on the road
had rendered this unnecessary before—and the creep-
ing death came to view. I have seen only one man
bitten by a death-adder, and we saved his life by ap-
plying drastic remedies in record time. The bite
was a little below the ankle, and while one of us
held the victim down and sat on him, another got
a thick cord, and, with the assistance of a rifle-
cleaner, twitched it tightly above and below the
wound. Then we cut the bitten part out and
jammed gunpowder into the hole. When we put a
match to it and fired the mine the patient gasped ;
when we repeated the performance he howled ; but
after feeling bad for a day or two he was as right as
ever.

“Sr” : Talking about the sagacity of dogs.
The Hobart Mercury, a coquettish sheet replete
with columns of gay, flippant copy, printed the fol-
lowing : —-
Brisbane, September 3.
The police have come to the conclusion that the
lad Harold Ilalliday, aged four, who was lost in the
scrub at Booyal, near Maryborough, has been killed
and eaten by wild dogs, which infest the locality.
Batches of young pups have been seen frequently by
the search parties, many of whom share the opinion
of the police.
It says something for the police of Brisbane that the
dogs share their views.

“J. P. Aitch” attends a North Queensland
art show. “Whilst roaming over the solitary expanses
of the Lemon River Valley, a couple of weeks ago,
I came across what I believe to be Binghi’s Royal
Academy, or something of the kind. It is an escarp-
ment of ferruginous sandstone ridge,
which once formed the eastern bank
of the river, and through which chan-
nels were cut at right-angles by the
waters of the ages. The Lemon River
may not be as ' --ell known to Aus-
tralians as the names or the Seine;
wherefore, I pause to observe that it
flows into the Gulf of Carpentaria,
about midway between the Roper and
the Marathon rivers. All three are
larger than the foreign gadder-haunted
streams, and could be made quite as
navigable for ships of moderate
draught at a comparatively trifling
expense. (I expect that the Japs will
attend to this when they foreclose on
the continent.) The facade presented
a glittering display of natural history
studies in the impressionist style.
Section B, on the right side of main
entrance, showed a trial stakes open
to all-comers. The ’roo, gohanna and
bandicoot were well ahead of the field,
and going strong. The native com-
panion and whistling duck almost
flew off at my approach; and the
gohanna and bandicoot looked round
for holes to dive into. But the trum-
peter goose was the pride of the col-
lection. I guess the coal-black Aus-
tralian artist must have worked pat-
iently from the model, and not re-
lied on a defective memory. No doubt
jealous critics afterwards jeered at
the photographic accuracy of his pic-
ture ; but it is till plainly a trum-
peter goose after many years. Only
after I had been walking round some
time did I notice that I was not the
sole person on the premises. Though
it was a warm evening, the other
visitor (a severely-silent and with-
ered old man), was well wrapped up
in a bark overcoat. For some reason
he had been brought along there for
his long rest, after his last Extended
sun-bath. I can’t stand these old
men who sleep in public places, and
I left in disgust without buying anything.”
“Billy Hopkins” : Bishop Frodsham’s story
of the London editor, who had heard of “Queensland’s
icy' mountains,” and the old lady at the tea party,
who “remembered that hymn perfectly,” is percolat-
ing through the northern papers. The yarn sounds
mildly-' humorous on the plains, but that editor may
have been pulling the Bishop’s—er—apron all the
same. The ranges at the back of Cairns are icy-
enough in winter to suit Polar bears or even pen-
guins. I have written my name in frost on the fences
at Atherton, only 2500 ft, above sea-level. At Her-
berton, 3000 ft., I have seen half an inch of ice on my
bath. A little to the south, Bellenden Ker towers
up to a height of over 6000 ft., and the Bishop could
make ice-creams there, without a refrigerator, for
the old lady he met at the tea-party. Bishops don’t
usually get into places like these, hence their tropic
mirth over the ignorance of the benighted Londoner.
