“Kobbi” : “Monkbarn’s” yarn(B. 15/8/’l2)
about a white woman living among the Gippsland
blacks recalls an incident in the early history of
Rockhampton (Q,). One Hewitt, who was well
known in his humorous day and blatant generation,
reported to the. local paper that in his wanderings
on the coast he had seen a tribe of blacks, in whose
company he observed a white woman. She was 6ft.
high, of fine form, and perfectly 1 iiude. Although,
apparently, past her first youth, she was still strong
and well. A ship had been wrecked in those parts,
and it was never ascertained with, certainty whether
the captain’s wife, a. splendidly-built woman, who to
some extent, answered the description of the nude
wanderer, had gone to Jones or not. Rock-
hampton built its own theory. Sensation climbed,
on the shoulders of Rumor, and Howl gathered on
the shoulders of Sensation, and on top of Howl was
wild-eyed Blither, naked and unashamed, and with
its hair down. This came to pass when it was
known that Hewitt was actually bringing the
woman to Rockhampton. Then Public Interest
dropped with a sickening thud and broke its neck.
The notorious jester displayed the white female
figurehead of some wrecked vessel.

“Kunisarta” (in a burst of enthusiasm) :
The little war in Timor will bring that almost for-
gotten island of Captain Bligh and earlier buccan-
eer fame into the world’s limelight beam. It is
about the only spot left in the East Indian Archi-
pelago which is not spoiled and infested by the
tourist. In the rugged hills, rajahs still keep just
that sort of state which fired the imaginations of
writers on India in the days of Clive and Hastings,
There is less treasure, of course, in little Timor, but
still quite enough of semi-barbaric luxury and color
to make the visitor feel that he is living through a
series of Arabian Nights’ tales. . The Timorese are
the simplest, most guileless, and joy-loving creatures
left over from the Golden Age. That is the period
they rightly belong to, for they make play of all
their work, and yet do it with exquisite skill and
finish. The women go in groups to pick the cotton
that grows wild in the hills, or the mealies, yams,
bananas and mangosteens which flourish in the com-
pounds. They spin, weave and dye their beautiful
sarongs in the open. Some of the sarongs are ex-
quisite in pattern and color. Both men and women
carve skilfully, and dance with a grace and rhythm
which make the grotesque and ungainly contortions
of the modern ballroom look utterly vulgar and
foolish. Both sexes are soft-voiced and gentle-
mannered, and the women are lovable, good-natured
creatures —unless they have been spoiled and demor-
alised by intercourse with the whites. The Timor-
ese respects the women, and invariably treats them
kindly, though here, as among all Asiatic races,
polygamy is the law as well as the custom. The
islanders are singularly fortunate in being free
from all the Asiatic diseases except beri-beri. This
may be due to their habit of bathing early and late,
also between times. The Dutch and Portuguese will
have an anxious time if the rising spreads, for the
rajahs are able to muster considerable forces. The
Rajah of Amurassie can call up 10,000 men for ser-
vice, while his neighbor of Gabenzoes is good for
about 5000. The European population will not
muster 1000 all told. And it is a very lazy and
self-indulgent foreign legion. The leading club in
Koepang is the far-famed lap of luxury itself. You
can there obtain delicious dinners, rare vintages, and
everything that tends to content and flabbiness. If
the Dutchman knows little else, he knows the art of
dining a guest from A to Z. Now he will have
something else beside feeding to think about.
“Tiaki” : Some information which may be
useful to “Tamumu” and co. is to be found in the
June number of the Wide World Magazine. Alva
L. Cloyd supplies the “facts,” and H. Lane the illus-
trations. Cloyd started on a walking excursion
from the coast at Wairoa (M.L.), and thence to
the Urewera country. On the way he witnessed
some weird and wonderful ceremonies, and at one
part he came across a rushing army of “savages,”
who were whizzing over the landscape and beating
“tom-toms.” (No savage, according to the English
journalistic idea, is complete without his “tom-
tom.”) They (the tom-tommers) had an old chief
anchored down to a “shed-like” affair, which received
its motive power from a scrawny horse, and the
procession shot, “meteor-like,” into the “umbrageous
shades of the forest.” 1 can’t imagine even a mos-
quito bolting “meteor-like” through the supple-jack
bush which adorns Maoriland, let alone a vehicle
like a Mormon caravan. The idea, it appears, was
to rid the rider of a spirit. According to Cloyd,
after an Urewera native is thoroughly and completely
dead the scene of his demise becomes “taupo,” and
the Urewera native is a savage distinct from the
Maori—in fact, he was the aboriginal in possession
when the Maori arrived. Eventually the intrepid ex-
plorer got bogged in under a four-foot fall of snow,
which, taking all the circumstances into account,
was rather an easy let-down. The present writer
was in the Urewera region m 188,9, and his weapons
consisted mostly <?f Matilda and a bottle of whisky.
The latter implement seemed to impress the natives
greatly. Cloyd’s story looks as if it had been written
up in the mighty solitudes of Whitechapel, and
touched up by a sub. who had never personally en-
countered anything more deadly than an infuriated
cab-horse.
BRAINS WHAT TELLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Labor party talks about nationalising land.
If ever it tries to run a farm it will fail for this
reason—that it takes brains to run a farm. Brains!
Why this Labor Ministry has shown that it has not
brains enough to run a saveloy cart.— Hot words
spoken at a recent meeting of the IVomen’s National
League in Melbourne.
Our Women’s League, awake, arise;
Great intellect, assert yourself.
O matrons, wonderful and wise,
Turned recently from dough and delf,
To guide a nation’s destinies,
Behold the rich upon their knees.
As you are brainy be ye strong,
And set aright the proven wrong.
These Labor men have shrivelled brains,
They throw the policies aside
By which their betters won. their gains.
The grand mistakes do not abide.
But you believe, O Freetrade ma’s,
In all the errors of our pa’s.
Your splendid duty do not shirk,
Revive the things that wouldn’t work.
Reforms that didn’t try once more,
Worn-out devices quick renew.
It is the policy of yore
That specially appeals to you.
We’re trusting now to woman’s wit
To drag the bones up from the pit.
Please God, she may with subtle brain
Imbue them all with life again!
victoria.
FAT.
An Official Who Would Save the State
Money.
Dear Bulletin : Touching the perfectly
veracious remarks of Plain Snaffle (B.
5/9/T2). The writer supplies the bane, as
regards an important side of our military
system, but he makes no serious attempt to
provide the antidote. That valuable horses
are being ruined by the hundred yearly as
the outcome of bad horsemastership; that
the State is thereby wasting large sums of
money ; that the business is inhuman as well
uneconomical —all these have been truisms in
the' service for some time past. The scandal
is largely due to the very natural ignorance
of the liner points of horse-breaking which
characterises 999 soldiers out of every thou-
sand. A skillful horse-master has to be not
only born but made. The type of man who
spends his life learning the soldiering busi-
ness—and this is the type that mostly super-
intends the purchasing and brealcing-in of
the Commonwealth’s remounts at the present
time —has not had the leisure to acquire ex-
tensive equine lore, even assuming he pos-
sesses the capacity. The consequence is he
relies largely, when buying, on the good faith
of the seller —a notoriously dangerous thing
to do in the horse trade —and he gives the
professional breakers a free hand where the
young remounts are concerned. What the
country needs is at least one man at the head
of affairs who knows as much about horse-
trading as any of the folks he is likely to
meet, and who knows more about horse-
breaking than the professional rough-riders.
One possessing the latter qualifications should
be easy to find. In practically every case the
local rough-rider is a rank ignoramus. He
has but one end in view when faced with
a green horse —namely, to quell its spirit
and bring it to heel in a beaten state in the
briefest possible space of time. When one of
these bruising specialists has made a horse
buck —a horse which, if properly handled,
never would have bucked—and has then
kicked and ridden every ounce of buck out of
it, he (the specialist) is satisfied and feels
that he has earned his money. He has grati-
fied his own vanity by providing a highly
spectacular show, and no one can say he has
not done his duty, for there is the cowed and
obviously bested brute to prove the supre-
macy of man to demonstration. That a fort-
night later the animal may be an incurable
outlaw, as a result of his efforts, doesn’t
worry him one iota. The generality of young
horses cannot be subdued to such an extent
as to make them safe and serviceable chargers
in a day, or for that matter in a week. They
need to be handled with care and patient
gentleness and the utmost discrimination,
sometimes over a very long period. The
writer has had a deal to do with horses in
his time, and he avers, without fear of con-
tradiction from knowledgeable quarters, that
out of any given mob of (say) 1000 horses
off the grass, not more than 2(> or 30 will ever
buck if they are handled skilfully in the
initial stages. What the Government needs
to do, if it has any regard for the lives and
limbs of its soldiers, and if it doesn’t want to
waste a vast sum annually on scrapped re-
mounts, is to secure a man (not necessarily
in the service) with high qualifications of the
sort indicated above, and hand the whole con-
trol of the remount business over to him. It
would be his job to see and pass every horse
that was bought by the State. And he would
be required more especially to superintend,
either personally or by a qualified deputy of
his own selection, the breaking-in of each
fresh mob of young horses from the bitting
stage right up to the point where they were
handed over to the men as finished convey-
ances. This sort of employee or official might
cost a trifle, but he would be worth his salary
(providing he was the right sort of man)
many hundred times over. —Yours, etc.,
Miles. *

By the Oldest Inhabitant: —
Apropos Melbourne booksellers (Bulletin
12/9/’12), one of the most noteworthy from the mid-
fifties, up to his death in 1872, was Henry Tolman
Dwight, mainly a trader in second-hand literature.
His immense stock was housed in shed-like premises
in Bourke-street East, adjoining the old White Hart.
At his death, Mr. Dwight’s stock amounted to 30,000
volumes, and Messrs. Cohen and Co. were 14 days
selling it under the hammer. James Caple 1 knew
well. H's shon was on the north side of Collins-
street West, about midway between Elizabeth and
(jueen streets. He was a large, bulbous-nosed man,
who took snuff in quantities. At one general elec-
tion he stood in the interests of the importing party
for North Melbourne, and was badly beaten. His
wife, a small woman in spectacles, also attended in
the shop. lie had a derelict brother, who sold
newspapers on the street corner nearest to Caple’s
shop. (Almost everybody has a derelict brother).
He told me that Janies bad invited him to a Sunday’s
dinner at his place at Elstemwick, but he would not
to, as he could get as good a dinner for what it
would cost to reach Elsternwick. The brother was a
pork-pie sort of man, with few gastronomic- refine-
ments. Mr. Caple at one time did the commercial
community of Melbourne a service. He caused the
establishment of a late letter fee at the G. P. Office.
The mailboats, in the late ’50’s, lay out in Hobson’s
Bay, and the mails closed in Melbourne some hours
before the sailing of the vessel. The mercantile
people grumbled, but the P.O. authorities took no
notice. Caple established a late letter bag in his
shop—it was No. 29 Collins-street West —where, up to
one hour before the sailing of the vessels, letters
could be ijosted at a late fee of Is., subsequently re-
duced. I think, to (id. when the business got bulky.
The fee, of course, went into Caple’s pocket. The
bag was sealed and sent on board bv a special messen-
ber, a boatman being in waiting at Sandridge.
After Caple had been working his late letter bae- for
some months, and making money at it, the P.M.G.
stopped the business. The G.P.O. established its own
letter bag. It should be mentioned that anyone
could post a letter on board up to the time of sailing,
but the cost of getting there, by rail and boat, was
heavy.
RALSTON, General Manager , National Bank
of Queensland.
Senator McGregor (S.A.), who witnessed
the Governor-General wheel away the first
clod of the Western railway, said he once,
as a navvy, wheeled a barrow and bran-
dished a shovel on the railway from Port
Augusta to Oodnadatta. Senator Fraser, he
said, was then riding up and down the line
as an overseer to make sure that he did his
work properly. ' That was 35 years ago.

The things that may happen to a man
these times (Adelaide Register, 18/9/T2) :
Deceased had been ailing for some time, and was
operated on about a fortnight ago. He also had a
bacon-curing business.
“0.K.” : Died last week, in the West, “Tom
Collins” —or Joe Furphy—as Bulletin
readers knew him. Furphy lived much of
his life at Shepparton, Vic., where the stories
of his domestic eccentricities are district
traditions. His capacity for remaining
silent in the presence of his nearest relatives
is said to have been unlimited. Years ago
he founded a local Suicide Club, and when a
death occurred among its members there was
talk of indicting Tom Collins for man-
slaughter, or for being accessory to the sad
event, or something. Of the many people
that are needed to make a world, the late
Mr. Furphy was one of the least ordinary.
And a man who steadfastly refuses to be
ordinary can ill be spared. He is one of
the little bits of garlic in humanity's salad.
A letter from Rockhampton (C.Q.) :
Exit Paul de Montalk, for 20 years a reporter and
sketch writer on the Rockhampton Record. The beer
habit hastened his end. As some poet-thinker or
other has observed: “The ways of Bung kill slowly,
but they kill exceeding dead.” A thing that distin-
guished deceased from the generality of ink-sprayers
was the azure tint of his blood. He was a Pole,
who came here because of some brick or mud he
threw at the Russian Government. He had been
educated at a German University and spoke five
languages, all of which he brought out with him.
At first he held a job as lecturer at a Maoriland
college, and thence he drifted to press work and its
liquid concomitants.
Rev. R. S. Willis, Anglican parson, who
died lately at North Sydney, aged
75, was nearly the last of the primi-
tive group of Sydney University grad-
uates. He was the son of Joseph S.
Willis (Willis, Merry and Co.), who estab-
lished himself as a merchant on Church Hill
in the first 40’s of the 19th century, and
had his private residence where now stands
the Australian Club House. Willis, junior,
went to the Ma State University
when that institution was a baby.
He took his B.A. in 1855, and was
ordained in 1865. The daily papers
said he was the final remnant of that early
group of graduates, but that is by no means
certain. One Fitzwilliam Wentworth is still
suspected or keeping above ground.
The Office American drops a line
Rockefeller, the absurdly rich old Standard Oiler,
of Abraham Lincoln’s country, who earnestly desires
to buy a second-hand stomach, is now reported to be
so badly scared by murder threats that he lives
through his rollicking days with a guard of 18 buck
niggers. John the Standard Baptist is probably so
well hated that, even if he divided up his dollars
and dismissed his tribe of savages, someone would
hunt him for the pure joy of it. If John were a big,
strong man, instead of a dyspepsia-racked Croesus, he
might get some safe indoor amusement by going down
into a bomb-proof vault, and throwing cash into
the air with a long-handled shovel. Then, when
he had worked off his first burst of energy, he might
order one of his splendid slabs of darkness to hold
out his hat, and pour dollars into it until the crown
fell out, and the money splashed back into a glit-
tering, close-packed lake of wealth. Or he might
take wretchedly hard-up people down in a pinioned
state to view the treasure, just for the pleasure of
seeing their mouths water and hearing them gnash
their teeth. But Rockefeller never gets, any real
amusement like that out of . his fearful hoard. It
gives him no more joy than turnips. All
he gets out of it is the jumps, and a band of prowl-
ing blacks, who probably give him more jumps bv
breathing hard on his neck and looking at him sud-
denly round corners.
I )ied in London, at the age of 67, Charles
George Brooke-Hunt, the last surviving
officer of the Orpheus, which was wrecked in
a particularly thrilling manner at Manukau
(Maoriland) in 1863. The Orpheus was a
steam corvette of 21 guns and 1706 tons, and
was cruising in the more or less aimless
manner usual with naval ironmongery when
the Manukau bar got in its road. The
crew took to the rigging, and tremendous
splashes of water rolled over them again
and again until every stick was torn out and
carried away. The commander, Burnett,
with many of his younger officers, died cling-
ing to the mizzen-top, and the wreckage,
freighted with dead seamen, was carried
inshore by the tide. The small steamer
Wonga-Wonga rescued 70 men, but 23
officers and 166 sailors, marines and boys
were lost. Take it broad and large, it was
one of the worst things the Maoriland sea-
shore ever did. The Manukau bar is still
there, and at times looks ready enough to
put in a bit of overtime at the work of
destruction.
“Scarde”: Paragraphs concerning the late
J. Si Butters, of Melbourne, omit all men-
tion of the man’s non-ordinary personal ap-
pearance. To look at, Butters was decidedly
“out from the ruck,” and it would be a
pity if the millions who never saw him were
left to picture a double of. say. Willie Kelly.
In 1887, and for some years after. Butters
(then well over 50) was an erect fathom of
well-proportioned humanity. He had the
tyicai “warm auburn” hair of Scotland, and
it oozed in wild whiskers and beard. Thin
lips joined firmly in a long line across a
not-too-aggressive jaw. He had a nose, but
all I recollect about it is that “delicately
retrousse” doesn’t describe it. His red eyes
were very small and knowing. His voice
was hard and -metallic;. His hand-shake had
the vigorous cordiality of raw tripe in win-
ter. The unvarying black ton-hat. black
frock-coat and black pants would have made
him conspicuous enough, but he also affected
a black vest cut high and worn open down to
the second vest button, thus exposing a gold-
studded white shirt-front and long, slender
gold chain pendant from his red neck. No
one knew all the sights of Melbourne who
hadn’t seen Butters’ unbuttoned vest, linen
facade and gold chain. He Jived high, and
was a connoisseur in choice viands. There
is large suggestion of chef in the name
James Stetvart Butters.

MR. GEO. REID IN CHICAGO.
MEAT IN AMERICA.
About 60 years ago Van Diemen’s Land
resolved to. change its name, and be known
as Tasmania for the future. It was a wise
decision, for at that time “Van Demonian”
was generally regarded as a roundabout way
of spelling “lag.” But one crusted institu-
tion completely ignored the change, and to
this day it continues to call itself the “Van
Diemen’s Land Company,” It is an ab-
sentee English corporation, founded in the
early days, which acquired for nothing or
perhaps less, a huge tract of territory up
Circular Head way. A century of unearned
increment seems to have done this property
and its absentee proprietors a lot of good,
although the Tasmanians who have really
given the land its value have derived little
or no benefit from it. One George Thomas
Bait, a director of the V.D.L. Co., whose ad-
dress was Ethel burgh House, Bishopsgate,
London, lately shambled into eternity, leav-
ing behind him the tidy little sum of
£377.926. Most of this morally belongs to
the Tasmanians, but apnarently not a penny
of it will cross the Equator. Details of
Bait’s will are published in the London
papers. Not a single Tasmanian institution
or charity is mentioned to the extent -of a
copper.
Henry Varley’s will has been proved in
London. The Anglo-Australian butcher-
evangelist, or meat preacher, left £2375. He
did not annex a wealthy wife, like some
other evangelists of our acquaintance. Var-
ley was running a sausage and kidney and
chop shanty in Pakington-street, Geelong
(Vici), when he discovered and developed a
talent for open-air preaching. He praught
in a fashion suggestive of his trade, and
when he smote the Bible in moments of fer-
vor, he always left the idea that he had
struck Exodus, in the eye with a hind quarter
of mutton. In the fullness of time he
migrated to London, where his admirers
built him a tabernacle on Notting Hill.
Spurgeon is said to have thought highly of
his preaching powers. Varley revisited
Australia from time to time, and specialised
on social purity crusades. When he ad-
dressed himself “to men only,” the house
was generally full. A number of his ser-
mons and addresses have been published in
pamphlet form. In one of these there is
some plain speaking about the irregularities
of the late Duke of Edinburgh, son of Queen
Victoria, during his stay in Melbourne. A
certain voluptuous female, named Sarah
Sagin, was Melbourne’s choicest gift to the
Royal visitor. For years afterwards she
announced her free-and-easy establishment
as “Under Boyal Patronage.” But economy,
not immorality, was Edinburgh’s strong
point.
Clerics have their housing
troubles as well as Governors.
When Dr. Mannix was due at
Melbourne as coadjutor or as-
sistant to B.C. Archbishop
Carr, it was obvious that he
must have a residence and a
parish. He couldn’t hang out
in an hotel, and “the Palace”
was occupied. Now there was
a fine parish and a commodious
presbytery at West Melbourne,
but_ Father Carey was in occu-
pation, as parish priest. He
couldn’t be canonically removed,
except for “grave cause,” and
good Father Carey looked about
the last possible man to supply
grave cause.” But the matter
was got over. Father Carey
accepted promotion downward
to the parish of Brunswick, and
upward to the status of Dean.
So, having thus started suddenly
on the ladder of promotion, via
the brickyards of Brunswick,
there may be a great future
before Dean Carey. He may
even be a Cardinal one day.
Meanwhile, the coadjutor has a
roof over his head, and is no
longer in danger of having to
camp out.
Consider the expert in all his
glory! A quarter century ago
Gyles Turner was the great
banking genius of Australia.
True, the smash that his bank
made was as the smash of 10
(other men are still busy pulling
the concern round), but the
great banker’s knowledge of the
theory of the game was illimit-
able, and if he failed a little
in the simple, dull-brown job of
making h wise selection of the
securities to lend some millions
of money on, well, no man can
be perfect. Similarly, Jacques
Martin, long autocrat of the
Colonial Mutual Life Assurance
Society, was called the greatest
life assurance expert south of
the line. He knew everything
about his business —even the
number of Tubal Cain’s endow-
ment policy. Still, some people
said that the expenses were too
high, and that the policy of
opening offices in Johannesburg
and Edinburgh and Dundee and
Aberdeen and Leicester and Bloemfontein and
all manner of places was altogether to rash
and. costly. Now. a fierce demand is made
in the Victorian State Parliament for an in-
quiry into the Colonial Mutual. Jacques
Martin, the great oanjandrum who devised
the policy of the office, was, as already men-
tioned, an exnert and a genius. Some con-
cerns get on better without a genius.
The mischievous old presentation habit or
nuisance was reduced to well-merited absur-
dity, the other day, when the Southall-Nor-
wood (Eng.) district council unanimously
resolved to “place portraits of the Earl and
Countess of Jersey on the walls of the coun-
cil chamber in recognition of the great in-
terest they have always taken in the welfare
of the district,” and followed that up with
a special resolution requesting the Jerseys
to “provide the portraits.” If every up-
country banking official, parson, etc., had to
personally fill the purse of sovereigns that it
is the imbecile local custom to heap on him,
previous to liis departure, marriage, or what
not, there would be more joy in the land
than if 99 sinners had suddenly died unre-
pentant.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The application made to the Victorian
State Parliament, for an inquiry into the affairs of
the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society, is a
matter which might well have happened much earlier,
not only to this concern, but to others. If the investi-
gation comes off, the results may be surprising, or
they may not, but if the event leads to a general
habit of investigation, it will not be trouble wasted.
A strange, quiet, peaceful, casual, slummocky place is
Australia, so far as its life assurance control goes.
Except for some limitations, which are almost too
small to be worth mentioning, anybody can start a
life assurance concern, which is much worse than
letting anybody start a bank. For when grave sus-
picion falls on a bank, the depositors rush it, and
thereby put it promptly to the test. But a life office
can’t be rushed with any degree of promptitude.
People can stop paying premiums, and lose their
investment; or they can ask with more or less success
for their surrender values (which are fixed by the
management—in the case of the dreadful 1.0. F. of
Canada there were none at all) ; also, they can study
the balance-sheet, which most of them find most in-
comprehensible. And that is about all. Nominally,
of course, the policy-holders control a mutual office.
They can shift the directors if they have any suspi-
cions, and appoint new ones, with instructions to
hold a rigid investigation. But when a concern has
policy-holders all over the world, it has to be a very
large and terrible suspicion that reaches in the ordin-
ary course to the ears of 2 p.c. of them. If the owner
of a £lOOO policy becomes so embittered that he
will spend £20,000 in rousing his fellow policy-
holders to an investigation of the accounts, he may do
something—provided he is able to get the addresses of
his fellow policy-holders. But people of such ferocious
energy are rare. The general course of things is this:
The management draws up a balance-sneet. The
management values the assets. The management
states that all is right, and implies that it (the man-
agement) is above suspicion. The management sends
out proxy forms to remote shareholders if there is
any urgent question to the discussed, or any attempt
to unseat the directors, and generally it gets enough
of them to vote down any number of local malcon-
tents who are likely to personally attend the annual
meeting. The directors are, as a rule, able, if they
so desire, by the aid of proxies, to virtually re-elect
themselves, or if one of their number is dead or tired,
to replace him by a friend of their own. They are
generally able, if they so desire, to elect the auditors,
who generally report that all is right. (The auditors,
by the way, hardly ever value the securities, which
may be scattered all over the world, and quite beyond
any one man’s valuation.) In fact, the whole busi-
ness is a good deal of a family affair, if the direc-
torial and managerial family likes to make it so.
There is no Government audit or inspection or valua-
tion. The States did nothing when they had the sole
power, and now the Commonwealth has the power and
it does nothing.
The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society was re-
puted, rightly or wrongly, to have been heavily struck
in the securities when the Victorian boom broke nearly
20 years ago, and in the absence of any State audit
or valuation it had no easy way of getting rid of
the suspicion. It might, perhaps, have asked for a
special Parliamentary investigation, but it didn’t. It
isn’t an absolutely first-class affair in point of size,
for its assets only add up to a trifle over £3,300,000.
It gathers its moderate bit of business over a very
large area, having offices at Aberdeen, Belfast, Bir-
mingham, Cardiff, Cork, Dublin, Dundee, Edinburgh,
Glasgow, Leicester, Liverpool, London, Manchester,
Newcastle and Sheffield in the British Isles; also at
Bloemfontein, Capetown, Durban and Johannesburg
in South Africa, and at many places besides. The
fact that all this wide spreading of its business ten-
tacles produces a comparatively moderate amount of
trade, suggests that, in its own country, where it
should be best (or worst) known, it has no great
connection. The widespread nature of the office must
needs add to the expenses; it makes it extra difficult
to keep an eye on the quality of the securities; it
makes it extra-difficult for any malcontent to rouse
the policy-holders to active investigation; it makes
it extra-easy for the people in authority to preserve
their authority unquestioned, if they want to do so ;
and it tends to add to the death losses, for Australia
and Maoriland are the healthiest countries in the
world, and all this expansion into less healthy regions
is bad for the burial average.
Last year (1911) the expenses of the Colonial
Mutual were much higher than the year before. The
closest estimate The Bulletin could make was that
they represented £26 16s. Bd. per cent, of the total
income, which was very heavy indeed. The assets of
the ordinary and accident departments consisted
mostly (nearly 70 per cent.) of mortgages and free-
holds, which is rather a dangerous kind of business,
unless closely watched, and in the absence of a State
audit and valuation it is difficult to say how much
watching was done. And the industrial department
was wholly depressing. Its biggest asset was “Exten-
sion of Organisation Account,” which is simply a
paper or goodwill item, and is so described (to save
space) in the table below. This paper item has been
increasing alarmingly:—-
Colonial Mutual Industrial Life Branch.
1909. 1910. 1911.
Liabilities .. .. £16,745 £89,209 £109,870
Visible .. .. £4,456 £7,047 £6,476
Paper or goodwill.. 11,289 82,162 103,394
£15,745 £89,209 £109,870
This showed a huge increase in the paper, or good-
will item (or, to speak plainly, the deficit) of the
industrial branch. And as the ordinary branch has
to carry its industrial brother somehow, the assets
of that branch now include an advance of £49,178
to the other branch —the one which has only £6476
of its own to bless itself with. Some people seem to
look unkindly on this asset of £49,178, and possibly
on some other assets, anil when it was resolved to
declare no bonus this year, but to hold over
the surplus of £149,732 (which is partly
made up of the £49,178 abovementioned) tiil
1913, they weren’t mollified in any way.
The reasons assigned for this proceeding looked
fair enough, but still the malcontents were not molli-
fied. It is alleged that the methods used to prevent a
new man getting on the board last year, and getting
a view of the inside aspect of things, were “over the
odds,” said methods consisting, according to the
story, mostly of proxies; and there is a demand for
a general pubb'c overhaul. The demand is one which
the directors should receive with applause. In fact,
they should demand a rigid Government audit and
valuation, and a complete public inspection. Then, if
all went well, the Society could advertise that it
was the only one of its kind in Australia which
possessed a State certificate of solvency.

These are exceedingly good times for the Melbourne
Tramway Co. It was founded in 1884 to work the
Melbourne and suburban tramways (it didn’t build
them), and it got a 32 years’ lease, so it has to
give up possession on July 1, 1916. It had to keep
the lines in repair, and hand over the system in good
order, and there is no reason to believe there will be
anything wrong with the system when it is handed
over, save that it will be about as antiquated as
Ahab’s chariot. Also, the co. has to pay interest on
the debt which the municipalities incurred to build
the lines, and to provide a sinking fund sufficient"to
extinguish that debt by 1916. All these things it has
been able to do without turning a hair, thanks to
low wages, high fares and general economy. The in-
terest and the sinking fund contribution are among
the expenses, and the profits shown above are net.
Also, the reserves are wholly the property of the
company, unless some small amount has to be ex-
pended for repairs in order to put the lines in the
good order and condition that the lease stipulates
for at the date of surrender. Yet that isn’t likely,
for as the first business after the surrender will pro-
bably be to scrap everything except the merest skele-
ton, and instal an electric system, nobody in author-
ity is likely to be very particular about small details.
Put in a condensed form this is how the co.’s assets
stand:—
1909. 1910. 1912.
Liquid assets ..£995,415 £1,023,708 £1,300,067
Freeholds . . 244,507 248,641 222,906
Other property . . 149,541 156,116 163,765
Total ..£1,389,463 £1,428,465 £1,686,738
Less debts.. 212,878 145,310 127,861
Surplus. . £1,176,585 £1,283,155 £1,558,877
Or, to go into further details, the assets, as at June
30, 1912, were as follows:
v Solid Assets.
Freeholds ' £222,906

Therefore, even supposing all the £170,439 worth of
More Doubtful-looking Assets only yield enough to
make the lines habitable when they are handed over
three years hence, the co. still has £1,395,112, plus
all the profits it is liable to make in the next three
years, with which to return its shareholders the
£250,000 which they actually put into the business,
and a huge bonus to boot. Yet, if business remains
on the present level, they will, by 1916, have again
got their original capital back nearly twice over in
dividends, and have got their capital added 11 times
over to reserve funds. Truly, this is something like
being on a good wicket!
A great deal of the luck of a monopoly depends on
the kind of Government that the monopolv strikes.
The Melbourne Tramway Co. was legally liable, after
a certain number of years, to have its fares revised
to any extent by the Victorian Parliament; there-
fore, its 32 years’ lease was never worth as much as
it looked. There was no written proviso for revision
of the charges of the Sydney Gas Co. And the Tram-
way Co. did things in the way of dragging stupendous
profits out of its customers—it has averaged within
a fraction of 100 p.c. per annum for the past two
years- which the Sydney Gas Co. never had the
courage to attempt. But the tram concern struck a
friendly Government and a complaisant Parliament,
and nothing serious happened to it. There is now
hardly time for anything tq happen to it. The
chances are that it will retire in a blaze of glory in
1916, and return the original capital and hand out a
lordly bonus of about £5 for every 20s. Of course
the market has been hard at work for a long time
discounting that bonus, but that is a way the market
has.

That 21f p.c. div. is reckoned on the written-down
capital, not on the money that the shareholders put
into the business. On the original capital it is only
a trifle over 2+ p.c. The co. was formed in 1885, as
the Federal Coffee Palace Co., Ltd., with an author-
ised capital of £200,000, of which £122,752 was
issued with Is. a share uncalled. When the co. was
formed, Melbourne was very thirsty, and the result
was that the property, etc., were very much over-
valued. In 1897, soon after the bank collapse, when
people were feeling sobered down, the co. discovered
that its property wasn’t worth the £151,083 that it
originally thought it was. Instead, it decided that
it wasn’t worth more than £65,000 ; and the furni-
ture sank in their estimation from £29,210 to
£17,866. There was also a big mortgage, £68,000.
and this plaster was bigger than the book value of
the premises. So something had to go, and the 19s.
shares were written down to 2s„ with Is. callable,
of which ljd. was then called up. This table shows
10 years’ progress:—
1902. 1911. 1912.
Liabilities £60,329 £45,088 £40,708
Liquid assets 3,090 2,448 2,488
Deficit £57,239 £42,640 £38,220
So in that period the co. has got out of the hole to
the extent of £19,019 —this, of course, besides paying
for renewals and repairs to property. The plaster
on the premises is being removed gradually. It now
stands at £38,500. During the year £4750 was
hacked off, and each bit taken off means less interest
to pay, and consequently a bigger chip to knock off
next year. At the present rate of going it will take
about seven or eight years to remove the whole of the
incubus. Then things will look up for the share-
holders. Meanwhile, they must be content with a
modest return of 6d. per annum on the 19s. lid. they
dropped into the plate.
In a financial way, the present N.S.W.
Government appears to be plunging down a very steep
place into a very deep sea. It has managed, in the
first eight months of 1912, to increase the expendi-
ture by £1,474,983, as compared with the first eight
months of 1911. Yet there is nothing special to
show for the extra outlay, and it hasn’t reduced the
expenditure of loan funds by a single copper. In
fact, the loan expenditure has gone up to a point
never before dreamt of in the State. The McGowen
Ministry shows a cynical disregard of its pledge to
reduce borrowing, and restrict loan expenditure to
works that will pay full interest and sinking fund,
which is a marvel in the way of the political Judas
Iscariot or turncoat business. The straits to which
it is reduced these days begin to arouse interest. A
sum of £2,150,000 has been borrowed on short
terms from the Federal Government. The trifle at
credit of the sinking fund has been borrowed. In
1910 the Treasurer had about £3,200,000 at com-
mand to the credit of various funds, but by June,
1912, it had all been spent, save £lOO,OOO or less.
The revenue, closer settlement, public works and loan
funds had oyer £2,600,000 on hand at 30/6/’lO. By
30/6/’l2 they were overdrawn to the extent of nearly
£700,000, and two months later they were over-
drawn to the extent of over £2,100,000. In fact,
every available fund seems to have been used up’
Perhaps the prize jest of the situation lies in the
fact that, last January, the N.S.W. Treasury gave
notice that it would redeem over £2,500,000 of old
4 p.c. securities. It wasn’t absolutelv bound to re-
deem them, for they were securities of the optional
variety, but it proposed to float a new loan at a
lower rate of interest. Now it becomes a verv
serious question if the loan won’t have to be re-
floated at a higher rate of interest. Short-dated
loans, short-dated deposits, short-dated overdrafts
threaten to fall due shortly all round the wildly ex-
travagant McGowen Cabinet, and its prospects show
no improvement every day.

The return is the poorest on the above list, and
the above list goes back to December, 1904. For
some reason or another a severe slump set in after
July, 1911, and has continued since. July, 1911,
was, on the figures, the pinnacle of the co.’s pros-

This half-year they have declined by another £303.
This is a condensed statement of the position:—
July, Jan., July,
r , 1911. 1912. 1912.

, £llBO £9Ol £556

So the verdict is (1) That the gross profits have
diminished within the last half-year; (2) that the
expenses increased greatly during the half-year which
ended on January, 1912, and have been reduced very
little since then ; (3) that the allowance for depre-
ciation has been reduced, presumably in an effort to
put a good face on things, but all the same a very
bad face has been put on things; and (4) that
despite the reduction of dividend and bonus, the
amount available for reserves is only about one-
tenth what it was a year ago.
The capital has increased a little of late, but even
now it is only £5OlB. Both the value of the stock
and the amounts owing to the co. are rather less than
they were six months ago. The co. seems to be in
a chronic- state of indebtedness to the bank. At
July, 1911, its overdraft, less cash and deposits on
the other side of the account, was £523. At Janu-
ary, 1912, it was £471. At July, 1912, it was
£1639. No doubt the famous general strike did
some harm to the business, but even apart from that
the position looks none too progressive.
* * * *
The Bill, now before Victorian Assembly,
re the State’s brick paddock at Thornbury, provides
for a 21-year lease of the land, at £l5O a year, for
purposes only. The Government claims
50,000 bricks a day if it wants them, at 31s. per
1000, on trucks at Thornbury. In case of failure to
deliver, the Government may purchase elsewhere at
the company’s risk, or it may impose a penalty, or
determine the lease. It looks a good bargain on the
State’s part, especially as the Combine’s present
price for baked clay is 40s. per 1000; but if it will
pay a private company to load the articles in trucks
at 315., the State could probably do it at 255. or
thereabouts. Anyhow, a lot depends on how the
conditions are enforced. The affability of a Minister,
or his overweening anxiety not to “inflict hardship”
on anybody, may turn the penalty clauses of the con-
tract into vain words.
* * * *
Ardlethan (N.S.W.) is suffering from a
mi Id sort of boom, on the strength of recent discov-
eries of tin in the district. Most of the land is
privately held, but over 200 applications for authority
to enter have been lodged with the warden. Report
says that the Carpathia mine has been sold for £7OOO
cash, and £lO,OOO in shares, but the name of the
purchasing co. is not stated. Neither is the number
of tons of crude ore from which was picked the parcel
of 10 tons, said to have returned over 40 p.c. tin.
The same line of lode has been tapped in places for
about three-quarters of a mile, but there seems to
be a disinclination to do any sinking worth the men-
tion, as the deepest shaft is not 20ft. in the most in-
teresting direction.

For the half-year ending July 31, Y. Water
tm mine (Emmaville, N.S.W.) produced 1.3171 b. of
tin oxide per cubic yard (195.78 tons). The grade
compares with 1.6551 b. per cubic yard for the pre-
vious six months, but the quantity of the globe
treated increased from 246,700 cubic yards to 333,000
cubic yards. The tin sold realised £24,785. Four
Is. dividends (£8000) were paid, £2660 was
written off for depreciation, and £5Bl paid for,
income tax, leaving £5784.
Last week the Junction North (Broken
Hill) mill . recovered 316 tons of lead and 18,5460 z.
of silver in 562 tons of concentrates, the average
assay being 56.4 p.c. lead, 330 z. silver, 14 p.c. zinc.
In addition, the mill produced 330 tons of zinc con-
centrates, assaying 42 p.c. zinc, 9 p.c. lead and 16oz.
of silver. The gross value for the week’s run was
£9270 (lead at £2O per ton, silver at 2s. 6d. per
oz., zinc concentrates at £2 per ton). The Junction
North is producing from 12,000 to 15,000 tons of
lead a year, and the silver recoveries average 58oz.
per ton of lead. It has been officially estimated that
the working profit is £3OOO for the week, and, if
this is correct, the Junction North should not be long
in securing a firmer financial foothold. Still, the
mixing of slimes with crude ore, and the adoption
of the mineral separation process was by no means
an unqualified success at first, and -the Junction North
having got into debt, has had to make up financial
leeway. A call of Is. was due September 11.
* * * *
The Mining Engineer answers :—“Chlor-
ide’s” howl about lodide is -not surprising.
In fact, it was to be expected, for therein
lies the explanation of his connection with the mine.
Yet what does the howl amount to? The “erroneous
ideas” (B. 12/9/’l2) are supposed to be established
by the peevish objection taken to a trivial detail of
the capital distribution, but the net result is not dis-
proved, unless perhaps “Chloride” means to show
that the concern is loaded with an extra £3500 not
suspected. He does not discuss the value of the -ore
reserves in their relation to costs, but merely says
that “valueless figures are provided.” The showing
of just wherein the figures are valueless is successfully
evaded, for obvious reasons, and attention is drawn
away to the more congenial subject of the immense
and dazzling possibilities. It is time that pause is
taken to cry out against 15s. per ton as the cost of
mining, and the triumphal fact that the ore is actu-
ally being broken for ss. per ton is loudly acclaimed.
But the wages cost of breaking ore is not the total
cost of mining, not by a long way, as even “Chloride”
would know, if he had ever been connected with the
operative side of mining, instead of only that side
which is concerned with long-winded, optimistic
scribbling to the daily press. This class of scribe
would have the public believe that the net profit
on that picked parcel sent to Cockle Creek was
£7 13s. per ton of crude ore, whereas it is doubtful
if a shilling was netted after the deduction of all
expenses. But, leaving out of consideration past results
and present costs on the ground that a new era is to be
ushered in—the position is this: The lodide is going
strong, and has improved almost daily since Fawcett’s
report. That it is a justifiable proposition for a
development company is beyond question, but there,
for the time being, -the matter should end. But this
course has not been taken, and, although the present
positive value of the mine is nil, or thereabout, the
lodide is now loaded with an absurdly ton-heavy capi-
tal. which is to be redeemed bv the little tin-pot
smelter proposed to be erected. “Chloride” misses the
significence of the siliceous ironstone, once again in
italics. His “best silver-lead smelter and adminis-
trator at liberty in Australia” ought not to be on
the show for at least a couple of years to come, but
the good administrator certainly was needed before
the float was rushed on to the public, long ere its
nr : me. But, the plan suits the promotion idea, be-
cause “mining” includes a good deal of speculation
in human nature, and occasional developments in
oxidised ore are a thing to juggle with and impress
the mind of the public, which rarely discriminates
between sensation and soundness. Budgery, C.S.A..
Cordillera, Mount Costigan, these and many others
are monuments of unseemly haste.

directors to hustle on with a new plant, as tin won’t

in commission is a waste of time and tin, notwith-
standing the £4OOO profit for five months from the
partial treatment of 900 tons of stone.

North Smithfield (Gympie, Queensland) got
on the old payable track when it shouted itself a
shilling dividend —the first since June, 1909. The
July crushing gave 711 oz. from 434 tons, and August
went better with 8560 z. from 300 tons. Most of
the payable stuff is coming from the 680 ft. level,
where a 14in. reef blossoms like the rose. Its divi-
dends to date total £4 6s. lOd. per share, which
isn’t bad on 48,000 half-crown shares, paid up to
13d.
“Kobbi”: Mount Garnet (N.Q.J is to be
resurrected, which is a further tribute to the ascent
of King Copper. The show put its first parcel of
stuff through its own smelter way back in 1901. At
that time, the directors reckoned on 209,000 tons of
6 p.c. ore in sight. It must be there still, or most
of it. It certainly wasn’t got out.
* * * *
Mount Molloy (Q.) has to face recon-
struction. It has produced 1370 tons of copper,
which realised £196,000, out of which it paid a
few thousands in dividends. It has a good supply
of water and timber, for it is close to Rifle Creek,
and is equipped with a smelter and converter plant.
The mine did very little good since the railway (22
miles) was made. It is thought that some of the
low-grade silicious ore can be concentrated, and that
the show is worth a course of prospecting. The ore
shoots have been payable but erratic.
* * * *
Hampden-Cloncurry has engaged a con-
verter superintendent from Port ltembla (N.S YV.).
A good run is expected for September, though a
leaky water packet caused some delav. Plenty of
ore, limestone and ironstone, are available. But the
supply of coke has been short. Smelters in Queens-
land obtain most of their coke fnm Hie south coast
of N.S.W., and the break-up of the Wollongong and
Port Kembla jetties has interfered with shipments.
The Hampden co. has secured a six months option
over the Answer mine, which has a shaft down 701 t.,
and is showing good prospects. First-grade ore
shipped has been 30 p.c., while seconds assay 22 p.c.
copper. So far the Hampden co. has been very
lucky, by securing mines like the Duchess, and the
Trikelana—lo miles from the Duchess—at moderate
prices. The Mascotte, Scalper and Answer, recently
taken in hand by the Hampden “0. . are promising
shows in the early stage of development. .1 be
Hampden leases supply the smelter with ore that
helps to flux the silicious material from the
Duchess. It is a favorable sign that the co. intends
to sink No. 1 shaft another lift. This shaft is down
214 ft. From No. 2 shaft the workings on vhe east
and west ore bodies extend a long distance to the
north. At 748 ft. N. a rise is up Onft. ibove the
280 ft. level; width of lode 39in., worth 5} p.c.
Standard Consolidated Tin Mines (Q.)
have purchased an up-to-date 20-head battery and gas
engine from the Golden Horn Co. (near Charters
Towers). Since April the old mill treated 838 tons
for 47.4 tons of tin oxide, assaying 68 p.c. metal.
The recoveries have not been good, and it is calculated
that it will pay to treat the tailings again when the
new plant is in commission. There are 86,030 shares
issued. Co. has three lodes, the workings on which
have been confined to shallow levels.
* * * *
At Charters Towers the Brilliant P.C. is
coming into favor, because of good prospects ob-
tained near the bottom of the winze 220 ft. east of the
New Brilliant Freehold’s boundary, and the extension
of the 1596 ft. level to the east on a favorable forma-
tion.
Brilliant Deeps (Charters Towers) has
opened a share register in London. At the mine they
are cutting down the western side of the rise and
winze connecting No. 4 with No. 2 level. It is in-
tended to form a main haulage-way or underlie shaft
some 173 ft. west of the crosscut, and consequently, in
the thick of the shoot of good stone. This reef, when
No. 4 level was being driven, produced 323 tons,
worth £2323, which is over £7 per ton. Of course,
the reef is rather bunchy and patchy, as shown by
the fact that the smallest lot crushed was 48 tone for
£l4 15s. 6d. per ton, while 202 tons averaged
£3 13s. No. 5 level has been driven 279 ft. For
more than 140 ft. it was a blank, then a reef sft.
wide appeared in the face. Since then the level has
shown a reef from a few inches to 3ft. in width. No.
5 level is in 279 ft., so it will soon be as far from
the crosscut as No. 4 level, and it will be interesting
to the Brilliant Blocks to watch the course taken by
the level, for No. 4 was undoubtedly heading for the
boundary, and was taking a much more southerly
course than was expected.
Mount Morgans are moving up in spite
of the mob of shares left by W. R. Hall, W. (Yd big-
nan and A. J. Oallan. There has been no attempt
to force sales at low figures. With - increased prices
for copper and good management, shareholders ex-

1S i. i°P that, with general manager Magnus at

* * * *
Fpr three months to end of August, Mount
Elliott (Q.) smelted 16,482 tons of ore for 2135 tons
of copper, 38770 z. gold, and 40050 z. silver. So the
furnace has required an average of less than eight
tons of ore to run out a ton of copper. The ex-
traction averaged 12.9 p.c. The August run smelted
the most ore (5710 tons) —in fact, a record quantity
to date—-vet the yield of copper was lower than for
the two previous months. June gave 749 tons ; July,
1 12 tons; August, 674 tons. This reduction of the
yield was expected, as some of the richest stopes
eased off. At No. 5 level driving is going on to
define the contour of the ore body, picked up by the
diamond drill, and this make of, ore will be sunk
on at no distant date, and all doubts about the verti-
cal continuity of the lode set at rest. Assays from
development work are not high. Hampden Consols
and the Trafalgar mine continue to develop on sound
lines* though the ore (6 p.c, and 8 p.c. copper) isn’t
nearly so r,eh as that being stoped at Mount Elliott.
Probably the latter will have to increase the capacity
of its smelting plant when a larger supply of ore
becomes available from outside mines.
The *Radium Hill finds it difficult to pre-
pare radium bromide in a state of purity at the Wool-
wich works (Sydney). The impurities contained in
radium sulphate from the furnace have been trouble-
some to separate, for it is only by the tedious pro-
cess of fractional crystalisation that the pure radium
salt can be prepared. To extract a grain of gold
from a ton of rock would be difficult, but it is com-
paratively an easy task to the extraction of a milli-
gram of pure radium bromide from a ton of concen-
trates. About 15 tons of the latter are treated and
reduced to radium sulphate and uranium oxide per
week. Though official information is scanty, it would
seem that it takes four or five tons of ore to pro-
duce a ton of concentrates. Salts of iron, barium,
uranium, etc., crystallise from solution under variable
conditions, so constant repetition of chemical methods
is required to eliminate the impurities and extract
the final germ of radium from the mountains of
.debris.
Jack Lennerberg and late Assistant Gov-
ernment geologist Gibson, who left the West some
months ago to prospect the Northern Territory on
behalf of Dorrie Doolette, have sent word that no-
thing worth mentioning was met with in the
McArthur River country, which is the track the rail-
way would take if it went through the Eastern States.
They are now sampling some copper shows at Pine
Creek, and intend to have a look at a “big thing”
near Port Darwin later on.
“Few miners are now employed, and the
completion of the treatment plant seems as remote
as ever,” remarks a local paper of Bullfinch. This,
taken in conjunction with the fall in the market,
seems to indicate that again all is not well with this
once high-priced bird. May be the moulting season
has come along with its train of worries.
* * * *
Sirdar (W.A.) has bowed to the inevit-
able and made preparations to wind up. During an
unprofitable existence, extending over some years,
33,000 tons of ore were dug out of the old General
Gordon mine, in the Kanowna district, for a recovery
of about £25,000. As is usual in cases where a
poor and struggling co, is working a low-grade show,
most of this return went back into the mine, and
the remainder was spent on plant and equipment.
Shareholders got nothing. Latterly yields have been
so disappointing, and prospects so discouraging,
that there was nothing for it but suicide. Arrange-
ments are now being made to realise on the assets,
which chiefly consist of 20,000 tons of accumulated
residues, and some odds and ends in the shape of
plant.
* * * *
Associated Northern (W.A.) has now got
all the water it wants from the Government scheme,
and is confident of treating from 10,000 to 12,000
tons a month on its Ora Banda show. The first
(partial) clean up realised £2300 from 2155 tons,
but solutions absorbed some of the gold. The next
return is not expected to be anything to blow about,
but the one after may. Costs, including “every-
thing,” are not expected to exceed iOs. a ton, and all
over this, minus London exes, will, it is alleged, be
available for dividends.
* * * *
Lawlers (W.A.) has diminished in the last
12 months from a flourishing centre to a dis-
mal community of 50 souls, six pubs, a'
store or two and many goats. All this trans-
formation is the result of the closing down of the
Northern mines. Several shows in the neighborhood
are believed to have the makings of good gold pro-
ducers in them, but the shoots are small, and, even if
they do turn out as well as the oldest optimistic in-
habitant anticipates, they won’t be much. The
chilly hand of Death seems to be pressing on the
locality.
Seen through the eyes of a Government
field geologist. Kurnalpi (W.A.) is sized up as fol-
lows: (1) More reefs and lodes may be found in the
vicinity of the old alluvial deposits. (2) Quartz
reefs, though numerous and strong on the field, do
not appear to live at depth, or carry payable gold
for any depth. (3) As rich contact gold has been
found in places—there may be other undiscovered
patches elsewhere. (4) Existence of payable lode
formations in the district considered doubtful. (5) As
regards the claims where the recent sensational finds
were made, other rich discoveries may be hit on, but,
in the absence of proof, the possibility of locating
anything else but an occasional dab is doubted.

To date, £3,300.000 has been recovered by
amalgamation from Westralia’s State batteries, and
the revenue derived from treating residues brings the
total recovery up to close on £4,000,000. The
aggregate crushing capacity of the mills is 280,000
tons per annum, but only about one-fifth of this ton-
nage is at present being handled.
High Finance in Collins-street is rubbing
its eyes and asking, “What next?” Everything was
sailing along smoothly when a raging gale about the
brown coal deposits and the State electrification
scheme upset things. No one knows quite how long
Morwell leases have been shepherded, or when Croker
first got into Altona. But everyone knows that three
big German brown-coal experts came out under the
direction of a Collins-street crowd to measure up
Morwell, and that they folded up their tents and
moved off as silently as the Arabs in the desert.
What they reported isn’t known outside, but the
attempt to get a concession to run electric cables
along the Gippsland railway line from Morwell con-
veyed the warning that tile pitch was being pre-
pared for big things; and now one of the Germans
has returned, and the Melbourne Lignite Electric
Proprietary Co., Ltd., has been registered. This is to
take over the goods and chattels of the Victorian
Minerals Co., Ltd. If no one had queered the pitch
no doubt there would have been a pass on to a big
co., according to the ways of high finance. Mertz*i
return to tell Premier Watt what to do with the
electrification of the railways has pricked most of the
bubbles. He goes for brown-coal with a central
power station at Spottiswoode; but this, according to
the Cabinet, means that tenders are to be invited
from private suppliers of electricty for two other
stations. This was too thin for even the Argus,
so it thrust fusty ideas into the background, and
demanded that if there is to be national ownership
of the railways the source of power must be in the
hands of the State. In turn, the Age, having cut
down the gas monopoly, can’t logically build up an
electric one, so it has joined in the hunt. At which
High Finance wonders whether it has struck a land-
scape or an iceberg. Meanwhile, Labor looks at all
this as a step in the direction of nationalising the
coal deposits, for which it has battled for so long.
Duke Extended, Maryborough (Vic.), was
formed to pick up the Duke lead beyond where
it was left by the English crowd that took the
Duke United off Bill Baillieu’s hands when liis high-
est ambition was to make money out of Victorian
mines. Old Sam Body used to swear that the gutter
made to the north in a straight line from the Duke
boundary. Instead it curled away out of a big crab-
hole, and hasn’t been heard of since. Baring showed
that it ought to leave the lease to dip into the Duke
and Main leads claim. Bumpy ground was found
beyond the crab-hole, but no one is prepared to take
his oath that the big in-and-out workings of the Duke
United beyond the crab-hole were ever on the true
glitter. Long after Bull dropped the claim, and his
gilt at the same time, James McCrorey agreed to have
another cast for the lead. This first draw was a
failure. So he cast back to do more under level
work. The tally now is that bores reveal a gutter
450 ft. wide with a fair sample of gold from every
bore. Should the co. genuinely prove an extension
of the Duke lead in a well-defined gutter, it has more
that the usual mining hope to go on with, especially
if the lead is on a north and south course. A good
sign is that the quartz leaders have been cut in the
under-level.
“Ruat Coelum”: Victorian Central Coal
and Iron Mining Co., at Lai Lai (Vic.), has, so to
speak, blossomed forth in a prospectus after having,
in mixed metaphor, been in process of hatching for
several years. For two of those years the co. has
had an emissary in London, trying "to tap John Bull
for the ooftish wherewith to mine brown coal and
make iron by electricity, and, incidentally, to sup-
ply electric power to a formidable list of towns as
far as 50 miles away. The mine was worked for
brown coal 25 years ago, by shafts and drives, all of
which are now full of water. The prospectus’ author
seems to think the mine can be worked by open cut,
or inclined shaft, or by tunnels. Also, the coal
might be raised by suction through an iron cylinder,
or it might be blown up to the surface by forcing
compressed air through tubes sunk in the lignite-
or a better way to raise it would be to treat it like
petroleum, and sink (say) to 5000ft.,* and wait till the
fine, brown coal was driven into the air by the
artesian water, just like an oil “gusher” in Pennsyl-
vania. And these three proposals, like those in the
prospectus, are chiefly rubbish. So is the suggestion
that iron can be made cheaply by electricity. This
new practice has been well tried in the Cold Country,
and entirely abandoned, because of its excessive
cost. The open hearth furnace still holds its ground
as the cheapest and most satisfactory method of
making pig-iron, and it is, merely silly to suggest
that brown coal can be raised at such a low cost at
Lai Lai as to generate electricity as cheaply as by
water-power in Sweden. Besides, the centre of the
coal lease is three miles on the plan from the
ancient pits of iron ore, so that a tramway
must be made, either to take the coal to
the iron, or the iron to the coal, both of which
will cost money. The present issue of 35,000 shares
at 2s„ will, at the outside, only produce £3500
which won’t go very far towards carrying out even
one of the four objects of the co.—-wh'ch are the
supply of power, smelting of iron, making briquettes
and making bricks and pottery. In fact, it will only
go _ far enough to get a proper report on the am-
bitious scheme outlined by the prospectus, from some
competent German chemist who understands brown
coal and its value. There is no lignite mine worked
in England, and, therefore, there is no English en-
gineer who can handle a big lignite project like this
at Lai Lai. The main value and interest of the Vic-
torian Central Co.’s prospectus lies in the fact that
C. H. Merz has brought under notice the obvious
fact that in its brown coal deposits, covering hun-
dreds of square miles, Victoria has an asset of more
than enormous value. There are billions of tons of
brown coal to be used as fuel, and it will take mil-
lions sovereigns to shift the goods.
Central Red, White and Blue, Bendigo,
has paid out another 20s. on account of gold being
got in the western side of the formation to the north
at 318 ft. Co. has already touched the £90,000
dividend mark, as the result of two years’ work, thus
showing that there is still some good stuff left in the
reefs of the Golden City. A lot is now being made
of the fact that Lansell’s Little 180, next door, has
been stacking up profits for the owners out of the
same run of stone, but the level on the eastern side
of the Central Blue, at 318 ft., didn’t disclose anything
phenomenal. It was only when a crosscut went into
the spurs on the eastern side that better stone was
got, and, as one man puts it, “the saddle presented a
more expansive appearance.” The distance to the
Little 180 boundary is 500 ft., and a lot can happen
in that distance, as has already been proved on several
occasions in the present workings of the mine. The
iaws of the office, which had ben hanging lower and
lower since the battery got go ; ng, have tightened
wonderfully since the crosscut went out.
Tin is quoted in London at £224 per ton
(22401 b.), which is exactly 2s. per lb., and black
tin oxide is quoted at 395. 6d. per unit in Sydney.
At that rate 70 p.c. is worth £l3B ss. per ton. A
large proportion of alluvial tin assays from 70 to 74
p.c. pure tin, so the Briseis, of Tasmania (alluvial),
with an output of 45 tons of black oxide per month,
is able to declare a 6d. ( £15,000), making the total
dividends 12s. 3d. per share ( £367,500). Briseis
has 600,000 shares, so it goes without saying that
the profit on 600 tons ‘ of black tin—rather more
than the annual output—must be very high to pay
two or three 6d. dividends a year. This year Is. per
share has been declared, and a total distribution of
Is. 6d. per share (£45,000) is expected. The in-
creased gold yield is a heln, still Briseis has a fleet
of dredges to keep in repair, which takes some
of the gilt off. During August the Wallace dredging
area yielded 6790 z. of gold from 132,800 cubic
yards. The previous yields were: July, 9480 z. ; June.
8650 z. ; May, 7830 z. This dredging property was
acquired in 1909, and has become the most pro-
ductive in Victoria (32750 z. in four months). There
are 2112 acres on Yackandandah and Sandy Creeks
(N.E. Victoria). Property was purchased for
£60,000, some of which is payable out of profits or
alternatively out of anything over 2d. per cub. yard.

For 12 weeks to September 4, Mount
Lvell (Tas.) smelted 74,296 tons of ore for 1568 tons
ol blister, containing 1550 tons of copper, 101,1070 z.
silver, and 19970 z. of gold. Average, 2.08 p.e. cop-
per, 1.360 z. silver, 14 grains gold. Precious metals
are worth, approximately, 6s. per ton of ore, so
Lyell cannot expect salvation from that source if the
base metal declines to a low price again. It will
take Lyell all its time to produce 3500 tons of copper
this half-year (ending September 30), for up to Sep-
tember 4 the total was 2867 tons. At the mines the
heavy weather has interfered with open-cut work,
and the mullock for refilling depleted stopes has been
in short supply. Boring northwards in search of ore
extending into the Comstock lease, has not so far re-
sulted in any important discovery, but when the
ground floor at the north end of the 1100 ft. level is
enlarged diamond drill work will be resumed, with a
better chance of success. There are over 900,000
tons of ore blocked out in the North Lyell, and a very
much larger quantity of low-grade fluxing ore in the
parent mine.
Winton (Q.) reports another opal find
at Britcher’s Creek, on the dusty track which leads
to far-out Boulia. The report is good, as all first
reports are. The opaliferous district in Western
Bananaland, like the sapphire country further east,
has had a big struggle to live. Opal output to date
is estimated at £169,500, but no one can live on
“output to date” any more than the mill can grind
wheat with the water that has passed to date. And
current output is none too gorgeous.

The great advance made in the construction of In-
ternal Combustion Engines has now been further
demonstrated by the fact of Petrol, Benzine, Kero-
sene and heavy Oil Locomotives being brought into
daily and successful commercial use. The machines
are thoroughly reliable, and are cheaper in very
many instances than steam or electric locomotives.
Messrs. Ironside, Son and Dvckerhoff, of London,
have supplied a great number of their famous “New
Century” locomotives nearly all over the world, and
their machines are even working continuously and
safely in “gassy” coal mines. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne, will be pleased to supply further par-
ticulars.*
