A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 23, 1912.
My Dear Arini. —
That dark-eyed, white-toothed young Miss,
the unmarried bud of the Mat Harris
family, is engaged. She’s going to give her
hand, and her flashing smile, and a snug
marriage portion, to one Rupert Cox, form-
erly of Melbourne. He has shifted his
moorings to this port. Ijptham, the large,
square-shouldered mansion that eyes the
rising sun from Darling Point (once the
Mitchell homestead, but now the property
of Knight Mat Harris), is said to be in-
cluded in the Ruby Harris dower.
An Australian in Kaiser-land sends a few
aeroplane remarks, which are hereby boiled
down and passed to you: “Invited for a
three-hours’ flight in the Zeppelin airship,
‘Victoria Louise.’ Left Hamburg early
in the morning. The huge machine fled
through the air to Kiel at the rate of SO
miles an hour —or less—to see the opening
of the regatta. A lovely sensation, accentu-
ated by sitting in cane armchairs in a long
observation car. Washed down our flight
with champagne and nibbled caviare sand-
wiches. over Kiel Harbor about 11
a.m. Saw the Kaiser standing on the deck of
his yacht, the Hohenzollern, waving his cap
to our airship. We sent him a postcard per
aerial post. This consists of a small oilskin
bag attached to a long streamer with the
German colors, which is dropped from the
airship, and generally finds its destination.
Hamburg, a lovely town rather like Syd-
ney, with a fine harbor and beautiful homes
scattered about its shores.”
Kirkb.v Lunn is steadily luring the multi-
tude to listen to her siren song. Saturday
night’s concert only left a few vacant chairs
tor the nipping draughts (a Town Hall
specialty) to whiz round, while Arthur
Mason, at the organ, plunged into the
National Anthem with his hands and feet.
Necks were twisted in search of Governors
or wandering Helps, but the only suite in
sight was upholstered in cretonne, and
stowed away in the Northern Gallery. It
was obviously to let. Plainly, the organist
had got away before the starter’s signal.
The' voice of the Chamberlain rounding
up his flock in the corridor then trickled
through the chinks in the gallery doors, and
the Denmans made a wary descent to their
P] a teau ; The organ bellowed a double dose
of God Save,” and the multitude broke out
111 joyful greeting to the G.-G., smacking its
hands and[ . stamping the soles of its shoe
leather. Since. the days of the Rawsons,
who were on friendly terms with every man,
woman and dog in the street, Sydney audi-
ences have turned a languid eye towards
the vice-regal perch at local concerts. So the
Gee-Gee, who blushed like a pale pink radish
over the edge of the balcony, had solid
reasons for looking as if he liked the fuss.
Arthur Mason, with a Bach “Fugue in G
Minor,” and a dreamy lullaby by himself.
“A Venetian Serenade” (a charming trifle),
got the municipal organ again on the trail.
Fiddler Ribaupierre and pianist Laur-
ence Phillip collaborated in Schumann’s
“Sonata in A Major” (all the majors and
minors seemed to be out on the spree the
other night). The mournful baritone,
Andrew Shanks carolled “O Star of Eve ’’
and William Murdoch had his fling at the
piano with three Chopin studies before the
siren Kirkby Lunn allowed her matchless
contralto to float across the hall. Her
breathing is one of the small miracles of her
art. It must occur somewhere. But neither
the longest phrase in the “Delilah” aria, the
subtle changes in two songs by Brahms, nor
any other perfect. sample of her vocal craft,
the other night, hinted at a breathing appar-
atus under the delicate bodice of veiled apri-
cot satin.
About the most picturesquely dressed
young man in this city is Fred "Niblo (of
Noo Yark), the Wallingford of the “Get-
Rick-Quick” co. The creases of his trouser-
legs are absolutely distracting in their beauty
and mathematical accuracy. His satin-
lined coats open over waistcoats that reduce
the wearer of domestic architecture, i.e., the
huckback chest-protector, to a state of
green-eyed frenzy. Before the beautiful
Aibio came to town the silver-gilt youths of
these parts looked half-choked by their new
neckwear, which is after the fashion of a
modified Beau Brummel stock. It rode
up over their collar-fences and backed into
their noble necks. See Niblo’s high stock-
cravat with one small jewel flashing in the
centre as he saunters into a fashionable
hostel. Then die, or get out of the clothes-
horse business.
Mrs. G. Kellick. who is a sort of compo-
site mother, grandmother and great-aunt to
the E resh Air League, attended its 21st
birthday greeting last week. She was in the
first lighting rank when it first struck the
golden idea of hurrying the sick worker from
the overcrowded city tenement house to green
paddocks where the lamb frisks near the
mint, and so on. The Fresh Air League has
various strenuous “stickers” on its com-
mittee, who will only be removed by Death
from service to the Cause. Mrs. Ernest
Broughton, Miss McCarthy and a few others
of the same fibre who belong to the original
Fresh Airers, were very firm in their accus-
tomed places when the Chelmsford lady sat
in the 21st meeting chair.
The last American mail brought the glad
news that Mrs. Hugh Ward was staying
under the domestic tile of the Bert Lews in
N’York.
Concerning the Gale comet, which walks
the evening sky in the direction of the
Southern Cross, I hear that comet-catching
used to be a recognised industry in the U.S.
Prof. Barnard built himself a large house in
Tennessee out of the proceeds of fishing with
a telescope for new stars with fluffy little
fails. He had a lot of luck, and got about
£4O (not dollars) for each stray he trapped
with the glass. Other astral-fishers in the
U.S. as elsewhere have been less lucky,
many, indeed, having caught nothing more
merchantable than sneezes.
Miss Rose Scott, who might be a militant
suffragette in the Old Land, if she weren’t
a Peace Society enthusiast in this, sends me
a letter from the National Union of Women’s
Suffrage societies (London). This docu-
ment deals agitatedly with the damage that
the incendiary, and the window-smasher, and
the hat-bashing brand of lady vote-chaser is
doing the cause. A codicil earmarks the
three groups of women’s franchise support-
ers in the House ,of Commons. These are
the Conservatives, who would fling a grudg-
ing measure of approval to ladies who owned
something in their own right. Secondly, the
adult suffrage M.P. (rather scarce), who
distributes his benediction on every petticoat
in pursuit of the balUt-box. The Norwegian
franchise is the last log in the track. Its
supporters would (with many mental reser-
vations), hand women householders, and the
legal proprietors of male householders, the
v ote. _ Lloyd George and Sir Edward Grey
are said to be biting gently at the “Nor-
wegian” compromise. Lastly, the “National
Union,” of which Mrs. Fawcett, LL.D., is
top lady, hungers for Australian news re
the nerve-racking theme—one woman, one
vote.
The Australian wild flower rally bursts
into bud at the Town Hall as this meets
your long-distance gaze, Arini. The Chelms-
ford dame is billed to tear it open with a
few botanical remarks. But the joy of
addressing the lilly-pilly by its fancy name,
Eugenia Smithii,” or bounding up to some
other humble herb to stroke its back and
call it “Darwinia fascicularis” can be yours
or mine without paving extra gate money.
There seems to be no patent remedy to
exorcise the small child (usually overdressed
to the verge of physical anguish), swelling
with importance behind a presentation bou-
quet. This species haunts every charity
show, and sidles up to the victim (generally
the wife of a Governor, Admiral or Mayor)
and hurriedly butts her with a heavy floral
trophy. At the recent garden bazaar in the
grounds of the Justice Pring domicile (Stan-
more) a male infant made the expected rush
at the vice-regal She, and dashed a bunch
of flowers into her firm right hand. Some
day the promoter of a raid for charity’s
sake will try the effect of a bold advt. to
say that the sale of socks will open itself, that
there will be no presentation marigolds, and
that any person who thirsts for a platform
appearance at the tender age of eight, nine
or 10 vears, will be briskly spanked in a tent
set apart for that purpose.
Placards of invitation are “out” for the
opening of the new Zoo, Athol Bight, where
hot water may be obtained by incorrigible
picnickers free of cost, or less, all the year
round. October 14 is the date of the Lion
and Tiger display.
The Tony Horderns will give a decorative
private hop under their own roof during the
October blizzard of festivities, likewise the
Jim Fairfaxes and the Sep. Levys. The
private dance is getting back into its old
niche under the domestic shingle, and the
average charity ball wears a distressful
countenance in consequence.
Two youthful visitors at State Govern-
ment House are the Hon. Margaret Best
and Miss Lees, sister of the little A.D.C. of
that name. Best is the tame or household
nomenclature of the Wynford familv which
is not old enough to turn blue-mouldy on its
ancestral tree. The first Baron (1824)
dabbled in law as Justice of Common Pleas,
and the sixth Baron Wynford is the parent
of the Chelmsford’s overseas guest. The
Hon. Samuel John, who’ll probably climb
under the present lord’s coronet later on,
follows the useful trade of growing tea-
leaves in Ceylon.
The Chelmsford’s garden party on October
10 will be their principal votive offering to
the stern, insistent demand of the well-to-do
for free entertainment during the week of the
Horse.
The Spencer Bruntons, when the last mail
left England, were dashing through Siberia
and other agitating patches in red Russia.
The recent amateur revival of “School” re-
minds me that Robertson wrote this comedy
in 1869, as contemporary matter. In the
Victoria Barracks production the male stars
got into the garb of the late ’3o’s and mid
’4o’s, and were, therefore, about 30 vears
older than they had any right to* be.
“Naomi” (the linotype tripped over this
name last week) is the sparkling type of
soubrette part the modern dramatist seems
to have mislaid.
Miss Grant Bruce (of the Melbourne
Age), the vivacious writer of bush stories
for the young child, is snatching a holiday
in this town.
Which branch of the numerous (Sydney)
Manning clan owns Mrs. Harold Manning
and the Misses Manning, I wonder? Any-
how, they’re billed as settled down in Paris to
study art and music for some time. This
sounds a large order, but perhaps they’ll
work it out in heats, with an hon. referee
to act as umpire.
“Agnes Storrie” (Mrs. Ivettlewell), the
well-known writer of melodious verse, is
dropping her locum tenens pen for the Syd-
ney end of The Australasian this week.
While, the usual nib was careering over the
face of the globe, “Agnes Storrie” draped the
banal dress report with a few literary frills
which that dreary topic doesn’t usually get.
Percy Hunter, N.S.W.’s tireless globe and
immigrant hustler, is pushing Continental
languages into his youthful offspring, at the
rate of 70 miles an hour or thereabouts.
After spending a strenuous time, intellectu-
ally speaking, in Berlin (under the eye of
their mamma), the two boys are now taking
a header into French verbs in Paris. Ru-
mors reach me of a brief breather devoted
to tearing across the French map, from Bou-
logne to Normandy, on rubber-tyres, but, re-
calling the furious earnestness and work-
hunger of the family, I discount them by
60 per cent., or more.
Mrs. Langloh Want, who’s one of the
amateur cast in the “Importance of Being
Earnest,” has some stage experience to back
up her performance for the “Ministering
Children’s” League. As Miss Newton, she
toiled for a while in legitimate drama. By
the way, it was her brother, a former
Chelmsford A.D.C., who married Miss Kath-
leen Verney. October 2 is the date of the
impending flurry in pursuit of the nimble
charity shilling.
Woman, plain and fancy, is beginning to
make herself a public and uproarious nuis-
ance at political and other meetings in this
city. Even the country mouse, since the
franchise drove her into the murky atmo-
sphere of politics, is often reported as a
persistent interjector. The practise of in-
sulting inoffensive word-spinners has long
been the privilege of the male barracker,
and he is welcome to it.
Society sent very few of its members tc
the Town Hall, when the meeting on the
vexed question “To go —-or not to go” was
torn limb from limb. But the plain citi-
zeness, getting her back up and her temper
on end, was the chief offender in the matter
of howling down Minister Beeby. Granted
that he was self-invited, and even allowing
a wide margin for the excitement of the
moment, he had a right to a hearing. Mayor
Clarke saw the tactical blunder of howling
down a Speaker who had the courage to
address a furiously hostile gathering of
rather less than 4000 persons. That’s the
Town Hall limit when it’s jambed to the
doors, but it lacked the squeeze, and showed
vacant chairs in spots of space. It was the
shrill treble of the noisy women in the body
of the hall which led the 'uproar. If the
woman’s vote is going to create a shrieking
sisterhood, who defy the ruling of a just
chairman (witness the frantic efforts of
Mayor Clarke, the other night) and “down”
an opposition speaker, so much the worse for
the woman movement.
Invitations are out for an Australian
Club Ball on the Bth October. Also, a num-
ber of bachelors, mostly members of the
Macquarie-street edifice, have asked their
friends to dance and eat at a tea-room about
the same time.
The M.L. Government is going to make a
bold advt. of its wares in this city as this
reaches you. Dominion Day (September
26) will be celebrated in the local M.L.
Tourist Agency from 11 a.m. to 3 p.m.
Business men only are invited. They’ll put
their commercial wits into Maoriland decor-
ations, indigenous to the soil. If they put
their knives and forks into anything edible
it will bear the M.L. cheese or butter brand.
As a mild house-warming or something of
the kind, for the youthful visitors, Misses
Best and Lees, a small dance will shake the
rafters of State Government House before
this letter greets you. A chaperon or two
may have been inserted into the proceedings,
but young men and maidens will chiefly
frolic under the vice-regal eye.
Oswald Anderson has written some tune-
ful music for “Two Short Songs.” The
words are adapted from the Rubaiyat of
Omar Khayyam. They side-step the lover
in “Love’s Wish,” and twine a graceful
melody round “Regret,” F. R. Hedges
shakes his lutfe on the old theme, “Sigh no
more, ladies,” from “Much Ado About No-
thing.” He makes more ado about some-
thing with a brisk accompaniment. Still
another song-march. “The Australian
Cadets,” by Robert P. Thomson, ought to
convince those khaki saplings that, if they
don’t grow into sturdy limbs of Defence,
this nation will inquire the reason why.
Palings are responsible for this rush of home-
made music.
That sunbaked elongation of six feet fouv-
and-a-half inches, Broome Smith, F.R.G.S!,
unloaded some good material at the Town
Hall last week. Traveller Smith, however,
has none of the instincts of a showman. He
recklessly gave away, beforehand, in columns
of press interviews, the heart of his African
story. Having read with starting eyes, for
one penny, the details of High Life in
Pigmy Land, and the daring deeds of gav
young bloods in Ashanti, that portion of the
public which likes to sip its adventures
through a fireside straw, stayed at home on
the lecture night. Most of the lantern slides
were new, and 28 years in the land of ivory,
rubber and witchcraft made a solid joint of
meat. But the prime cuts had been given
away—and very few citizens wanted a
second help at two or three shillings a head.
Prof. David presided over an aching void of
empty “reserved” seats. Under cover of the
twilight gloom, while the cannibal gentry
were floating across the screen, the back
seat holders were beckoned to the front.
They made a joyous stampede and helped to
take the chill off the lonely heathen troglo-
dites on the desert of white sheet.
Mosman, assisted by Neutral Bay and
North Sydney, held its sixth annual ball in
aid. of. the Mater Misericordiae Hospital,
which is located in the vicinity of Crow’s
Nest, North Sydney. Warringah Hall.
Neutral Bay, was the scene of the
revel, and the gentle Lady Mayoress lent
her aid. Fancy sets and individual fancy
costumes were the evening’s attraction for
the onlookers. There were dainty and
demure 1830 crinolined damsels, who con-
trasted strongly with a tribe of aboriginals
and their piccaninnies and lubras. There
were Esquimaux with sleds and a tame Polar
bear; semi-barbaric Valkyries with flowing
hair and golden armor, and girls in half-and-
half costumes of black and white. Also,
there was a highly original set, labelled
Daily News. Familiar caricatures of our
great morning dailies, closely followed bv
the Speaker and his Sergeant at Arms, led
a procession of 24. In their wake came
Shipping, Sport, Billiards, Counter Lunch
(a Domain resident bearing a tray of coun-
ter delicacies and a dipper of foaming beer).
Music (with her miniature piano) and
Gilded Finance, her gown jingling with coin
of the realm. The Gas Bill was very pro-
minent, in the shape of a stork-like birl with
an enormous bill and flashing orbs. He paid
marked attention to the Baby Bonus, and
seemed on very good terms with a leg-ironed
Chidley labelled “Dangerous.” An evicted
Governor-General, in his cocked hat, looked
rather dejected as he grasped his luggage in
one hand and some camping-out utensils in
the other. The Law, Military, Advertise-
ments, Amusements, Strikes (represented by
a red-bordered white skirt with a matchbox
corsage) were there, too. Only the Church
was missing. Perhaps its stipend was not
up to a night out such as this. A real live
baronet appeared as a “Woman of No Im-
portance,” and another was disguised in the
garb of a gentleman of the 20th century.
.The forthcoming dance on H.M.S. Drake
will be the last of its kind on any British
naval flagship in these waters. After the
final quack on the Drake (which soon steams
away to the grey North-land) the Aus-
tralian squadron will sit down at this end
of the fighting line.
The ball at Federal Government House
on October 4 is another final or
dead finish. For the sound of packing up is
heard in the land, and the Governor-Gen-
eral’s luggage is ready for the Great De-
parture from N.S.W.
Sincerely yours,
VANDORLAN.
A suburban judgment and some reflections
thereon, xl fair young bride, whose husband
is not rich, recently set up housekeeping in
a “good” suburb. She had to do her own
housework; but there wasn’t much of it,
and, being a dainty girl, she didn’t see why
she would make herself a disordered appar-
ition in the process. So she designed a
pretty dusting cap with ribbons to protect
her hair, and did the job nicely and just as
effectively as any ramshackle and maudlin
charlady. Some curl-papered and dressing-
gowned cats saw the vision going about her
household duties, hurriedly convened a back-
fence meeting, and came to the conclusion
there could be no marriage lines in the little
establishment—all on the evidence of the
pretty dusting cap. According to their way
of looking at it, matrimony is inseparable
from awful flannel wrappers, haystack coif-
fures, and the morning air of dilapidation
that shatters a bridegroom’s illusions and
makes watchful bachelors fear the altar as
they do the scaffold. So once more Frowsi-
ness is placed second to Holiness in Life’s
handicap. Between the unbrushed suburban
cats and the anti-ocean Wowser who stands
on the beach and waves a threatening brolly
at the Sunday surf, Cleanliness gets a very
poor run for the money it expends on soap,
ribbons and bathing dresses.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 23, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Rather unexpectedly, the G.-G. attended
the Gaud Mayor’s dance in most of his offi-
cial haberdashery and wearily trod a cere-
monious measure with the Gaudess, while
State Governor Fuller pranced gaily with the
black-clad lady of Admiral Creswell. The
Gaudess was amazingly maize-colored, with
gold threads in old lace and a purple sash.
The Fuller lady, fair and daring, wore a
brilliant pink-red sal,in with a tiara flashing
like an electric sky-sign. The Forrest dame
trailed wattle-yellow garments from neck to
toe and her private roof regalia. There were
no flies, crows or vultures on this function.
At 10 o’clock, the hour of the official step-
ping, a great buzz of anticipation went up ;
but it was the buzz of a swarm of honey-
laden bees. Supper was strewn in various
parts of the building, and the new dining-
room of the adjacent coffee palace was also
utilised, in order that the famishing would
have plenty of room to charge. Every avail-
able corner of the furbished and brilliantly-
lit Town Hall was made available for sit-
ters-out and card players so, though there
were 3000 in the building, there was a
reasonable amount of elbow room for every-
one.
The frocking was unusually good and fre-
quently heavily jewelled. Mrs. Edwin
Miller had emeralds and rubies in the em-
broidery of her black ninon. Mrs. Ross-
Soden positively clattered with pearl and
crystal fringe. Mrs. Mayoress Wilks, of
vice-regal Malvern, wore a gold-embroid-
ered tunic over her white dress; and Mrs.
Frazer, wife of the M.H.R., sported crystal
diamond hangings on her grey ninon gown.
Lesly Madden glittered and gleamed in a
black satin upholstered with jet; she pro-
vided a sombre contrast to Mrs. Geoffrey
Syme (just out from nursing her burglar-
mauled husband), who wore a coat woven
of pearls and lace over a dress draped in
ivory chiffon folds. The blue Chinese tunic
and trousers of the daintily-tinted wife of
the Consul-General struck the only quiet
dress note in the whole dazzling pageant.
During his visit to Melbourne, last week,
the spruce little G.-G. left the motors at
home and actually walked up C.ollins-street
on his own feet, with his blue suit unpro-
tected by overcoat or umbrella. Help Bart-
telot striding alongside was enveloped in a
checked blanket great coat, and bore an
umbrella to keep the witches off. But, then,
Barttelot is, comparatively speaking, com-
mon clay and possesses none of the divinity
which fences off a viceroy. All the same, if
it had commenced to rain, he would probably
have had to deliver up the umbrdlla or be
indicted on a charge of high treason.
The uproarious doings at the last night of
the Glaciarium are leaking out in cautious
whispers. Ten o’clock or 10.30 is the usual
hour for closing; but, for the final session,
the management generously allowed the
skaters to glide till one hour before mid-
night. When time was officially up, the
gong clanged loudly and attendants started
to shoo patrons off the ice. But manv of
them glided on defiantly. Once again' the
attendants implored them to desist and go
home. But they went smilingly on their
way, and appeared not to hear. Then said
the manager, “Hose the ice!” The hose was
run out; but before it had time to fill, fiend-
ish skaters cut it to pieces with their foot-
irons. Another hose was brought into play,
and began to splash ominously. Some of the
revellers put on macintoshes and skated
gaily through the spray. Suddenly a young
cadet of the Very Best order seized the
hose and turned it on the man who was
using it. The attendant fell and slid along
the ice under the force of the jet. Another
attendant grabbed the nozzle and turned the
stream on the cadet, who struggled and
fought to. regain possession of the weapon.
When he got it, he mischievously turned it
'on the watchers. Squeals and sobs of rage
went up from the previously merry specta-
tors, as the flying water, splashed and dam-
aged frocks and furs. Waterproofed police
finally restored sobriety and decorum. But it
is beyond the power of O’Callaghan’s force
to restore the ruined garments.
Some Hawthornites, bazaar-bent, are re-
hearsing. for a Pierrot performance in the
garden-girt hall which is affectionately
known as “the Kew Rec.” A man per-
former evaded the little co. the other day,
and his place is to be filled bv Colonel Bal-
lenger’s smart little sporting daughter. She
made such. a success-of her donkey-boy dress
at the “Kismet” dance that she has ceased
to feel truly happy in skirts.
A gnashing of cashboxes seems to follow
every charity entertainment which is at-
tended by society. The Best People buy
and sell tickets with a light heart, and then
often forget to forward the cheque to the
harassed treasurer. Thereupon the treasurer
resorts to every known polite device to jog
their memories. Lesly Madden, who looked
after the financial part of “The Cassilis
Engagement.” and Mrs. Jolley, cashboxer of
the Girls Training Home dance, are both
bothered at. present over this troublesome
part of their duties. It isn’t exactly bad-
debt collecting, and yet, in some respects, it
is worse than bad-debt collecting. And
worse than bad can only be described as
rotten.
Funds for the Babies’ Home are now
being chased by the Very Nicest people. Mrs.
Albert Miller’s Whernside f§te—due next
month—is at present being prepared for at
top speed. The secretary enlivened, the last
meeting by bursting forth in a cherry-red
hat and a blue dress from the interior of her
cherry-red and blue motor. In stage circles
it would have been described as “a splendid
entrance.”
The Melbourne Hunt Club escaped the
floods by a few days for the last run of
the season at Clyde, down towards the coast
swamp-lands; but the members had to gal-
lop through the preliminary showers of the
v ictorian Deluge. An early morning special
train carried a load of pink-coated men, but
only three of the hatpin sex —a McCullagh
Miss, from Woodlands, Miss Raleigh and
Miss Creswick. Locally the sporting Duffy
girls joined the adventurers, together with
Mrs. Bullock. Jack Baillieu, carrying a
stiff arm from a recent spill, cut out the
fences. Jim Osborne galloped with the best;
and the women of the district, preferring the
comfort of carriages to the saddle, whipped
up a tea party for the drenched sports-
women within coo-ee of the return train,
bnc Maxon, of the “Ben Hur” co., attended
the function in dandy attire, and was con-
spicuously ornamental at the refreshment
rendezvous.
This year the Wesley College Old Boys
are going to have a fancy-dress procession of
past and present students, as well as the
usual garden party and cricket match. The
gay pageant (it is determined to be gay)
will go round a large square that takes in
Chapel-street. It is said that the function
wnl mildly emulate the annual procession
of University students.
The annual kick-up of the Ilyams’ pupils,
which has been converted from a “ball”
into a “dance,” will happen (d.v). at St.
Hilda Town Hall on Tuesday, October 8. Of
fate years, the Hyams event has rarely or
never come to pass in the Gaud Mayor’s
municipal barn, as of yore. The frisky
suburb-by-the-sea has a somewhat larger
hall, with less draughtiness on the premises
than the place where the pneumonia comes
from.
Major-General Kirkpatrick’s lady has
strewn invitations amongst the nicest moth-
ers tor a garden treasure hunt on Thursday
ot this week If any unlucky infant chances
to exhume the forgotten victim of some rat-
trap tragedy, loud roars of indignation will
make the welkin ring and necessitate heavy
bribes of mdigestibles and inflated drinks.
Ihe treasure-hunt idea has its dangers.
In spite of the hostile elements of last
Ihursday night, a full Town Hall audience
gathered to hear Bostonian Bliss Knap talk
Christian Science—the creed which invites
,you t0 m Call yourself a liar every five min-
utes. The .audience came and went in a
nianner which bespoke the free show of no
particular grip. Pig-tailed misses and wild
Salvationists were numerous ; but the audible
words of the lecturer were few and far be-
tween, owing to the dry rattle of coughs
and incessant shuffling of feet. When Bliss
subsided in a sad way, there was a frantic
stampede to evade the suspected collection
plate. lam not quite clear if Christian
Science has ever managed to cure itself of
the terrible collection plate habit; but the
audience seemed disinclined to give it the
benefit of the doubt.
The Wattle Leaguers finished off last
week with a tea and bun outburst at the
Alexandra Club. Mrs. Geelong Watt did
the talking for Dame Staughton, who was
occupied with an attack of pleurisy. The
Fuller lady is to be asked to patronise the
next raid on the suffering golden groves.
•Tack McComas, who married a Geelong
heiress, a few weeks ago, nearly converted
Ins bride into a widow, during the honey-
moon among the snows of rugged Buffalo/l
forget just what death he escaped from, but
it was very thrilling. The two are now
safety and happily flatkeeping in a Mel-
bourne pile which is composed of lavers of
homes.
Dr. Mary Booth is back again in Mel-
bourne, looking much better for her health
trio to sunny Bananaland, where the winter
is more or less of a jest.
Miss Maggie Maguire is the latest recruit
to the Bush Nursing corps. She hails from
the comparatively urban Goulburn Valley,
where miles of river frontage are neatly
divided into smiling orchards, and neighbor-
bors yarn to one another over the fences.
And she has gone to work at Buchan, in the
richest and least accessible part of rugged
GiP’-'sland.
Talking of nurses—Miss lima Toomath,
who matroned the Foundling Hospital at
Last Melbourne for two or three years, has
put her quaintly pretty name on her own
biass plate. Weary of well-doing for mere
wages, she lately took over a private hospi-
tal at Malvern from the Bevan sisters. The
name, of Toomath is associated, thereabouts,
with the cure of souls, as well as cure of
bodies. Miss Ilma’s father, the Rev. An-
drew, abides at Armadale these times. He
is said to be “one of the oldest Anglican
parsons living”—which means, presumably,
living in Australia. In England there are
country vicars who have lived the simple
life for ages and ages, with a repertoire of
three or four time-honored sermons which
they mumble in turn to their drowsy flocks,
on the rare occasions when the curate gets
a holiday. The very oldest Anglican clergy-
men have achieved their antiquity under
good old English conditions, I take it. This
country is not the best on earth for them.
The Norman Brookes baby began her
social career early by inviting most of those
who attended her parents’ wedding, two
years ago, to roll up and see her christened.
Norman Bayles, of the butterfly moustache,
played godfather, and the sedate Wrixon
lady solemnly undertook the duties and re-
sponsibilities of godmother along with Mrs.
Charles Emmerton. Little Cynthia masquer-
aded in her mother’s wedding veil. For the
subsequent feast, a layer of the wedding cake
was re-iced and served up. Amongst those
who trailed expensive clothes round the
house were Dalrymples, Dunbar Hoopers,
McKinnons, Millers, Fiskens and Fairbairns.
Ivy Clarke’s fashionable tailorings ran a
neck-to-neck race with the smart Parisian
garments of Gladys Chirnside, just back
from the Continental chiffon stores. The
adoring mother hung over the cooing new-
comer in a lace dress, ermine furs and a top-
dressing of the inevitable ostrich plumes. A
social gathering these days makes one’s
heart bleed for the unfurnished fowls that
must rush round the feather farms.

last week to marry Victor Ilyndman. Sister

Hotel, and brother-in-law handed over the
very dainty bride to her happy man. Two
cloud-grey and sky-blue bridesmaids hovered
prettily in the rear whilst Canon Gason
(what an ominous name!), of St. John’s,
blessed the pairing. When the bride put on
her headgear for the red carpeted sprint to
if was discovered to be an ador-
able blue confection sprinkled over with
rose petals.

A smart matron, who is noted for her
musical parties, has a glittering series of re-
ception rooms, half-curtained off from one
another and full of Venetian mirrors. The
other day, while her guests were listening
more or less to a piano rhapsody, a beaury
girl who had arrived late was observed out-
side, rehearsing her entrance against tlm
time when the music should stop. The fatal
mirrors passed on the joke to those inside,
and shrieks of merriment were stifled with
cambric as the unconscious one smiled and
smirked and bowed to herself. At last the
Rubinstein riot stopped, and the artless
maiden floated in wearing her most demure
air. She appeared to be extremely gratified
with the general smile of welcome with
which she was received.
A leading light or gas buoy in the

children are stated to »eed a mother’s care;
but as the family in question is a very well-
grown one, dear friends are busily emploved
seeking another reason for the dame’s sudden
loss of political enthusiasm.

Local health resorts having failed to effect a

to her sunny homeland.

A superior but unmusical person, who
finds it necessary for official reasons to sit
out many lengthy concerts, has found a way
to dissipate the awful tedium. He goes
armed to the uproar with a pair of powerful
glasses, and, when he has finished taking
observations of the platform star, carefully
searches the auditorium for the Prettiest
wrl Present. If the critical stare brings a
blush to the cheek of any competitor, he
only stares harder.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Every other woman wore a coat and skirt at the
Fort Adelaide Racing Club meeting on Saturday, for
the week had wound up with a damp curse arid a
meteorological howl, and the sporting of chiffons
would have been equivalent to a demand for pneu-
monia. Mrs. Rasp, Mrs. Herbert Rymill and Miss
Gardiner; Mrs. Hyland and Mrs. Jervois were amongst
the female enthusiasts present.
The G.-G. and several M’s.P. left us on Monday,
after a trifle of spade work at Port Augusta. The
latter township, by the way, is a rather pathetic
spectacle. It was expected to Boom, in no uncertain
fashion. And so the State sowed wharves and massive
public buildings, banks and churches all over it, and
then sat back and waited for the profits to come up.
They never did. On the contrary, Port Pirie came
with a rush, and knocked Augusta’s hopes out with
one .relentless punch. For years now it has been a
wilting place, filled with relics of great and awful
failed expectations. Maybe, with the arrival of the
transcontinental, its luck is due to turn.
By the way, G.-G., Denman mentioned, while he
was here, that he would come back in the summer
with a polo team. The cheers were simply deafening.
Polo is booming in this city for several reasons. For
one thing, so soon as a man gets married or engaged,
he taboos hunting; or has it tabooed for him. Women
consider that the aniseed sport is unduly dangerous.
Polo, on the other hand, they approve of, and their
male relatives are encouraged to play as long as
avoirdupois makes it possible for polo ponies to carry
them.
The Prince Alfred Old Scholars gave a dance in the
Norwood Town Hall-on Thursday evening. Mr. Miller
and Mr. Shuttleworth were the secretaries, and they
did their work like tradesmen.
The engagement is announced of Miss Lillian Davis
—a musical : lady with a milk and roses com-
plexion—and a Queenslander, whose name has eluded
me for the moment. It isn’t Smith, however.
The Ingrams settle permanently in Melbourne early
in October.
Miss Rose Loweay’s pictures, recently exhibited
in this city, won the critical admiration of Prime
Minister Fisher on Monday. He said the nicest
possible things of them, in the broadest, Scotch.
By the way, a lot is said of the woman-worker
who is continually robbing men of their billets,
but a big drapery house, ’t’other day, gave the
woman herself similar cause for complaint. One
of the firm’s windows displayed a bedroom, “taste-
fully” furn'shed with an “exclusive” suite, and two
large and brawny men roamed about in the window
o’ mornings, making the bed and removing dust from
the mirrors. Is this unjust competition, or is it a
gentle warning re a possible solution of the Marv
Anne problem ?
Cleric Severn —son of the artist of that name,
and a nephew of that Mrs. Ivenn’on whose husband
was once our bishop-—has renounced his vows of celi-
bacy, and become engaged. The girl comes from
the West Coast, where the Rev. S. has been evangel-

Cables from London announce the death of Mrs.
Morgan Hawkes, who left here early in the year, to
attend the marriage of her youngest daughter. Mr.
Hawkes died about 10 years ago.
Now that spring is fairly on us, excursion trains
on Saturdays and Sundays are beginning to fill up.
The hills thr's year showed the effect of the long, dry-
spell, and were slow in changing from drab to green,
but they’ve done it at last, and are smothered in a
thick, velvety mat of grass. Here and there are bril-
liant masses of dandelion and oxalis, smothering:
green in gold, and early wild flowers are beginning:
to add their colors to the carpet that Nature is
spreading. _ Nearer town, English trees are slowly
breaking into leaf, and flowering peaches are iri
bloom. In short—we’re looking our best, and we
know it, and we challenge comparison with any
town in the- Commonwealth that feels like putting*
up its money and having a race.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
The “hardy pioneers” • who did so much
for this country, and, incidentally, for them-
selves, have left P’ran (Vic.) a problem
which it doesn’t know what to do with. The
trouble is Chapel-street, which is so narrow
that a. lusty athlete could almost jump
across it. It is a busy thoroughfare, and it
gets more congested every day. Between l
frequent trams, more frequent motors, cabs, ]
carriages, carts, lorries, drays, buggies, soci- ;
ables. barrows, perambulators and other
vehicles it is so dangerous that the council- i
lors and other representative citizens hardly
dare risk crossing it. When the electric
trams come along and race up and down
like the Melbourne Cup, they will simply
not dare to cross. Of course, they want to
widen the streak of misery, but that means
buying up, demolishing and re-erecting about
two miles of shop fronts and other orna-
ments. to say nothing of the Town Hall, pic-
ture theatres, banks, etc. The owners of the
premises are demanding about £20,000,000 of
compensation; and P’ran can’t see it. Those
early pioneers left some heavy burdens to
posterity.

The Age casts Alfred Deakin off for good,
the Akgus allows its correspondents to re-
pudiate hint as a Liberal leader, but the
Women’s .National League embraces him.
Alfred may yet come back to State politics
and become a member of the House of Land-
lords.
Prime Minister Fisher’s reply to Admiral
Tate’s claptrap, re Australia being unable
to man its national navy (an accusation, by
the way ? that was palpably levelled to tickle
the ears of “Loyalist” groundlings in Can-
ada ), was fairly explicit. Said he : —
Until less than 12 months ago, other than recruit-
ing for the English navy, there has been no attempt
to get men for our fleet. The Commonwealth has
been handicapped by want of ships and shore estab-
lishments in which to train men. Notwithstanding
these drawbacks, there are drafts of men recruited
in Australia now in England, waiting to come out
with the ships. The cruiser Encounter, lent to us
with a nucleus crew, has a full complement of re-
cruits. Our shore establishments are full, and we
are extending them to make room for more recruits.
Within six months we have entered 135 boys on the
training ship Tingira. By the time the fleet unit is
ready, we shall be able to fill all the lower ratings
with Australians. The scheme works well and
smoothly.
Though rather a quarantine than a fiscal
matter, it is pleasing to note that Customs
Minister Tudor lately prohibited the impor-
tation into the Commonwealth of the dead
bodies of persons whose decease was due to
any infectious or contagious disease.
Heavens knows why anyone should ever
want to import such things! This paper
has never perceived any large demand for
the article —even when the spring sales are
on —and, anyhow, the Commonwealth itself
produces the normal supply with mathema-
tical regularity. Still, it is interesting to
note that in one item of commerce local in-
dustry is protected.
“G.V.W.”: Maoriland Customs Minister
Fisher proposes to send an officer to Eng-
land to prevent sweated goods from other
European countries being re-exported from
Fogland as the pure British article, and
gaining admission into Maoriland under the
preferential tariff. The proposal would be
all right if it were not lame in its feet and
rather buzzy in its head. Does Fisher
imagine that there is no sweating in Eng-
land? Also, does he imagine that one officer
can keep an eye on all the goods that are
sent out from Bull ports to Maoriland —
that he can know in advance every firm that
is going to send out goods by the next boat,
and get to that firm’s warehouse in time,
and inspect the goods, and avoid being
kicked for his interference? It looks a most
hopeless business.
“G.V.W.”: Observe the futility of the
Maoriland land purchase system:—
Iouseph Ward speaks: “It has taken the Govern-
ment the best part of 22 years to purchase £6,300,000
worth of land, and to settle 3500 souls on it.”
Iouseph on his own futility: “The former Govern-
ment did its best in the time at its disposal.” (Did
he want a millennium ?)
Iouseph, out of sad experience: “Freehold purchase
in quantity would lead to depreciation of the value
of land. Extensive private borrowing would lead to
higher interest and probably a slump.”
Yet, as a fact, Iouseph’s Government raised
land values steadily and on a system, and
used the fact that they had risen as a lure
for Cohen when negotiating fresh loans. The
land speculator, in turn, used the Govern-
ment valuation as a lever to extract higher
prices from the land-seeker. The land-
seeker was thus always between two devils,
each worse than the other, according to
whether you saw him from the end of his
tail or from behind a tree.

“The Nut” discourses:—
“Demos,” in his appeal (Bulletin 28/8/T2),
that State Labor members should be encouraged by
the party to contest Federal seats in Tasmania,
misses the main point at issue. In every other
State the local Houses are elected from single elec-
torates, or by the block vote, and where a seat is
given up by a sitting member the chances are that
the same party will win it again. In Tassy the
position is entirely different. The island is divided
into five districts, each of which returns six mem-
bers on the preferential voting system. The result
is that, even in the most conservative district, Labor
gets two seats, though in a minority of 4000 votes.
Suppose the holder of one of those seats resigns to
contest the Senate. Whether he wins or loses, his
State seat must be lost to his party in a straight-out
fight, and where there is at present only a single vote
between the two parties in the Assembly, there
would then be three. No one will deny that the
best tiio for the Senate can be selected from the
Assembly, but, if that means wrecking the State
Opposition, it may be bad business for democracy.
There is only one electorate where Labor can be
sure of winning a by-eleetion, viz., Darwin. In
all the others the party was beaten last April.
The claim of “Demos” that restriction of choice is
undemocratic, will scarcely hold water, as in this case
the question to decide is whether the interests of a
vast number of iState electors shall be considered, or
those of ambitious State members who would grasp
the bigger prize. If a special effort were necessary
in order to hold the Federal Parliament, the suggested
step might well be taken, because, after all, the
national legislature should have first call, but Labor
has a majority in the Senate for another three years,
and in Tas. it has no chance of winning any more
Rep. seats for the present, even with the strongest
men available. Whv, then, should the Executive risk
a reduction in the party strength in the State
House ?
A deputation called, the other day, on
N.S.W. Premier McGowen to utter a
muffled curse about the increased rents of
Sydney. Incidentally, it demanded that im-
migration should be instantly stopped as
regards the whole of N.S.W. —a great por-
tion of which has about half an inhabitaiit
to the square mile—because Sydney is
already overcrowded. The idea that immi-
grants might be sent to some place in
N.S.W. besides Sydney was beyond the
scope of the deputation’s politics. It had
evidently never heard of any other place.
This fragment is from the Brisbane
Courier of September 4, in the year 1562
b.w. (Before Willis) :
A most disgraceful scene occurred in the Assembly
last night. While the members of the House had
adjourned for refreshments, it was reported to Mr.
Buchanan, the eccentric and notorious member for
Morpeth, that Mr. Terry, another member, had called
him a blackguard. Buchanan then went up to Terry,
and struck him, whereupon the latter returned the
blow, and the little “mill” was ended by the by-
standers. It was rumored afterwards, however, that
Buchanan delivered a challenge to Terry, which that
gentleman very wisely tore up without reading.
When the House resumed, the circumstances were
reported to the Speaker, hut he ruled that he could
not interfere, as the fracas alluded to did not take
place within the precincts of the House. Buchanan
made an explanation, and, in the course of his re-
marks, called Terry a coward, but his words were at
once taken down, and he retracted. Subsequently,
while the House was in Committee, Buchanan, who
was intoxicated, several times interrupted the pro-
ceedings, and, the House having resumed, his con-
duct was again reported to the Speaker, who im-
mediately signed his warrant for the refractory
gentleman’s arrest. Buchanan struggled violently,
and the Sergeant-at-Arms had to be assisted by
several hon. members in ejecting him from the
House. He was locked up until the Assembly ad-
journed, and then discharged.
In those days the N.S.W. Assembly was very
like that wonderful gathering of hogs,
Huns, pushites and larrikins, the Hungarian
Parliament of 1912. The august body in
question has just met in an atmosphere of
curse and blither and free fight, and a con-
siderable-sized piece was bitten out of a dis-
tinguished nobleman of the Opposition. The
trouble arose out of the strenuous efforts of
the Opposition to disembowel the Cabinet.
THE FINAL JEER.
The Government made the formal announcement
in the House of Representatives to-day that there is
to be no new tariff during the session....It is to be
hoped that the Victorian Protectionists, who gave
their votes to Caucus Candidates at the last Federal
election on the plea that the Labor party was the
only one which would supply another dose of duties,
are satisfied. —Sydney Telegraph.

“Briner”: It is to be hoped that no dis-
aster will overtake our expensive plums, the
warships Australia, Sydney and Melbourne,
through lack of navigating officers. Natur-
ally, Britain will try to keep all the best
men for use in the coming scrap with Ger-
many, and the best men will want to stay,
because a row offers chances of rapid promo-
tion. Good navigators ought to be obtained
from our merchant vessels, if our naval
machinery is sufficiently elastic to allow it.
About getting scientific officers and handy
men there need be no special anxiety. Big
money will always draw them, even if they
have to be sought on land.

of country members, Victoria’s
Watt Ministry has driven a ten-
penny nail into the coffin of Closer
Settlement by moving amendments
to its own C. S. Amending Bill —
which amendments were, of course,
carried—providing that (1) an
owner of resumed land may keep
£lO,OOO worth instead of the £6OOO
worth formerly proposed; (2) a
closer settler may sublet his land;
(3) the restriction prohibiting the
transfer of these holdings to per-
sons who already own land in the
State worth more than £2500 be
abolished; (4) a settler may get
his Government grant—with the
right to transfer—at the end of 12
years instead of the 31 years orig-
inally agreed upon. Thus does the
Rich Landlord work his point and
play his old, disastrous, retrogres-
sive game of land monopoly. But when the
Federal land tax is increased he may find the
game not worth while.

have wives in every port, is it not possibffi

Bah ! the thing disgusts me! Sailors make

silly old English estimate of them is driven,

girls their chances as legitimate birthrate
enhancers are small. In the meantime they,

like water on beer and rum. A sailor in

better off; but the old British stigma still !
hangs to him like pitch, and will take a
lot of scraping off.

TIGHT!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Griffith yesterday foreshadowed the postponement
of sanctioned public works on account of the tight-
ness of the monev market. “At no period in the history
of the State,” he explained, “have so many railways,
irrigation works, harbor works, and school buildings
been under construction as at the present time, and
it is a vast pitv that the money markets of the
world have chosen this particular moment to get
‘tight.’ ” — Sydney Telegraph.
Tight!
Ev’ry money market’s tight!
Ev’ry precious golden quid is on a mad financial
“skite” !
British capital is reeling towards the railways of.
Cathay,
And it’s “Pass the bottle, brother!” all the bright
and careless day,
For the battleships and railways—for the docks,
canals and schools,
For the schemes of honest thinkers and the greedy
dreams of fools.
And the cash —cash —cash
Makes a loud metallic splash—-
Makes a tinkle and a rattle as it swiftly flows away
To accomplish distant wonders while the night suc-
ceeds the day.
Oh! it talks all sorts of lingos and it buys both beer
and bhang,
And the white men struggle for it like the shuffling
nigger gang;
Ev’ry camp and town is merry and there’s laughter
and delight.
But the joy is all suspended when the money market’s
tight!
Tight!
We have all been very tight!
We’ve been spending, strewing, flinging borrowed
money left and right!
Now there’s nothing in the bottle and it’s time to
sober up,
Or go reeling down to ruin with a more expensive
cup.
Cohen’s minted beer is finished and he offers us
champagne;
But the drunkard who has wisdom will not seek his
bar again.
And the cash —cash—cash
That has made us far too rash —-
That has lulled our honest hatred of the int’rest-
loving Jew,
And has kept us from the straight road where there
is no I O U,
Now is filling up the glasses of the Canuck and the
Chow,
And the Towel of Repentance binds our hot and
bursting brow.
So we find a flaming moral by the painful morning
light:
Lo! the, Borrower and the Market at the same time
can’t he tight!
N.S.W.
FOE.
THE LETTER OF THE LAW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The upsetting by the High Court of Mr. Justice
Isaacs’ judgment means that the Commonwealth At-
torney-General has completely failed in his attack
on the coal vend and the associated shipping com-
panies.—Tragic news item.
The Letter of the Law!
How beautiful it is!
The legal mice that gnaw
With practised tooth and jaw
Beneath the wainscoat quiz.
The Letter of the Law!
How sharp and keen it is!
The Letter of the Law!
Defiance bids to sense ;
The teeth that grind and gnaw
Have found at last a flaw
In this fine house immense.
The Letter of the Law
Now mocks our impotence.
The Letter of the Law!
What enemy need fear?
What need to hide the claw?
Rise tigers from your straw!
, Your allies are in here!
Oh! teeth that grin and saw!
Oh! cruel eyes that peer !
The Letter of the Law!
The rats hold high carouse!
No trap holds head or paw,
Or grips the crushed and raw
Remains of rat or mouse!
The Letter of the Law
Goes scamp’ring through the house!
N.S.W.
E. O’F.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Exactly what went wrong with referee
Arthur Scott at Sydney Stadium on Saturday night,
writer knoweth not; but, had he laid himself out to
make a hash of things he could not have done it
more thoroughly. It was an absolute masterpiece.
' His handling of the fight was slovenly, and his deci-
sion simply putrid. The difference in the weight of
Read and Til was said to be only four pounds ; but
Read looked easily a stone heavier ; and it is obvious
that the little Frenchman realised that to mix things
from the jump meant being worsted. And it did.
For in the early stages of a fight Read is a whirling
cyclone; an animated mass of aggression which
bounds, rushes and throws around blows like rain.
Even so hardened a bruiser as McCoy, who also looks
heavier and stronger than Til, found Read’s
early onslaught almost more than he could bear. So
the little Frenchman, it was obvious, had decided to
let Read exhaust that initial energy on thin air, and
when the first blush had worn off his aggressiveness,
to wade in. It was the right game, too. And he did
it. This idea that a man should get points for
coming in to attack, whether he makes good his
attack or not, is the most crack-brained of the many
mad notions held in Sydney regarding boxing. Til
eluded Read’s furious onslaught with considerable
skill, and when he had worn off the keen edge of his
aggressiveness, rushed in, swarmed all over him,
knocked him into the ropes twice, so that he would
have gone right down but for them, and scored a
clean, straight knock-down; and, finally, had him
hanging on desperately to see time through and avoid
a knock-out. Another couple of rounds at the most
would have seen Read beaten to ruins. Til fought a
scientific fight, and won absolutely beyond the shadow
of a doubt. Scott’s decision was most unfortunate,
and a rank injustice to a clever, gallant boxer.
There was one circumstance which makes the hand-
ling of the fight by Scott look very slovenly. In the
fourth round, Til suddenly doubled up, and his face
assumed an expression of intense pain. He claimed
to have been hit low, and staggered to the comer,
where he sat down for a brief space. Scott had seen
no foul blow struck, and Read’s look of surprise was
the expression of a conscientious man. The explana-
tion given afterwards was that Til, in fending a belly
rip, had knocked a drive of Read’s downward instead
of to the side, and thereby caused it to land in the
forbidden area. All this writer has to say is that
after having been hit in the same locality three times
in the course of a mis spent youth, he cannot under-
stand how the deuce Til came up so quickly and put
up such a wild-cat fight as he did. Writer has grave
doubts about the genuineness of that alleged foul
wallop. It certainly was not severe, or Til had not
resumed so gamely. If he was fouled, even accidentally,
Til was entitled to a verdict on a foul. If he was
not fouled, then Read should have got the decision on
the spot. It’s one thing or other, and all talk about
considering the public, and saving it from disap-
pointment, is just plain flap-doodle. The referee is
not there to consider the public which puts up the
money—he is there to see that both fighters get a
square deal, and to interpret a very plain set of
rules. It was obvious from the way that Til came
up and resumed the disagreement that he had never
been really fouled at all. He may have been pinked
low—but injured he certainly was not —unless he is
differently constituted to the writer. It was after
that alleged foul that he made an absolute chopping
black of the Tasmanian, and as referee Scott had
allowed the incident to pass, Til should have got the
verdict.
* * * *
It is not by any means certain, after all,
that The Mclntosh will succeed in landing Johnson
at Rushcutter’s Bay Stadium ; as that lengthy hum-
bug has been up to his old tricks again. His latest
flapdoodle was to stipulate that, in addition to the
$lO,OOO guarantee Mclntosh should also put up the
$5OOO expenses. Mclntosh snid he was agreeable,
whereupon Johnson cabled that the total $15,000
must be put up _ with a particular friend of his.
Then Mclntosh kicked. He wanted to deposit the
money in a bank—Johnson or his unknown friend
could nominate the bank, so long as it was one of re-
pute. But Johnson would agree to nothing but that his
nominee should hold the money. It was such an
obviously impossible condition put up to gain time,
that the Sydney promoter could do nothing but get
off the grass. At the time Johnson fondly believed
he would get £6OOO for fighting Jeanette in New
York. He was anxious to fight there, till he learned
with disgust that the Commission had no objection
to his fighting another nigger, but would not' allow
him to fight a white man, as it could see no white
man who had a reasonable chance. Johnson is a
great bluffer. While he was humbugging Mclntosh
in Svrlney with impossible conditions, be was us : ng
the offer of the Mclntosh to try to bluff a higher
price out of Gibson, of New York, for the fight with
Jeanette. Gibson got worried, and, going to the
long-distance telephone, rang up Johnson’s “Caf6 de
Champion,” as he calls it, in Chicago. Johnson was
there. “$20,000 is the best I can offer for a fight
with Jeanette on Labor Day!” said Gibson. “I can
get $20,000.f0r fighting suckers. My price is $30,000,
an’, sav, Mista Gibson, I mus’ knoo right soon, so’s
to begin and fix ma dates fer ma Australian tour.
They’re real anxious out ther’ to have me back ’gain,
Mr. G'bson, so Ah guess the $30,000 mus’ stand,
Mista Gibson.”* Then Gibson hung up and rang off,
and Johnson went back to attend to the “Caf6 de
Champion.”

“Magpie” : Re goal-kickers (B. 12/9/’l2).
The Messengers are not the only pebbles on the
sward. As regards mere numerosity, David
McNamara, the Essendon (Vic.) Soccer champion
licks them hollow. Up to time of writing he has
booted 99 goals in 19 games, and with possibly two
more matches —certainly one—to go, it is a cer-
tainty he will top the century. On April 3, 1909, at
St. Kikla (Vic.), this player kicked a football 84yds.
from boot to bounce—a world’s record.
Saturday’s Melbourne lioofball finals
were played in ideal weather, though an occasional
breath from the coming summer warned the ’bailers
that it was time they put their playthings by. All
the disturbances were of the hard, close-fought order,
and none of the competing teams could take a
moment’s respite till the last bell rang. On the
Metropolitan ground 47,356 persons paid £1790 2s.
6d. to watch the Carlton-Essendon collision. A 2000
larger crowd watched the same clubs battle out the
finish in ’OB, but Saturday’s push was apparently
“flusher,” as it parted 225. 6d. more than the pre-
vious record. Play was brilliant in spots, but the
teams were hampered by having top-notchers who
were either stunned by the importance of the oc-
casion, or were too tired to bustle themselves, or
something. In brief, the ’Dons were smarter and
quicker-witted. Going off at a great pace, they got
a small lead, and never lost it. The Old Blues had
what their barrackers called the “rotten luck” to hit
the goal post four times, but it was rather selfish and
unsystematic play than luck that lost them so many
points. In the second quarter the “Same Old” made
determined efforts to get away from their opponents,
and reached the interval nine points to the good—
which wasn’t much. The third and last terms saw
the Blues doing their darndest —the bigger battlers
plunging round and tearing through regardless—but
ever as they put on a goal or a behind the ’Dons
would square the account with a similar score. And
always they kept ahead. The Blues got within four
points, but before they could storm the longed-for
goal the ’Dons had added one, and the position was
as bad as ever. They put up a tremendous rush and
gained a goal, but even as they were tearing down
the ground for another the bell* rang, and the ’Dons
had won by four points—s 2 to Carlton’s 48. The
winners meet South this Saturday in the “grand
final,” but as one of their forwards was limping
badly through most of Saturday’s game, and their
champion rover broke his leg just at the moment of
victory, their chance against the “Southern Angels”
doesn’t look too rosy.
The Ass. final, at North Melbourne, viewed by
23,000 people, worth £342, afforded a suggestive co-
incidence. Last year the two Essendons beat the two
Magpies in the final matches; this year the two
’Dons, both in the third place, have successively
beaten the leaders and runners-up, and now have to
meet the leaders again in a “grand final.” And on
Saturday both ’Dons won by four points only. The
North-Essendon encounter was a case of wits against
main force —and wits won. The ’Dons boast the
State’s champion goal-kicker—his record for the
season is 105, miles ahead of any previous tally—
and North told off two men to block him. They suc-
ceeded to some extent, though he got six goals ; but
while he was galloping round and often pretending
to go for a mark, his two small fellow-forwards were
playing shuttlecock with the ball, and tossing it to
and fro till ihey got into position. The result showed
in the scores-—Essendon. 16 goals six behinds ; North,
14 goals 14 behinds; 102 points to 98. The breeze
alternately favored either side, and when the ’Dons
started the last term 17 points in arrear, it was felt
that it was a case of goals or nothing. North during
the term added another 19 points, but the others, by
brainy play, making the most of their chances, got
six goals four behinds—and the odd behinds won.
As the last goal only came in the last minute, the
finish was exciting enough, and the roars of the bar-
rackers could be heard nearly out to Essendon itself.
The ’Dons have now to meet the “minor premiers,”
’■Scray, and that ends the riot.
McINTOSH GETS A MOVE ON.
Previous to last week's elections by tlie
clubs composing the V.C.A. of their respective dele-
gates, barrackers for the malcontents, anticipating the
defeat of the former delegates and their Board of Con-
trol nominees, were eagerly advising everybody to
decide the issue once for all, and then bury the
hatchet—“in the deep blue sea,” as Nawman Bayles
put it. The elections are over, and the hatchet has
been buried all right—in the necks of the malcon-
tents. The former V.C.A. delegates to the Board of
Control were re-elected with 24 and 17 votes, their
opponents polling 10 and eight. For the selection
committee, Peter McAlister, E. E. Bean and Mick
Ellis got 67 votes altogether, while their solitary
opponent, Harry Trott, carried only 11. The East
Melbourne C.C.’s annual meeting, a few days before,
gave a forecast of what was going to happen.
The Melbourne Cricket Club is in reality a body
of persons using certain public lands and the build-
ings and appurtenances thereon for its own amuse-
ment and delectation. Its revenue last year from
subscriptions and entrance fees was £10,367 14s.
The trustees of the Metropolitan Cricket Ground,
whereat and whereon the M.C.C. members get the
worth of their subscriptions, simultaneously acquired
a net revenue of £2231 Os. Bd., to which football
contributed £lO2l 10s. ; cricket (with an English
team playing international and test matches), £lOl7
11s. 9d. ; concerts, £lB2 os. 3d. ; and miscellaneous
sports, £9 18s. Bd. The cricket takings only reach
that height triennially or thereabouts; the concert
revenue depends on the weather and public taste, but
the football gate is a regular and abiding circum-
stance. No wonder the ground’s trustees don’t set so
much store by the M.C.C. as that body does by itself.
The Soccer struggles for the Rawson and
Association Cups are soli raging in Sydney, and if
the present rise in the temperature continues, the
’bailers will find the final allocation of those utensils
a very perspiry job. Saturday was a mighty warm
day to play football; but all the same, the Soccer
crowd did two desperate matches at the Sports
Ground. In the matter of the Association Cup, New-
town, which is second last on the list, with only five
competition points, after much wrestling in prayer,
decided it was not worth while to go up against West
Sydney with its 23 points, and sent along a forfeit.
The result of this was that Balmain and West Syd-
ney are now equal on points, and must eventually
meet in battle array to decide which of them shail
have the use of the splendiferous mug for the coming
year. And great will be the battle. In the Rawson
riot Balmain fell on Sydney, which was leading by a
couple of points; got it down, and bit it by two
goals to one, and thus equalised things. Wherefore
and whereby they, too, must meet again to decide
which shall bite the dust. The Navy attacked New-
town, and overthrew it by four goals to three —but
got no nearer the goal practically. The Navy, even
with its honors, bruises and contusions thick upon
it, still shares with Canterbury the dubious distinc-
tion of driving the rest of the competing clubs before
it, and Newtown is last in the procession of the
driven. But, all the same, the two clubs struggled
as desperately as though the fate of the Cup depended
on their exertions. The meeting between Balmain
and Sydney was tremendous. Balmain discovered
that Sydney had been unable to bring its best team
into the field, and hastily decided to force things
and see what rushing would do. The Sydney defence
was rock-like, and when it wasn’t, it was lucky.
Sydney’s captain lifted his men after the repulse of
each attack to another splendid charge. It failed,
but it was a great effort. Back each time came Bal-
main, and at last, with a three-man pass, tore into
the goal. Balmain, one; Sydney, nil. That goal
was the taste of blood to tigers; and Balmain was
out for meat. Also it was loaded for beer. Sydney
struggled desperately, hut again the men from the
Ragged Suburb broke through, and the score was
two to nil. Close upon half-time Sydney burst
through, and wiped one off the margin. After that
they swayed about helplessly, struggling desperately,
almost too exhausted in the heat to play systemati-
cally ; and there was no further score. Balmain won
by two goals to one.
* * * *
In the Australian Rules section, Y.M.C.A.
met Sydney, but for once the Loard hid His face
from His children, and the heathen triumphed might-
ily—by 78 points to 31. Exactly why the Good
Young Men should have gone to pieces so utterly is
hard to explain. The previous Saturday had seen
them utterly smash Paddington, the “minor premier
club,’’ and the general expectation was that it would
be a hard and bitter day for Sydney. It wasn’t.
Sydney’s splendid team worked like a machine. Its
kicking was superb, its charges irresistible; its com-
bination perfect. And the result was the utter over-
throw of the Anointed.
* * * *
The Scots’ College Sports produced a
youthful phenomenon in the person of little Eric
Senior, one of twin sons of the lady who is known
in journalistic and musical circles as Miss May Sum-
merbelle. Young Eric is only eight years old, but he
won the mile handicap from the 380yds. mark in
smin. 19see., with 25yds. to spare, from boys many
of whom were double his age. His sprinting was
beautiful to watch; but about the wisdom of the
school authorities in allowing an eight-year-old boy to
compete in a mile race, this writer has very de-
cided opinions—which are highly uncomplimentary
to the Scots’ College athletic authorities. Still, the
boy is certainly an abnormality, for in addition to
this deed of derring-do, this almost baby also won
the high jump handicap, and put up some startling
performances in a number of sprinting events. There’s
not much of Eric, but what there is seems to be of
fine quality.
* * * *
Midway in the long billiards game between
Lindrum and Harverson, at Alcoek’s Parlor, in Mel-
bourne, another new cloth was substituted for the
somewhat nappier one which had seemed to handicap
the Australian champion. On the new cloth he at
once began to make up his points, without the
assistance of the Gray stroke. Both men have been
playing well of late, and the game will be an inter-
esting exhibition right up to its last session on
Saturday night. Next week Alcock’s are to intro-
duce the novelty of a Bijou-table tournament, con-
fined to bona-fide amateur experts in “pony” billiards.
Probably Lindrum and Harverson will open the pro-
ceedings with an exhibition of the Bijou-table’s pos-
sibilities for the making of big breaks at small ex-
penditure of elbow grease.
Backers of Flavian for the Cups, on the
strength of his two “seconds” last year, begin to
think that his mighty fine appearance is not quite
satisfactory. What he has gained in weight the
horse may have lost in speed. On Saturday, at
Caulfield, the handsome six-year-old started at odds-
on for a 11-mile race, but was beaten by a 1 ght-
weighted commoner. Parisian and Bobadea ran un-
backed in the same race, the latter making the better
show. If the Victorian nop-notchers are to be fit to
hold their own against the best of the N.S.W. cham-
pions in four or five weeks time, they will have to
put in some hard track work in the meantime.
The richness of the A.J.C. Derby stake is
a most commendable thing from both a sporting and
punter point of view. It ensures a reasonable chance
for criticism of “crack” three-year-old “form” in
the near future events, and disposes of a lot of con-
fusing doubts that arise from the bad running of
good horses during the few weeks prior to the Syd-
ney event. Within the past 12 years seven winners
of the A.J.C. Derby have gone on and captured the
V.R.C. ditto, two of them —-Poseidon and Prince
Foote—have taken the Melbourne Cup also, and
three others—Malster, Abundance and Mountain King
—have run second or third for the Cup. Two of
the A.J.C. Derby winners in those 12 years have run
second for the V.R.C. Derby, and six of the minor
placed ones at Randwick, have been placed again—
first, second or third —in the three-year-old test race
at Flemington. Evidently the A.J.C. Derby is an
honest guide to three-year-old form, and as the rich
stake grows richer, its guiding power increases rather
than otherwise. The form may be very moderate in
some years, but its comparative value holds good.
Last year Cisco missed his second Derby by a head
only, after going amiss in his training. Next Satur-
day week should show whether Wolawa, Cider and
other lately disappointing crack three-year-olds have
lost ground since last season, or have been getting
ready for what is apologetically termed a surprise.
About the recent doings of the more elderly gee-gee
aristocrats there seems to be no doubt whatever.
With only one or two exceptions they were under-
stood to be making an unholy show of their need
for a few more gallops or a wider betting market,
or both. Nobody nowadays expects to see the best
horses win in August or the early part of September.
Curiosity centres in their manner of getting beaten
and the measures adopted to impart an air of re-
spectability to their defeat.
The combined batting averages of Bards-
ley and Macartney for the Australian Eleven, in Eng-
land, amounted to one run more than the combined
averages of Kelleway, Gregory, Jennings and Mayne,
the next four men on the list, who ranged from
28.57 to 21.39. Ilazlitt, with an average of 7.93 for
37 innings, was the tuft of the long batting tail, but
he shared with Whitty the honor of taking more than
100 wickets, at the reasonable cost of 18 to 19 runs
apiece. Both in batting and bowling Whitty came
out a trifle ahead of Hazlitt. Macartney, second in
batting and first in bowling averages, was unap-
proached as an all-round performer. Then came
Matthews, Kelleway, Minnett and Emery. McLaren,
who took 27 wickets, was less of a failure than non-
bowlers Webster and Smith. Carkeek’s batting aver-
age of 9.17 made him a costly wicketkeeper as com-
pared with the keepers of yore.
The four Australian cricketers who took
the £4OO in lieu of a share of the profits got the
best of the deal after all, as it turns out the other
11 will receive about £2OO each —which is none too
bad,_ considering the way the weather went into
liquidation. By the time this is in print Gregory,
Kelleway, Whitty, Matthews, Mayne, Emery, McLaren,
Smith, Webster and Carkeek will be taking their
first glimpse of the land of America, where, with a
“pick-up,” most likely a South African, they will play
the brethren of Philadelphia and divers other churches
till the end of October. Macartney, Bardslev, Jen-
nings, Hazlitt, Minnett and manager Crouch will
return home by the Marathon, arriving Sydney, via
the Cape, on November 25.
There is always balm in Gilead; and
South Africa, though outclassed and utterly walloped,
not to say routed, managed to collar two medals on
the tour. Nourse’s 213 against Hampshire is the
highest individual score for either visiting team;
but it was hard pressed by Macartney’s 208 against
Essex. Also, Pegier captured more wickets than the
most deadly Australian.
“ * *
The curse of “King Willow”: Unreason-
ing- newspaper reckoners, who regard international
cricket as a club-swinging endurance exhibition, are
whooping that Bardsley has carried off the batting
honors of the tour, with an aggregate of 2416 runs,
and they give Macartney a gentle pat for 2207. But
the hotch-potch “all in” total credited to Bardslev
includes no fewer than 855 runs made it matches
that do not count, or shouldn’t count, as they don’t
matter, being outside County, Test or M.C.C. fixtures.
In the latter Bardsley made 1561. Macartney only
troubled to make 459 in the unimportant knocks;
but in the merit matches he aggregates 187 more
than Bardsley, and when runs were badly wanted in
a tight corner, it was Macartney who smacked them
up. In June Australia first struck trouble, being-
beaten in two successive matches, but in these
Macartney totalled 111 and Bardsley 10 against Lan-
cashire; Macartney 66, Bardsley 26 against Surrey
in the return. In the first Surrey Macartney made
148 runs, Bardsley 46. In the two drawn games
with the then coming champion county, Yorkshire,
Macartney 91, Bardsley 37.. The left-hander is a
good bat of the stolid sort, but on neither of his
English tours has he been the best man in big things,
whatever newspaper averages may allege.
Yorkshire, with 72 per cent, of points has
won the First Class County Championship of England
from 15 opponents. The system of points governing
this competition is one that might well be applied
or adapted to the performance of teams touring
the Land of Darkness and Deluge. For an outright
win five points are awarded. Where each side com-
pletes one innings only three points go to the leader,
and one point to the other team, in consideration of
the fact that it might have won had the match been
completed. For any play less than one innings each
side, no . points. By that system the wearisome
“draw” is eliminated from many unfinished games,
and the play is brightened by the effort to reach at
least the “minor” points stage before the rain and
murk resume. It took Fogland until last year to
discover this eminently sensible reform. Just note
the application thereof to the Australian Test,
County and M.C.C. matches (the only matches that
are of any real significance) : Matches commenced,
27. Unfinished at less than one innings each, and
therefore wiped off the slate, three. Balance, 24.
Completed, 13. One innings each completed, 11.
Under the present misleading system Australia’s re-
cord in that series of principal matches is sum-
marised as:—■
Played, 27. Won, 7. Lost, 6. Drawn, 14.
But these 14 “draws” include no fewer than six
county matches in which Australia led on the first
innings, and one of them was the return with the
champion County, Yorkshire. The points system
would present Gregory and Co. thus: Played, 24.
Won, seven. Won first innings, six. Lost, six. Lost,
single innings, five, or 58 points out of a possible
98, equal to 59.18 per cent., as against 53.84 per
cent, for seven outright wins in 13 completed
matches.
* * * *
A gaudy rumor started by a Melbourne
paper, has just about blown itself out. The fairy
tale was that the Mitchell Bros., owners of Trafalgar,
were importing Frank Wootton from England, at a
fabulous fee, to ride the champion in the Melbourne
Cup. Many noble sports, who are trusting to Car-
bine’s grandson to do the trick at his fourth attempt,
did not fancy Frank Wootton on such a horse in
such a race; and they will be relieved to hear that,
some months ago, Sydney jockey J. E. Pike was
engaged to ride Trafalgar in his spring engage-
ments, and. that, at time of writing, there has been
■no alteration of that agreement. The aforemen-
tioned marvel of horseflesh is at present in as good
fettle as ever in his life, and is due in Sydney
this week, with a nose for three A.J.C. weight-for-
age events. Although he has started 56 times for 23
wins,. 10 seconds, and s'x thirds, and won £20.003.
and is now a seven-year-old, he has never been turned
out for a spell. Which is more than can be said for
Carbine, who started 43 times for 33 wins, six
seconds, three thirds and £29,476. Trafalgar looks
safe to beat his grand-dad’s money, this season, as
well as his number of wins before retiring. Already
Trafalgar heads the list of Australian-bred stake-
winners, for Carbine was a Maorilander.
The great tug-of-war carnival will* burst
into being on Saturday night at Mclntosh’s Stadium,
Sydney. The opening scene will be a procession of
the visiting champions and the pick of the local
men, such as the mighty Heckenbergs. Later on. the
“Parramatta Pygmies”, (a team consisting wholly of
members of the lleckenberg family from Parramatta)
will, settle the question of which shall represent Aus-
tralia by argument with Fitzpatrick’s crowd, which
is described as “a little lot.” Of course, it is all a
matter of comparison. The International struggle
will be opened by Scotland “talcin’ aa pu’ at Ire-
land.” The volcanic tuggers from Maoriland and the
Melbourne cask-jugglers will be down against Sydney
unionists and storemen —an airy team whose average
weight is 18 stone. Blue-jackets ‘from the Cambrian
and the Drake will decide which is the better war-
ship. So altogether the veriest glutton should not go
away altogether unsatisfied.

“Some months ago,” writes Mr. J. Bur-

ment on a previous occasion, I had the bal-

with warm water. It gave me almost in-
stant relief, and in a few days all traces of
the accident had disappeared. I really think
the Liniment saved me several days of in-
capacitation.”
A sprain is an injury to the ligaments of
a joint, usually the wrist or the ankle, by
accident or over-straining. Sprains are
usually attended with much pain and swell-
ing of the part, and sometimes produce faint-
ing and _ vomiting. Comstock’s Nerve and
Bone Liniment is an ideal remedy for
sprains, and should be applied frequently
and well rubbed in until all traces have dis-
appeared. As sprains are one of the most
frequent accidents, every household should
contain a bottle of this valuable Liniment
for this purpose, if for nothing else. Sold
by. all leading chemists and storekeepers,
price 2s. 6d., or will be forwarded by post at
same price by the W. H. Comstock Co.,
Ltd., 23 Lang Street, Sydney.
