“F.C.” writes :
Whatever may happen in other States at next
general election, Labor in Victoria will probably have
some difficulty in answering insistent queries as to
why it has so long delayed carrying out the protective
policy on which it was elected in April, TO. When
Victoria’s Government delegated Mr. Whitehead to
the Cold Land, to rope in artisan immigrants, many
manufacturers applied for mechanics of sorts. The
Trades Hall Council objected that they weren’t really
wanted, and a compromise number was agreed on.
Whitehead got his men, many of whom left fair
billets to come out, and, now that they are coming
in shoals, the manufacturers can’t find jobs for them.
Of course, this is quite wicked on the part of the
manufacturers, but their explanation is that imports
are increasing, as they never increased before, and
therefore manufactures, instead of growing, as was
naturally anticipated, are about stationary,' and they
can’t employ men when they have no work for them.
There is much in the plea. The only remedy the
electors now have is to fire out at next June election
every candidate, Laborite or otherwise, who will not
promise to assist in passing a genuinely protective
tariff within the next 12 mouths.
Wales is the stronghold of Baptists. Every
Welsh village has a Baptist chapel. In one
of them, at Pontypool, a preacher was say-
ing disrespectful things about King David
a few Sundays ago, when a member of the
congregation rose and objected on the
ground that David was not there to defend
himself. “What’s the good of all this mud-
slinging?” inquired the interjector. “For
months you have been preaching these de-
rogatory sermons. First, you hit out at
Moses; then you spoke of Esther, as if she
were a bod woman; and last Sunday you
dragged Job through the mire. You seem
to delight in this sort of thing, but we
don’t.” As other members of the congrega-
tion seemed disposed to say ditto to the in-
terrupter, the preacher brought the sermon
to "an abrupt olncn ”

bacteria.
It is the rinsing of the mouth and
the brushing of the teeth with this anti-
septic and delightfully refreshing mixture
that constitutes the process now known
as Odolising.
Odol penetrates the interstices in and
between the teeth and permeates the
gums and mucous membrane of the
mouth, exerting its marvellous powers
not only during the few moments while
using it, but for hours afterwards.

“Corkonian”: Hugo’s big minstrel company opens
at Wirths’ Melbourne Hippodrome. The first negroid
minstrels in Australia were Rainer’s Serenaders.
Rainer long afterwards had the enormously lucrative
panorama of the United States Civil War. The
better panorama of the Franco-German War, with
Kyrle Bellew as lecturer, never caught on like
Rainer’s. The next after his Serenaders were tiie
Backus Minstrels, from San Franc.sco, showing at
Coppin’s Melbourne Olympic. Coppin brought, out
the Nubian Minstrels, the worst frost ever he bandied,
and the worst ever known, except Simonsen’s Spanish
Students. The so-called Christy Minstrels opened at
Melbourne Royal in 1860, and Burton’s Circus showed
there the same year. The Christys included Tom H.
Rainford, a baritone, not to be confounded with J. H.
Rainford, the well-known Sydney actor, manager and
publican. Other Christys were tenors Stewart and
Melvyn, pianist Steele, and comedians Joe Brown
(of course not our little, fat theatre manager) and
Wash Norton. Two years later another good com-
pany, at the Melb. Haymarket, included tenors Abecco
and Herberte, of Belle Brandon fame, also Nick La
Feuillade, the violinist. The Weston and Hussey
Minstrels included Harry Kelly and Charley Holly,
afterwards partner with Tom Buckley. Maxey was a
well-known corner man, who visited Australia. Emer-
son’s first batch included Sutton, Scott and Rocke-
feller. Billy voted Australia rotten. Yet Emerson
came twice more. The second time he had the
Cogills, Walsh and King, Stanley and Pixley, and
Dan Treacy, clog-leaper, afterwards manager of Melb.
Gaiety. In Emerson’s third visit he was with Wood,
described as the Somewhat Different Comedian. And
he certainly was different, though what he was differ-
ent from is another matter. Tommy Campbell was a
well-known baritone. After dropping out of mind,
he turned up again from San Fran., with a renowned
nineteen-guinea fur coat. Good Australian performers
have been Johnny Cowan, Buckley, Kohlman, the
brothers Dave, George and Johnny Gardner, and Cal
Gay. Hiscocks and Batchelder’s Minstrels held on
long. Horace Bent, a Geelong man, did well for
many years, not only in Australia but in America.
Charley Hugo had a good spell of performing and
managing. I think the first woman that blacked up
in Australia was Nellie Hosmer. Then there was
little Fanny Wiseman, Monty South’s wife, so capi-
tal as Topsy, in the eightv-nigbts run at Melbourne
Princess’s, with her song of “Chingy Ring a Rickey.”
Her daughter has also taken up the Topsy mantle,
which consists of a bit of old gunny-bag. So much
for my recollections of M ; stnh Johnson, Bones and
Tambo. And I’ve forgotten the two Georgia Troupes
"ud M'Adoo’s.
“Lantana” : Recently bought a ticket for a charity
dance in connection with a well-known city maternity
home. Printed conspicuously across the top was a
legend conveying the compliments of a reputable firm
of undertakers. The association of ideas was cheer-
ful.
C. M. S. McLennan, the man who, under the name
of Hugh Morton, wrote “The Belle of New York,”
which netted £40,000 for George Musgrove in Lon-
don, has perpetrated a new musical comedy, to be
called “Her Left Shoulder,” and has been talking to
the London interviewers about it. The story deals
with artists’ models, and the hunt for a correct left
shoulder for a picture of Venus. This obviously
involves inspection of a crowd of pretty girls at
close quarters. McLennan says it is much harder
work to write these light, frothy comedies than to
perpetrate serious plays. He has done both, so he
ought to know. Some people say that he has done
both badly.
Franz Lehar, the composer of “The Merry Widow,”
is reported to have made £lOO,OOO out of the cheery
female with the famous waltz. He has also done
well out of his other musical comedies, and is pro-
claimed by a London authority as the “wealthiest
living composer.” But he had his struggles. When
a student at the Prague Conservatorium he was a
“boarding pupil,” which is not an agreeable form of
existence. His parents were poor and unable to
rtjnit him sufficient funds. “On one occasion,” he
told a recent interviewer, “I collapsed in the street
from sheer hunger.” He showed Dvorak.a couple of
his juvenile compositions, and that master said:
“Hang your violin up on the wall and devote your-
self entirely to composition.” He took the advice,
and gave up being hungry and collapsing in the
street.
The London County Council exercises jurisdiction
over all the variety houses, theatres and concert-halls
in the latter-day Babylon, with one conspicuous ex-
ception—the biggest place of the lot, the Royal
Albert Hall. This is the colossal rotunda which,
with the absurd “memorial” statue in front of it, is
supposed to perpetuate the memory of the husband
of Queen Vic. and the grandfather of King George.
Hitherto, the influence of royalty has prevented the
County Council or anybody else interfering with the
control or regulation of this place, although it is
frequently used for concerts and big public meetings
of all sorts. At the last meeting of the Council it
was decided, as a matter of public safety, to appeal
to the High Court for authority to bring the danger-
ous building up to date and in line with other struc-
tures of the sort. According to the Chairman of the
Theatres and Music Halls Comniittee, the Albert Hall
holds 10,000 people, whereas the exits provide for
only 4000. Also, most of these exits are blocked up,
and would be death-traps in an emergency. Further-
more, there are no attendants qualified to direct
people through the maze of passages and corridors.
And finally, the doors leading from the vast promen-
ade gallery are always locked. He prophesied a holo-
caust there some day or night.
“Monkbarns”: The Melbourne Theatre Royal being
newly done up, may justify a glance over its history.
The first Royal opened about the middle of 1855. It
was built by Black, who made a fortune as carrier
to Ballarat and Bendigo, and here dropped the swag
and the loot and the profits. The only previous Melb.
theatre had been the Queen’s, opened in 1845. Three
theatres arose in 1855 —the Royal, Olympic and Ast-
ley’s—on which last G. B. W. Lewis' lost £30,000.
Coppin and Brooke obtained the Royal in 1856.
Brooke was sole lessee in 1858. Brooke and Coppin
again in 1860. The next lessee was Wilton, under
whose auspices Barry Sullivan appeared in 1862.
Sullivan was lessee in 1863. Hoskins and Clarence
Holt, Bland’s father, arrived in 1865. Co-partnery
of Lambert, Harwood, C. Vincent, Bellair, Hennings
and Stewart in 1867. Various changes followed, in
most of which Coppin and Greville figured. Theatre
burnt in February, 1872. New one, built by Coppin,
opened in November of that year. He subsided into
one of the proprietary, while Williamson, Garner and
Musgrove raised the house to new glory. When thev
opened the new Princess’s, the Royal fell back into
the barn stage. Bland Holt lifted it again, and with
the new improvements it has recovered its old pres-
tige. Meanwhile, the Princess's sags somewhat into
the barn category.
“Vulcan”: Even Tokio’s brown heathen suffers
from tlie fatuous theatrical censor. Recently, after
witnessing* a performance, the Home and ii<ducation
Departments suppressed Sudermann’s “Magda,’ on
the ground that it “contained too many sentiments
contrary to the ideal of filial piety, which is so much
prized in this country.” A wild press howl resuited
and raged unabated until the authorities permittee
“Magda ”to resume, but in a mutilated form —“in
order to avoid that which is contrary to the old
public morality of Japan.” And the laugh of the
Yoshiwarra rang out right across the Monkey Sea.
In Paris there seems to be a lot of money flying
about in the moving picture business. Owing to a
squabble among the shareholders of a leading cine-
matograph company, there have been revelations in
court. The company paid Le Bargv £760 a year as
“artistic adviser,” and the same annual sum to !
I.avedan as “literary adviser.” Also, there were
three stage managers, each in receipt of £BOO a
year. The sums paid to the actors and actresses
seem small in comparison with the salaries of the
advisers and stage managers. That grand old girl.
Sarah Bernhardt, heads the list with £6O a perform-
ance, while Rejane gets only £4O. And Bernhardt, in
addition gets a id. for every yard of film. Some of
these films would stretch from Sydney to the Middle
Ages, so that humble coin really represents a very
substantial addition to the £6O fee.
“Stargazer” : Re effort to encourage the making of
plays in Australia, it strikes me that the music-hall
offers a better field for beginners than is discoverable
in any other direction. Vaudeville patrons like little
plays, and their taste is not bad. But the supply of
even moderately good small comedies or 16-minute
farces for the halls would appear to be woefully
limited, with the result that the opening promises
to be choked with sheer litter. Many of the dramatic
spasms presented lately have had no quality of work-
manship to recommend them. Actors of some ability
come from the legitimate stage, with playlets to try-
on the dingo that are utterly hopeless to begin with
-—bald, brainless, humorless stupidities, without even
the coarse, knoek-about mirth that may save a tum
form the ultimate depths of dulness. Give music-
hall audiences a short, sharp play, that has a rattling
idea, one striking situation, some decisive character-
drawing, a good caricature and a fair seasoning of
humor, and they love it.
A DANGER TO ENGLAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. George Edwardes has been compelled to insert
a clause in his agreements with actresses prohibiting
them from marrying while under engagement to him.
He has taken this course in order to prevent the in-
convenience caused by actresses quitting the stage
whilst a play is running. —Daily ray.
George Edwardes, must this really be,
And can you then afford
To flout the aristocracce,
You who so love a pedigree,
And venerate a lord?
Reflect, sir, what it means like this
To bar the ballet girls
From rushing to connubial bliss.
’Twill knock the census all amiss,
And block our dukes and earls.
Now how are men in highest spheres
To be with equals mated,
If you withhold the dancing dears?
Why, hang it, man, the House of Peers
May be depopulated.
VJCTOKIA.

Concerning an early Victorian: The San
Francisco Newsletter, of August 9, 1869,
had the following respecting George Francis
Train, who was a Melbourne merchant,
1853-’55:—
George Francis Train, that incomprehensible com-
pounded agglomeration of impudence, brass, conceit,
egotism, vanity, insolence, ignorance, shrewdness,
cunning, cupidity, vulgarity, buffoonery, obscenity,
infidelity—the intellectual eunuch, the oratorical har-
lequin, the verbal limber-jack, the financial burnt-
cork clown, the political dancing-monkey—that
social eruption that keeps the epidermis of the civil-
ised portion of the human race in a constant state of
irritation—that perambulating moral cancer that is
eating out the life and patience of two hemispheres
—-that universal pedlar of stale fish, whose mental tin
horn sternly vexes the ear of the world—the insuffer-
able bore and ineffable blatherskite—is still holding
forth in San Francisco before gaping multitudes,
who accept his senseless balderdash for wisdom be-
cause thev do not understand it, take his insolence
for bluntness, his buffoonery for wit, his ranting for
eloquence, his sickening, self-laudation for independ-
ence. Stockton Gazette (our contemporary) is fair
—very fair—but mistaken. The audiences of Mr.
Train accept that gentleman’s eloquence for nothing
of the kind. They go to look at him for the same
reason that they go to North Beach to see the nasty
bears and monkeys. George Francis is amusing, and
if our people keep a tight grip on their noses, per-
fectly inoffensive.
The Newsletter also says :—
George Train stated that the English were a nation
of drunkards and prostitutes. Mrs. Jones, of Pros-
pect-place, Clay-street, resented this insult to her
country-women by collaring the blackguard in public
and spitting in his face. Mrs. Jones, of Prospect-
place, Clay-street, is entered on our books a£. a per-
petual free subscriber to the Newsletter.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. Name
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.
B. North: “She rang like hell” (or per-
haps it is “rang the bell”), “and the white-capped
nurse entered softly, bringing tights and a skunk.”
Or perhaps you are trying to say that she brought
lights and a drink. Anyhow, it doesn’t matter. No-
thing matters to a person who writes a hand like
yours....A.G.M.: Your suggestion that The Bulle-
tin should take up the cause of the British suffra-
gette to hand. But Britain is a good way off, and
this paper isn’t sufficiently swelled about the head to
imagine that anything it could say at such long
range would have very much influence. It seems
like attacking your enemy with an axe at a range
of 100yds. However good the weapon may be within
its own radius it has its limitations.... G. M. Lob-
ban : There is no market in all this vast Common-
wealth for such verse as “The Cruise of the Rangi-
tira.”... . Jiggi: Beautifully written set of gags, but
otherwise deplorable Orara: Curse the beach
worm. It is not often we speak thus
harshly of one of God’s dumb creatures, but
there has been such a flood of copy re worms, of late,
and the subject of worms and their ways is so soon
exhausted, that the thing is getting on our nerves.
We have arrived at such a pass that we don’t want
to see or touch or read or hear about a worm for
months. .. .The Caretaker: Your reputation as a

The Message of Sanatogen
No preparation of its kit'd has ever achieved such
a reputation as Sanatogen.
Many causes have contributed to this unpre-
cedented result.
Pre-eminent among them is the constant prescrip-
tion of Sanatogen bv the medical profession which,
as a body, has awarded it the highest praise. More
than fifteen thousand doctors have written in the
most enthusiastic terms to describe the benefits they
have obtained from the use of the preparation
which The Medical Press and Circular states “has
strengthened the physician’s hsnd a hundred-fold.”
Everyone knows now that it gives
To the Sick —Health
It does this by its power of revitalising the
nervous, vascular, muscular, and digestive systems,
wtiich it rapidly restores from a condition of depression
to one of normal action. By virtue of its composition
—pure milk casein in chemical combination with
organic phosphorus, an essential constituent of the
brain and nervous system—it feeds the brain and
ne ves, it improves the condition of the blood, it helps
the stomach todigestother food, and it thereby develops
and strengthens the muscles. In this way it gives
To the Weak —Strength
and that delightful feeling of returning vitality which
tells the sufferer that he has conquered the disease
which had conquered him, and is once more on the
road to health, and the possibility of taking up his
life again where he had laid it down.
Sanatogen, however, does even more than this.
It has its message for those whom disease has not
touched. Everyone is capable of increased vitality and
vigour. By feeding the vital centres Sanatogen gives
To the Healthy —Vigour
so that work becomes a pleasure, toil becomes enjoy-
ment, and the whole systbm takes on a feeling of
buoyancy, alertness and energy which is as delightful
as it is unusual.
Sanatogen’s merits have not only been eulogistd
by the medical press, and by the medical profession,
but have also been endorsed by hundreds of the bes -
known men and women who have testified to the
cures it has wrought in their cases.
Sanatogen may be obtained from all chemists.

The N.S.W. Truck Deficit.
The commission which has been inquiring
into the shortage of trucks, etc., in N.S.W.
has unearthed some interesting facts. The
bitterness all through the State, and especi-
ally at harvest and shearing times, is that
there are not nearly enough vehicles to go
round, or that they don’t go round fast
enough. ; Farmers are often months behind
contract-delivery time in consequence, and
lose money all the while. The average num-
ber' of trucks supplied varies from 10 to 30
per cent, of those ordered. In one case men-
tioned a wheat buyer ordered six trucks a
day, and got 10 in three weeks. Meanwhile,
the wheat has to be stacked, and the farmer
has to pay for space and also for stacking.
At Barmednian, right in the centre of the
wheat-belt, a man who was just craving to
load four trucks a day, obtained 55 trucks
in 108 working days and none on Sundays.
Even when the trucks are secured the farmer
never knows how long they are going to take
to get to Sydney. Some recent performances
—-averages for the first four months of this
year—are as follows: Temora to Sydney,
•U days’; from Wyalong, 31 days; Corowa,
4i days’; Manilla, 31 days. That is not
counting Sundays. If Sundays are counted
in, the average goes up. Thus a train from
Corowa, 409 miles from Sydney, taking 41
days, maintains an average speed of 3| miles
per hour, and from Bathurst the average is
41 miles per hour. And this waste of time
and money will continue as long as every-
thing is dragged to Sydney. The goods yards
in that city are only capable of holding 116
trucks. When they get full a bloated arma-
ment of trucks is formed at Clyde, where
they have to wait their turn. And when
Clyde gets full, as it sometimes does, traffic
from all stations is stopped, and wheat lies
and rots, and trucks loaf idly qn sidings. At
times there are as many as 200 trucks wait-
ing at Clyde. When several other harbors
are opened up and lines built to them, this
congestion will cease, till then it will get
worse and worse as more land is settled.
The truck shortage isn’t really caused by the
truck insufficiency. It arises through the
last and boundless length of time the truck
takes to get to Sydney at the rate of 44
miles per hour, unload, and return at the
rate of 4J miles per hour for a new cargo.
And that is caused by the congestion at and
around Sydney, and that is caused by the
struggle to drive the trade of 310,000 square
miles to one port. Probably if the number
of trucks were doubled, the vehicles would
congest so much more that they would take
twice as long to get back for their next
load, and then the old trouble would be as
bad as ever.
“Wonga” makes a few remarks: —
For years now a wrangle has been in progress in
those places where sheep, goats, etc., do most abound
anent the fate of the Pasture Protection Boards. The
latter, which took the place of the old Stock Boards,
have, been, for a long while, regarded as useless ex-
crescences by a big proportion of the cookies, who
think that their work, such as it is, could be done
for less money by the shire councils. On the other
hand, the big landowners want them not only to be
retained, but to be given greater powers. In their
eyes the Boards have virtue, inasmuch as they repre-
sent about the last remnant of the good old days
when “property” had the commanding vote. Amid
the wordy warfare that has been raging among the
bucolics, no one seems to have paid any attention
to the interests of that good old entity, the General
Public. Yet the General Public, and more especially
the section which lives up-country and eats local
meat, had a peculiar interest in the matter. The
P.P. Boards boss the stock inspector. That is to
say, they pay his wages. But he is also under the
Government, and cannot be dismissed without the
consent of the Minister. Experience has proved,
however, that where an inspector is obnoxious to the
Board, he is invariably “outed.” The Board repre-
sents the stock-owners and is composed of stock-
owners. The inspector is the chap who has to keep
an eye on diseased stock. And stock-owners are not
pleased at having their stock diagnosed as “diseased.”
The inspector, if he would keep on good terms with
his employers, must move warily—too warily, in fact,
for public safety. Any man, not of independent
mean?, whose job consists of censoring his bosses’
products, is Unhappily situated. Many rough-and-
ready stock-owners consider that a piece cut from
a “lumpy jaw” bullock is quite harmless. How is
the conscientious inspector to retain the confidence
of that reckless type, and at the same time guard the
public health by condemning a diseased beast, or
promptly reporting anthrax, and causing the infected
district to suffer the loss and inconvenience of quar-
antine? The stock inspectors should be given a
chance to do their duty fearlessly—that is to say,
they should be made responsible to no one but the
Chief Inspector of Stock. Also, the Minister might
see that they are men who have a thorough knowledge
of their vitally important duties, and pay them
sufficient to obviate any necessity of eking out their
allowance by sojourning for long periods at the
homesteads of stock-owners.

has quite done talking Archbosh about
“mixed” marriages, and trying to keep one
Australian out of every five or six from
having anything to do with the other four
or five, he might answer these few questions
and make us all feel more comfortable —or
more uncomfortable :
(1) Does he know a- Romish animal answering to
the name of Motu Proprio Decree?
(2) Was one of these animals promulgated at the
beginning of the year, and does it bite Australiat
• (3) Does this animal' automatically, and immedia-
tely excommunicate and deprive of the right of
“spiritual benefits” any Roman Catholic who takes
any steps, directly or indirectly, in a civil or a
criminal matter to bring any clerical person before
a civil or State court?
(4) If any Attorney-General, or Crown Solicitor,
or Crown Prosecutor, or Police Prosecutor, or Judge
takes any ordinary (or extraordinary) legal steps
against any clerical person, does Motu Proprio bite
him ?
(5) If any base Protestant brought a suit against
any clerical person under the forbidden conditions,
and if the jury were, or happened to be, largely, or
partly, Roman Catholic, would the jurymen not he —-
er — reluctant to bring in a verdict against any cleri-
cal person t Also, if they did bring in such a ver-
dict, would the Motu Proprio anim&l bite them?
(6) If this animal was promulgated at the be-
ginning of this year, and if- it does bite Australia,
will Archbishop Kklly kindly say what effect, legal
or moral. Motu Proprio is supposed to have on his
followers ?
(7) —And last, my beloved hearers!— Are the
R.C.’s of Australia hound by the law of Australia, or
by the animal named like a Latin creallure of one
kind or anotherf
I hope there will not be a rush of clerical
persons to “explain.” Questions need only
an, answer. —Yours, Petronius Earbiter.

KATOOMBA
The Metropolis of the Blue Mountains.
FAR from the City’s smoke and turmoil, Katoomba, with its keen, crisp
air, is an ideal resort for health or pleasure. A matter of two hoars’
journey, the train, on its way through the mountains, carries me
through one of the most magnificent scenic panoramas in the world. Deep
gullies with a wealth of ferns and foliage, lie on the left hand far below
the traveller, whilst in dim distance on the horizon may be discerned the
soft, hazy lines of the City. Katoomba is the most convenient centre of many
Mountain Sights, such as the Leura Falls, the majestic Katoomba Falls, the
Valley of the Grose, with its gorgeous sunsets and bewildering color con-
trasts, the Federal Pass, and other famed beauty spots—all of which may
be easily visited on foot.
The Tourist who desires to enjoy his sojourn to the utmost will find in
the “Carrington” —Katoomba’s finest Hotel—a pleasant, comfortable base
equipped with all modern conveniences. Provision has been made for 120
guests, and no expense spared on details that would tend to increase the
pleasure of the visit. Since its inception, the “Carrington” has always been
patronised by all Vice-Regal Representatives. The Bedrooms are beautifully
furnished and lighted from the “Carrington’s” own Electrical Plant; a
Luxurious Lounge, Drawing and Music Rooms, occupy a large section of
the first floor, and a well-appointed Billiard Room provides a source of
amusement for the Gentlemen. At a cost of over £5OOO an up-to-date Bar
has been installed, facing the street. The Kitchen of the “Carrington” is
superintended by an expert Chef, and the cuisine is as perfect as one could
wish. In the Summer months the “Carrington” is ventilated in a thorough
manner. A modern Garage is a feature of the “Carrington,” designed for
the use of Touring Motorists. Equipped with every Motor need ; each Car
kept under separate lock and key. The “Itala” Cars used on the Caves
trip are stalled here.
Summer Tariff, extending from November to April, is £3/10/- per week,
or 12/6 per day. Winter Tariff, from May to October, £3/3/- per week, or
10/- per day. These charges cover full attendance, etc.
The “Carrington” also possesses a modern Hydropathic Department, in
charge of Experts, who are prepared to treat Ladies and Gentlemen in
accordance with Medical instructions. High Frequency, Electric Light,
Vapor and Electric Baths.
DAILY MOTOR TRIPS TO THE
JENOLAN CAVES.
The Jenolan Caves, 47 miles away, are visited every day from the
“Carrington” Hotel. A special fleet of high-powered “Itala” Cars is kept
for this purpose. These Cars are noted for easy riding and reliability, and.
ensure a pleasant, safe trip. Cars leave Katoomba at 9 a.m., and return in
the afternoon. Return Fare. £2.
THE CARRINGTON HOTEL,
KATOOMBA.
City Office: 16a Imperial Arcade, SYDNEY.

PIKE
ERS LTD.,
BRISBANE.
IN
BANANA LAND
“Swagman” dumps Matilda and tells of a
petrified town he knows: Boolburra is its
sleeping label, and its hiding-place is the 7
valley of the Dawson River iC.Q.). Twenty-
five years ago this secluded spot was in-
habited by live aboriginals. To-day it is the
silent abode of a partially-dead white com-
munitv. About 150yds. from the leaning
shanty which does duty as a post office
dreams the local cemetery. I guess it must
be the largest graveyard in the world, for it
has no boundaries of its own, and, therefore,
extends to the fence of the next cemetery
somewhere over the horizon. Some of the
graves are divided from the living world by
a small picket fence; but others are not so
cramped. As I gazed and mused on the
friendliness of the underground townspeople,
a lethargic grey' ’gelding chafed hjs itchy
lower jaw on the “"wooden erbss .that leant
above the resting’place of a pioneer, and
sneezed twice before taking a tuft off grass
from the .mound with large, yellow teeth.
Boolburra possesses a church, and the resi-
dents have planted their cattle-dip less than
50 paces from the door. The result is that
the officiating clergyman has to hold the ser-
vice with one hand and his nose with the
other. At frequent intervals the metropolis
is inundated by the flood-waters of the Daw-
son. Then and only then do the burghers
become animated and shift their feet. From
the high ground they look down mournfully
on the spires and domes, and wait ; for the
waters to subside. When dry times arrive
again they return and shovel out the stink-
ing sediment from the art gallery, theatre,
museum ano Hall of '’Victory. 4 Boolburra
has no hotel,; An adventurer once attempted
to establish a grog shop, but the school-
master said that it would poison the morals,
of the children. The residents, thinking
“morals” was the technical term for
“bowels,” sternly refused the freedom of th£
city to the poison vendor or snake-in-the-
glass. So he departed and became a revolu-
tionist in Central America.
To Northward.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To Northward far where the sunrays shiver
On brown sand beaches and vine-clad tree,
In deep, lone valleys there runs a river,
Through sun and shadow, towards the sea.
And there the palms with their regal crown-
ing—
Their wealth of trailing blossoms hung,
With the spent bloom drooping above and
drowning
Stand, as thev stood when the world was
young.
(With never the smoke of a grimy steamer,
And never the churn of a driving screw;
The white crane out on the sand, a dreamer,
The brown hawk poised in the boundless
blue.)
The vine-clad heights, with the pine trees
lifting
Their stately heads to the winds always,
Or the barque of a native fisher drifting'
Around a bend in the waning days.
And there the days bring forth no dangers
To the wild shy life in the flowering trees;
And there the gulls and the deep sea rangers
Come for rest from the stormy seas.
(A water hen from the rushes peeping,
A wild duck sporting, that takes no heed
Of the suaden sound of a mullet leaping,
The splash of a falling mangrove seed.»
The morn is a dainty bridesmaid, dressing
Her golden hair for the hallowed day ;
The sunset falls like a mother’s blessing,
And sheds its gold on the broad tide why.
And then the night, when the stars are scat-
tered — ,
A wealth of pearls in the water flung,
With dim suggestions of things that mat-
tered
In the long ago, when the heart was young.
(Only the cry of a bittern calling
Somewhere off in the tideswept ways,
The scent of a broken blossom falling—
The still sweet nights, and the dreamy days.)
The lazy tide in the river reaches,
The trailing vine and the towering tree;
And far away on the ocean beaches
The drowsy boom of the dreamy sea.
QUEENSLAND.
Francis Kenna.
Australian Ladies Blackmailed.
PARDONABLE INDISCRETION. HEAVILY
AVENGED.
A woman* who has once committed a certain very
natural apd pardonable indiscretion is ever after-'
wards at the mercy of her own act, unless she learns
—as will be shown in a moment —how to free her-
self.
It is a very obvious figure of speech to say that
any lady who, afflicted by, superfluous hairs on face
or arms, once uses a depilatory or cuts off the hairs
with scissprfj or a razot, can only be freed from the
effects in one way, ; Unless it is treated again and
again with the cutting steel or the burning paste or
fluid, it will crop out again and again and humiliate
her by its degrading, masculine appearance. Just as
a human blackmailer must be paid again and again,
so this silent blackmail of superfluous hair must be
dealt with again and again.
The only escape is by destroying the enemy at the
root: and this caff only be accomplished in one way.
She must send for a self-acting electrolyser which
Mme. Tensfeldt, 120 a Princes Street, Edinburgh,
Scotland, has invented specially for the benefit of
ladies who cannot go to her for treatment. This
apparatus, which only costs £5 55., carriage paid,
will dissolve painlessly the root of every individual
hair. The hair cannot possibly reappear on the same
root: and this work is quite sample and painless.
When the apparatus has done its work it can be
sent back, and 255., the second-hand value, will be
returned in exchange for it, provided this is done
within six months.*
“St. Nick”: Note that “Don Q.” (B.
12/9/T2) says two or three wild things
about a deputation that interviewed Digby
D. regarding a certain stupid law passed
ere the time was ripe. I likewise ob-
serve that he is on the wrong scent.
The deputation did not ask that the
law should be absolutely repealed. What
they desired was that a law similar, but
hardly so drastic, should be made uniform
over Australia. They pointed out the fool-
ishness of demanding that all boots should
be all leather. F’rinstance, ladies evening
shoes and certain classes of house slippers.
Also, they showed that, whereas the Queens-
land retailer could not sell certain boots
under the Queensland Act, his Queensland
customer qould write to N ; .S.W- or arty other
State, for that matter, and obtain tljem by
post. As a matter of fact, the deputation
was merely a living witness of the neces-
sity of passing the Commonwealth Referenda
proposals without further delay, and of the
fact that hasty legislation is not a special
perquisite of the Labor party, as Liberal
organs have A habit of shudderingly inti-
mating to the unsophisticated person who
owns terraces of houses.
THE THING THAT
REMAINED.
HUMOROUS TEAMSTER (to his
mate): “These ’ere motor-cars has come to stay all right. Eh? "
Maryborough (Q.) buried old William
Southerden, the other recent day, setat. 81,
one of its oldest inhabitants. He helped to
build up that rather unassuming town on
the Mary—that is to say, he lived in it, said
it was a good place, and waited for it to
grow. Southerden. was all the things, that
gladden the heart of the usual “local citi-
zen”—from Mayor in ’67 to trustee of the
burial ground. He formed one of the first
Gympie gold escort, which brought the metal
to Maryborough as set forth on page one of
the history of those parts.
The other day representatives of the com-
bined unions of Queensland, assembled in the
Trades Hall forwarded to Prime Minister
Asquith copies of resolutions condemning
the Industrial Peace Bill, and asked the
British Premier to request Governor Maegre-
gor to withhold his assent to the same. This
is a poor attempt at aping the imbecility of
Premier Jack Verran, who also clamored for
British interference with the self-governing
rights of S. Aus. some time ago. Is the Q.
Labor crowd so purblind as to be unable to
see that if Asquith were so demented as to
comply with their request a Tory Prime
Minister of England might similarly meddle
with Q. Labor legislation in the interests of
Fat on some future occasion?
The Mount Morgan branch line is no
longer the best paying in Queensland. It’s
23 miles still give the good return of £lO
3s. lid. per mile, but the steel track from
Stewart’s Greek to Ayr, 44 miles, has scored
£l3 4s. lid., and easily heads the list.
Charters Towers to Ilughenden comes third
with £lO ss. 3d. It is to be noted that the
best dividend payers are not in the south-
east corner of the State where the Queen-
street Ministry spreads its blankets. Trav-
elling is comfortable in that region, but it is
where the public bones are rattled over in-
different tracks in carriages of the dog-box
type that the cash is earned which Drings up
the railway average. Bananaland has in-
creased its lines by 1128 miles in five years,
apd has now a total mileage of 4266. Also,
the total proportional returns are not as
good as they were.
“Kobbi”: Somebody in Winton’s Shire
Council (Queensland) got the idea from the
Chapzander mission, or somewhere, that the
show was not being run on sufficiently godly
lines. He moved, accordingly, that the
weekly squabble be opened with prayer, “as
was done by other Parliaments.” But the
western person has got out of the habit of
praying, and he resented the proposal to
delay the row between Councillor Tonks and
Councillor Beef by even so much as the time
spent in asking the Almighty to see fair
play. Also, he argued that a Shire Council
was a more or less reputable institution, and
had no right to be bracketed with Parlia-
ments and other affairs that badly needed
praying for. The proposal was bumped out
with enthusiasm.
Rock: Nobody seems really to know
whether Kenneth McDonald Grant, of Rock-
hampton, late Minister for Public Instruc-
tion, fell or was pushed. But quite a num-
ber of people think he was pushed, and that
the Queen-street push pushed him.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 21, 1912.
My Dear Tiiargom indaii, —
The Macgregor returned from his jaunt
to Blaekall on Tuesday, bringing. with him
his chieftainess, as is the way with leaders
of this clan. He attended the function,
observance, celebration or shivoo at the
United Service Institution the same even-
ing. On Friday, he opened the show at
Nerang, and to-day (Saturday) the new
Sandgate Town Hall claimed his attention.
And yet there are disloyal sneerers who
hint that Governors don’t work!
Miss Forrest, the younger daughter of
E. B. Forrest, of this region, was married
to Southwell Lord at Suva, Fiji., on Sep-
tember 6. The Bishop of Polynesia at-
tended in all his glory to weld the chains of
matrimonial gladness, and one Langton gave
moral support to the 6ft. 2in. of bridegroom.
There were no bridesmaids, but the bride
worried through in a lace frock under a hat
adorned with yellow roses.
On Wednesday, owing to an incorrect
rumor, or an error of judgment,, or an in-
different guess or something, a large and ex-
pectant crowd gathered at St. Stephen’s
Cathedral to see Phil Forrest and Kathleen
Real permanently hitched under ecclesiasti-
cal sanction. Bitter was the disappoint-
ment when it was discovered that the happy
pair had dodged the eyes of the curious, and
that the said hitching was already all over.
It seems that at 2 p.m. Judge and Mrs.
Real, son Eddy and the daughter bride-lady,
together with Mrs. Forrest and the bride-
groom, and all the absolutely necessary
bridesmaids, groomsmen, best men, clerics
and paraphernalia had wandered into the
place of prayer and wandered forth again in
less than a quarter of an hour with the
whole business transacted. It leaked out
that the bride’s wedding garment was of
Limerick lace and liberty cr@pe, that Miss
Feez and Miss Mann, of Sydney, were the
female accomplices,: and that Roy Murray
and Ted Real helped the groom to bear up
under the stress of the ecclesiastical warn-
ing. The central male person in the observ-
ance presented the lady of his choice, and her
girlish support with diamond trifles set in
simple platinum.
Mrs. J. R. Sankey and her sister, Miss
Brown, worked like the forwards of a League
football team on Thursday, when a coin tea
in aid of the Alexandra Home for destitute
children was held at Mrs. Sankey ? s, pretty
house. In spite of the ungentle and over-
grown zephyrs which sprayed the charitable
enterprise with grit, some £lO was acquired,
and the kiddies in whose interests the cash
was extracted had the time of their lives at
a picnic which was held next day to eat up
the remains of the feast.
On Wednesday afternoon the collective
mother and aunt of the Boys’ Grammar
School arrived in wind-swept parties on the
more or less historic green, and watched the
annual sports with an interest keen but pen-
sive. Cheery little Mrs. Bousfield flitted
about through the throng, and a horde of
flappers from the Girls’ School descended
upon the place of athletic battle and urged
the combatants on to victory, or glory, or
second-wind, or something.
On Friday the Girls’ School itself got on
the warpath, held its annual dance, aired its
best frock, and grew giddy and glad, while
each tender bud swayed in the arms of the
male flapper person of her special choice.
Miss Wilkinson looked dignified in white
crgpe de chine, and the staff wore its party
frock with a grace and charm that dulled
the brightness of the other decorations.
On Tuesday, death came along to claim
Mrs. Miller, wife of banker Miller of our
hamlet. Young, popular, handsome, happy,
it seems as if the grim old person with the
antiquated reaping machine had called her
out of her turn, and two little Australian
children are left without a mother.
To-day (Saturday), the Yachting Club’s
season opened and the river was crowded
with every variety of craft, from paddle-
wheel steamers, whose splash was out of
all proportion to their go, to flying yachts
with their acrobatic crews hanging far out
to balance the swelling sails, and chattering
motor-boats that skipped and zigzagged, care-
less of wind and tide. Nautical costume was
the order of the day on everything afloat,
and on the breakwater a fluttering array of
summer frock shook itself to the breeze.
There were some collisions, bumps and
things, but only a little paint was damaged,
and nobody suffered from anything worse
than the excitement of an expected ship-
wreck which didn’t come off.
The gallant Commercial Travellers have
again allowed a selection of womenfolk to
enter their sacred and baggy portals. The
occasion was a concert at which Mrs. Lee
sang one or two songs, and veteran Joe
Bottger explained his desperate and brigand-
ish propensities in a tuneful voice.
Lena Conly, the third of a series of de-
pressing concert artistes, has been warbling
here this week. She sang mostly to empty
benches, as did Cisneros and Lalla Miranda.
Sally (I mean Kirkby) Lunn will be with
us next week, and, judging by the booking,
should be the most successful of the quar-
tette. This place is apt to wilt under too
liberal supplies of vocal genius.
Yours, amid the turmoil of wealth and
fashion,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Kobbi”: After the general strike in
Queensland the Government felt so pleased
with its John Hop that it swore by several
gods, into the ear of every reporter, that it
would make his lot happy and see that he
became the best paid Dunn in all Australia.
The bloke in the uniform was elated, and in
sheer joy bought a new pair of boots or a
motor-car or a crayfish, or something, and
waited with a glad heart to see his new and
inflated price posted in the Estimates. When
that document duly arrived, the force
dashed nearlv arrested it on a charge of act-
ing in a way calculated to disturb the peace
and provoke anarchy and bloodshed. It
read this way :
(1) A Commissioner’s salary, raised from £BOO
to £lOOO.
(2) Chief inspector, raised £5OO to £6OO.
(3) New billet, senior inspector, £5OO.
(4) Four first-class inspectors, £450 (a rise of
£5O).
(5) Three first-class sub-inspectors, £340 (a rise
of £4O).
(6) Four second-class sub-inspectors, £290 (a
rise of £4O).
(7) Five third-class sub-inspectors, £250.
Then this: “In the non-commissioned ranks
increases are provided which will bring the
men well up to the rates paid in NEW
SOUTH WALES.
If you can afford it, writing plays, taking a
theatre, and producing the literary goods at your
own risk and expense is as good and pleasant a
hobby as stamp collecting, or suburban gardening,
or chronically watching cricket and football matches.
There is a man in England named W. H. G. Nation,
who has been practising this hobby for close on half
a century, and he is still going strong. At latest
he was in possession of Wyndham’s Theatre, London,
where he was running a couple of his peculiar plays
in the presence of skimpy; audiences. Nation rather
fancies himself as a song-writer, and on his present
bills and posters he prints the titles of a dozen of
his songs with the words “By W. H. G. Nation”
after each. His name thus figures on his posters no
less than 15 times, which must be a bit of a record.
It was in 1866 that he started management at Sad-
ler’s Wells, the North London theatre where William
Hoskins (afterwards of Australia) gave the stage-
struck youth, Henry Irving, his first lessons in the
mummer’s art. Nation knew Charles Kean, Samuel
Phelps and that old-time Australian idol, G. V.
Brooke. “These three,” he says, “could not be
matched at the present day. And in comedy we
have no one man who could compare with Buckstone.”
He deplores the change in the public’s taste and the
lack Of interest in the serious drama. Nation is a
wealthy Devonshire squire and a lord of the manor.
He enjoys his rural delights for nine months of the
year, and then he comes to London, takes any theatre
that happens to be available, and runs a season of
two or three months with limited success and a smat-
tering of applause. The list of his plays is tremend-
ously long, and in addition he has published a score
or two of books. London Chronicle starts an inter-
view by describing him as “poet, author, artist,
dramatist, theatrical manager and country gentle-
man.” Nation’s interest in the theatre is hereditary, for
it was his grandfather who discovered Edmund Kean
(then an almost unknown barn-howler), and en-
abled him to achieve his first great success at the
Exeter Theatre. From the Exeter his fame soon
spread to London and beyond.

ALE •
The Wine of the Barley.
FOR countless generations Ale has been a favorite beverage.
It was drunk in Egypt 3000 years before the Christian
era; it was known and appreciated by the ancient Gauls;
it was the favorite liquor of the Anglo-Saxons and Danes, and
is still the premier drink of the most alert and active peoples of
the world. It was the drink of the lusty and strong, the “doers”
in the history of the past.
Cascade Ale is akin to the ale of old England, for.it is rich
in the qualities that promote health and strength. That its merits
are appreciated by those most competent- to judge is proved by
the rapidly-growing sales in every part of the Commonwealth.
Sold at all first-class hotels and stores.

It is not always the best looking watch
that gives the greatest satisfaction. A
smart looking case does not necessarily

“Hermes” writes: —
The Laud Mayor of Sydney is anxious to have a
clean city, but a large proportion of the citizens are
seemingly indifferent on the subject. With a view
to stirring them up, his Washup proposes' to have a
street patrol of 20 officers, who will wander round,
and grasp the delinquent who heaves a tram-ticket
on the pavement, arraign him, find him guilty, fine
him, and collect the penalty on the spot. There
are points about the scheme, although it has its
weak spots. The proposal does not go far enough.
The, patrolmen should also be Authorised Kickers.
The vehicle conductor who drives full tilt past a
stationary tram-car when the whole footboard is a
mass pf alighting women handing out children
should be seized, yanked out of his car, and kicked
hard till his nose bleeds. The indi-
vidual who stands in the middle of a narrow Svdnev
pavement, talking’ to a friend, with his stick slipped
crossways between his back and elbows should not
be spoken to, but just suddenly kicked; he might
also be fined afterwards. When a small boy just
out of knickerbockers is discovered trundling a
handcart loaded up with about 2cwt. of goods, the
name and address of the employer should be ascer-
tained, the goods confiscated, and the employer called
on and kicked. The parties of young hoodlums who
gather at tram stopping-places and corners in the
suburbs, and make remarks about girls and women
who have occasion to pass, should be charged back-
wards by Authorised Kickers, walking on their hands
and hitting out with both feet. The tout outside
the drapery shop should be kicked, and the person
who walks on the wrong side of the pavement, and
the profane individual, and the dropper of orange
peel—in fact, so many people require to be kicked
that I don’t see the end of the business.

ters Burst and Spread, Clothes
Stuck to Them and Caused Great
Suffering. Used Cuticura Soap
and Ointment, Sores Disappeared.
“My two children (ages: girl, 10, and boy
8 years) broke out in sores on the face, arms,
and lower part of the body. The sores came
out in a kind of water blister and would
burst and then become full of matter and
would run and then spread bigger. They
caused great itching and burning and we had
to tie the boy’s hands in cloths at night to
keep him from tearing at them. Their
clothes would stick to the sores on the body
and caused great suffering.
“We took the children to a doctor and for
two months we used his remedies but they
not only did no good, but the sores got worse.
I saw in the papers of the cures effected by
Cuticura Ointment, so I got a box of Oint-
ment and the first application gave such
relief that we kept on with it. My wife used
to wash the sores two or three times a day
with warm water and Cuticura Soap and then
apply the Cuticura Ointment each time and
in a month the sores disappeared, leaving a
red blotch on the skin. We kept on with
the Cuticura Soap and Cuticura Ointment
and their skins were soon free of all marks.
I think the Cuticura Ointment a wonderful
cure and feel sure that if we had not used
the Cuticura Remedies there is no telling
how long the children would have suffered.
I can safely recommend Cuticura Remedies.”
(Signed) Thomas Christie, 22 Ashmore St.,
Erskinevilie, Sydney, N. S. W., Dec. 10,1910.

Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When 1 had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time—yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. 1
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing, com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have , actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world —I am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest;

The Inky Way.
“Lud”: The Times cock-a-doodle-doos over No.
40,000. Established 1788, by John Walter the First
(1739-1812). Not a printer or journalist, but an
underwriter, who lost a fortune through French cap-
ture of merchant ships. In 1803, John Walter the
Second (1784-1847), the great one, took charge of
the Times. Refused to take the foreign news
ladled out by Government to the press. Walter or-
ganised his own system. Refused an offer of exclu-
sive Government intelligence, in exchange for
consistent support. Refused almost everything.
Times printed by steam, 1814. Barnes editor in
1816, assisted by Sterling, called the Thunderer.
Delane followed Barnes. The third John Walter
took the ribbons in 1847. Good man, but not up to
the second, and the paper needed genius. The Times
was the Times up till about 1867, when it began
to be threatened with real competition, and now
what is it? A shop, like the Salvation Army. De-
lane’s time of efficiency was up when he shunted,
but the paper slumped awfully under Chenery, an
Arabic Professor, nick-named Tiglath-Pileser by
Yates in the World. After Tiglath, the proprietary
went back to something like another young Delane,
in Buckle. Now it is G. G. Robinson. Doesn’t mat-
ter. In 1906 a company took over the business,
with Northcliffe, of the Mail, as chief boss. I suppose
the paper will become the Mail-Times, or the
Times-Mail, or simply the Tail.
* * * *
John Corlett wrote a farewell article when he
quitted the London Sporting Times or Pink ’Un,
which he had owned and edited for 39 of its 48
years. He mentioned that the paper paid from the
start; its capital “was a £6 note, and was never
fully drawn upon.” Also the largest verdict ever
given against it in a libel suit was one farthing, “and
the Judge said that even that was too much.” The
Pink ■’Un has lately dropped its motto, of “Old
Toryism, Old Port, High Church, and High Farm-
ingj for ever!” but it is “to be carried on on the
same lines as before.” This, if correct, means that:
(1) its pet original stories will all be of the bright-
blue or smoking-room order; (2) it will sling
savage sneers at all democratic movement; (3) It
will keep on stealing from The Bulletin without
acknowledgment.
* * * *
Lord Morley, better and more respectfully known as
plain John Morley, lately presided over a journalistic
dinner in the London Hotel Cecil. He made a pass-
ing allusion to the Commonwealth law requiring
political articles and reports at election times to
be signed with the names and addresses of the
writers. John has only a hazy idea of this law and
its scope: “On this point of anonymity he was told
the other day, and he believed it was true, that in
either the Commonwealth of Australia or one of the
provinces, they had passed some regulation to this
effect, which he commended respectfully to the at-
tention of the company, that in the period before a
general election every article should be signed.” He
gave some interesting reminiscences of the period
when he was editing London’s leading evening paper,
the Pall Mall Gazette. His assistant, understudy
and successor in that post, was W. T. Stead, of the
spook Julia and the Titanic, to whose memory he
paid a tribute. There never was, he said, a more
heroic spirit. Whether Stead made the best use of
his genius was a question which he would not dis-
cuss at the moment. But there was no doubt Stead
had a tremendous opinion of the functions of a jour-
nalist, and he showed it by . taking for his tele-
graphic address the word “Vatican.” He had really
and truly convinced himself that he could be the
centre of a new spiritual power akin to that of the
Leos and the Gregorys who had exercised world-
wide jurisdiction from the great Papal Palace in
Rome. Morley proceeded to dilate on the changes
in the journalistic world since he was promoted
straight from the editorial chair of the P.M.G. into
Gladstone’s Cabinet. On the whole, he thinks the
press has distinctly improved during recent years,
but he deprecates some of its developments. He
does not like the “flaming garish colors, the dashing
emphasis and the Brobdignagian attempts in type of
all kinds,” to which certain sensational sheets—the
Daily Mail and its numerous satellites —are prone.
He thinks the “unrest of the present age is traceable
in some degree to this sort of distracting printed
shriek.” And he uttered one pessimistic note. He
had n,ever advised anybody to take up journalism as
a profession. “It was so precarious and insecure,
so subject to the caprices of time and fortune.” Yet
there are other professions that are equally sudden.
Suppose a man brought up his son to Hooleyism!
“Linesman”: The Melbourne Herald is not what
it used to be. The closing days of many an old
dodderer in the Benevolent Asylum were once
brightened by the interest he took in solving the
puzzle-competitions which it formerly used as head-
ings. “How, When and Where” was its usual cap-
tion for a murder, and if it was not dead sure that
murder had been committed, then would it ask, in fat
black pica, “Suicide, or What?” Aforetime, nobody
ever died in the columns of that paper. They either
“passed,” like euchre players with poor hands, or
“crossed the bar,” like glasses in an hotel shindy.
At Show time, the bucolic eye of the farmer, who
had come to Melbourne for the joy of having his
boots blacked by a real night porter in a real
coffee palace, was wont to be gladdened by the
spread-head “Agricola.” The spielers and the guns
were not dead sure that it was really Gup time until
Field-Admiral Reay, then editor, had announced the
fact under the ingenious and scholarly crosshead,
“King Equus,” as evidence of his Latinity; nor
was a petitioner certain of his divorce before the
granting of the decree nisi was reported under
“Breaking the Bonds.” “Retribution” headed the
sentences handed out in the Crim. and the Sessions,
while “News and Notes in a Nutshell” was the allit-
erative frieze that decorated the summary of the
day’s happenings. “The Fire Fiend” and “The Dust
Devil,” standing headings, kept the printer’s devils
warm through many a hot summer; and no account
of a wedding was regarded as fit to pass the
eye of Mrs. Grundy until the fragrant and romantic
caption “Orange Blossoms” ratified its respectability
and ensured the sanctity of the marriage tie. No:
the Herald is not what it used to be. Even the
newsboys give it its aspirate nowadays, and on
Thursdays it runs a literary column.
TO THE EDITOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Who are these mystic blighters,
These weird, fantastic writers,
Who decorate your pages every week?
We know each nom de plume
From Cooktown round to Broome,
But it’s inside information that we seek.
So will you kindly tell
Who “KODAK” is and “SNELL,”
And who the deuce “PHILANDER F.” may be?
Who grills the morning chop
For “SATAN” and for “HOP,”
Who brews “RELIGIOUS EDITOR” his tea?
You might mention, by the way,
If “0.K.” is just “0.K.,”
Some anecdotes of “WANG” and “BILLY B.”
And tell us if “MACHETE”
Is one of the elite,
And give us “CURSE O’ MOSES’ ” pedigree.
Of straight names there are plenty,
Such as ADAMS, MORTON, HENTY,
DYSON, 11. McCRAE and ROBERT BROTHERS,
And a dozen more or so,
But it’s little more we know
Of them than what we know about the others.
We should like to know what manner
Of a woman is “JOHANNA” ;
And is she of the brand that’s known as “new” ?
And if the poet BRADY
Is a man or just a lady,
And lastly, who and what and how are YOU?
QUEENSLAND.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad
to hear of a simple and scientific device which will
restore your hearing, no matter how old you are.
.There is no mystery or secrecy about it; we describe
it fully and explain just how it acts.
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and in the
same way you may use this sound magnifier as an
aid to weak hearing. But, unlike spectacles, the
Wilson Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and
comfortable. You can’t tell you are wearing them.
No one else can, either, because they are not seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off
them—the strain of trying to hear dim sounds.
What a blessed relief to have done with that
tiring strain !
Being made of the purest and softest Para rubber
they cannot be felt, even when the head rests on a
pillow. For the same reason, a blow on the side of
the head will not hurt the ear. In fact, they pro-
tect the ear, the tender inner parts that are so
senstive, from cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing
sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why these
facts are true, and why the Sound Magnifier restores
the hearing, we give an illustration of one as it lies
in the ear. You will notice that the Sound Magni-
fv
fier is shaped
like a trumpet,
and so con-
veys even the
faintest sounds
right to the
natural e a r-
drum. Being
of the softest
rubber, it can-
not hurt the
ear. It fits the
orifice of the
ear with preci-
sion and com-
fort. The di-
vision that
runs across the
mouth is to al-
low the remo-
val and inser-
tion of the
Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
T h e shape
of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has
been chosen
after years of
experience a s
the best adapt-
eel to carry the faintest sound right down to the
natural ear-drum.
To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not only
trying to have people shout at them, but it hurts
the ear-drum. When age comes the ear-drum some-
times gets thickened, or loses its tightness, and
shouting does not act properly on it. It only
makes a confused and tiring noise. But the
Sound Magnifiers concentrate the sound in the best
spot for perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-nerve
completely, or were born deaf, we cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could do that, in spite
of some of the absurd claims made to-day. But if
you can hear the faintest sound, do not despair.
You will never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people in
Australia and New Zealand, who have been cured,
and you can be cured, too. You may wear the Ear-
Drums day and night., and take them out whenever
you wish. Children can wear them (we have spe-
cial sizes for children from six to twelve). Even
a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they
are only sold in pairs), a fine_nickel remover and
also an inserter. After the first pair is bought you
may purchase a single drum at any time for 8s.,
but the pair you get at first will last quite
two years. Do not ask for a trial pair. We do
not send them out on trial, and so they are never
secondhand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Outfit will be sent you at once, with the fullest
instructions. Don’t delay, but have the comfort of
good hearing as soon as you can.

The Modern Wreck.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sea-travelling has not by any means reached the
point of absolute safety. The men who do business
in the great deep must still be prepared to meet the
inevitable With the nonchalance, of the eai’iy Voyagers.
— cdmment.
HK Mtvfes ifrefe singing to the breeze,
I The hiingfy deep had claimed its owd*
J-- It was but fight it should have
fclfiimed it,
And ho ohe vefy greatly blamed it;
For ih the middle of the seas
The Ship was sitting on a stbne.
The witter foamed along the deck
Like liqhids in a pewter cup,
The purser took it rather sadly,
The first mate swore about it badly,
The cook remarked it was a wreck,
Which summed the situation up.
So wet it was, the captain said :
“Ho, bring my green umbrella aft!
And launch some boats upon the ocean,
Extravagant as seems the notion,
Some of us may be shortly dead
If we remain on this old craft.”
They launched the boats and left the
ship,
The captain only stayed behind,
He sat Upon the bridge serenely,
His large umbrella glimmered greenly,
A pleasant smile was on his lip
As if he really didn’t mind.
The boats were damp as damp could be,
And though they tried to bail them dry
They could not make the matter better,
For every moment they got wetter,
Until they foundered in the sea
And everybody had to die.
The skipper sat and smoked his pipe,
And drank his rum to keep him brave;
And when the old ship started breaking
HiS fioble thirst he still was slaking,
Hg merely said, “The time is ripe,”
And floated down a lonely wave.
This is the way things should be done;
The Wide sea teaches men to end
Ip Simple, serious, noble fashion;
Beyond all hope and all compassion
They meet the death they cannot shun
While othefs draw the dividend.

CURSE O’ MOSES.
The Thirteenth Juror.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MU. dUSTICU HARRON climbed the
sandy way up from the bathing box
on the long sickle of Hobson’s Bay
beach. To the north the red roofs of
Brightoii showed among the pines. Behind
him the sun-haze made the bay a shield of
ruffled steel. Before him the beautiful forms
of the flowering ti-tree descended to a carpet
of green and chrome-lush grass and the
yellow ribbons of the. Cape weed. Despite
the greyness of his beard, there was about
him the air of a man in his first prime. He
walked up the banks of yielding sand on
his toes; his step was springy as he reached
the level walking of the height. There he
stopped, ankle deep in the glossy, yellow
carpet, and looked at the isles of ti-tree re-
peated to infinity, shafted with sunlight and
dappled with gloom.
The old and recurrent memory came now
to spoil his holiday—a memory that was
always the skeleton of his feast —the ever
present knowledge of a betrayal that said
to him always: “Be not lifted up! For
this thing has been, and your life is failure.
He had succeeded for a week in seeming
to forget—except when alone at night, that
time and place which finds a man out, and
shows him himself as he is. _ And now he
had remembered, when enjoying the perfec-
tion of physical health, and walking in a
kind of sober intoxication at the beauty of
the world —newborn this spring. And there
was a month yet before he had to return to
his judicial work in a northern State. At
the prospect of four weeks of doing nothing
but remembering ne was almost afraid.
For memory showed him this. Himself
a struggling lawyer. The girl who meant
the world and Heaven to him, radiating sun-
shine, bringing gaiety to his study—a butter-
fly among the books. Too much a butterfly
in other ways maybe • shirking mother-
hood, spinning not nor toiling—making her
love of life a snare for life. Her name
linked —almost certainly without justice—
with a man of that growing class of doctor
who is appointed to save life and grows
rich by killing—-by murder of the human in
its blindest and most helpless stage.
And then —a discovery of his own self-
content and his immersion in work —too late.
She had said to him once: “Did you ever
read a book called ‘Too Much Alone’?” and
he had replied, with only a glance _ in her
direction: “No, my dear —life and its pro-
blems take all my waking moments. I
haven’t time for romances.”
And now, whether he would or no, he had
to find time for romance, since he had
burned his own romance unread. A hus-
band awakened too late, and a woman who
was sunshine gone to darkness when she
should have been a smiling mother with a
laughing child.
And the man who had destroyed the two
lives under cover of the license of the pro-
fession he prostituted went his way, not
only unpunished but even rewarded —rich
with the price of blood, honored as a dis-
creet and successful murderer nf the race.
Into the Judge’s eyes, as he thought of it,
came a weariness that took the youth from
him, and was suited to the greyness of his
beard.
The weariness was succeeded by fierceness
—the craving to see a credit entry against
that scoundrel whose debits were all so
black. And then Mr. Justice Harron smiled
grimly and said: “One day.”
He had been saying it for 15 years, won-
dering how he could ever meet that day;
for the mal-practitioner who had killed
Harron’s wife had let his northern practice
drift after that last exposure, and was now
mostly in Melbourne—but richer and more
successful than ever. And in a month Mr.
Justice Harron would return to his work,
and might never see his enemy again. Yet
with the strongest of hopes, which is revenge,
he said again, “One day.”
The day had come! It was to-day.
In the garden of his hired cottage near
Black Rock that night he read the morning
p a pe r —for he was half a day behind the
world —and the holiday habit left the chron-
icle of the world's pretensions and its misery
until the day had taken all he had Wofth the
giving. 1
He read cflfelessly—selecting by ittstiflct ;
and habit the law hews. “Commonwealth
High Court Appeal! Argument before
State Full Court! Judgment iU Admiralty
And theU the scare-heads caught him: —
THE MYRTLE fiOWNES INQUEST.
VERDICT OF WILFUL MURDER.
bR. ANTIOCHUS COMMITTED FOR TRIAL.
Harron, with the old ache renewed in its
first agony, looked at her photograph, which
was all of home he carried with him, felt
the appeal of her wistful glance_ at him,
knew that more than half her failure had
been his own —and planned. He had yet a
month to fight in—four weeks before work
would claim him again, and this time work
would be helped by a new forgetfulness.
“lie who cannot revenge himself is con-
temptible. He who can and will not is
despicable,” said Harron.
Later on, a suburban barber had a visitor
who was unexpectedly fastidious —for that
suburb, at least; and when a beardless,
clean-shaven man left the chair the identity
of Mr. Justice Harron was lost in the super-
ficial change.
The Criminal Court Sessions opened a
week later, and the list was short. “The
King against Antiochus” commenced 10 days
after the committal for trial. There was a
heavy jury-panel, and the prisoner exhausted
his challenge early. The State challenges
were almost complete when the crier called
the name of “Henry Strange.” The clear-
cut, student’s face of Henry Strange, said to
be an iron-moulder, did not give the cue to
his occupation; his white, strong, nervous
hands looked as if they might mould men,
but not iron. But the appearance of many
men unfits their pursuits. In any circum-
stance the State had no reason to challenge
this eminently _ respectable man. And
Henry Strange, iron moulder, entered the
jury box. When the jury was invited to
elect a foreman, Strange was appointed
almost without a word. A grocer juryman
nudged a builder juryman, and, with a finger
pointed at Henry Strange, said “Him!” So
Henry Strange was appointed foreman.
The prisoner had scarcely looked at the
men who held his life in their hands. He ap-
peared nonchalant, though he may have been
afraid; but, to help him there were the ex-
periences of his own and other malpracti-
tioners —men charged with this method of
murder, which is so common as to be almost
legal, and whose proofs are so elusive as to
brake the murderer immune. Antiochus was
a pitcher who had been often to the well;
and Hope and Experience told him that he
would not be broken. He talked much to
his Solicitor, who stood deferentially by the
bars of the dock, and at short intervals
prompted his counsel; and he smiled to re-
assure himself that all the byways and loops
and side-tracks of the law were meant to
help him, and that justice was still
sleeping far away. In effect, his attitude
said: “I cannot prove myself innocent; but
I defy you to prove me guilty by the forms
of law.”
The examination in chief of the leading
witness for the State, a young man whose
attempt to rescue Myrtle Downes from con-
ventional shame had ended in her most un-
conventional destruction —was at an end.
Cross-examination shook it slightly. There
was no need for more than that. The un-
corroborated testimony of an accessory
before the fact bade fair to end in the usual
way—the failure of the State case, the
acquittal of the prisoner; congratulations,
and a new advertisement of him as a man
“safe” and immune.
And then, as the young man, who was
repentant because exposure had come for him
as a penalty of death had come to Myrtle
Downes, prepared to leave the witness box,
a new voice spoke and brought to Antiochus
his first thrill of fear.
It was a voice measured, cultured, digni-
fied —the voice of a man wbo knew his posi-
tion, privilege and responsibility—the voice
of Henry Strange, iron moulder and fore-
man of the jury. But that which made Dr.
Antiochus most afraid was that the voice
was familiar, or had been—not with the
familiarity of the voice of the man you meet
every day, or of the friend whose utterance
you could recognise after long parting, but as
a voice he seemed to have known ma,ny years
ago. _ The prisoner looked at the foreman of
the jury, but saw nothing familiar in his
face.
“A question, your Honor,” said Henry
Strange. “Witness, you went to the prisoner
before you brought Myrtle Downes to him?”
“Yes, sir.”
“How did you pay—by cheque?”
“No, sir—he refused a cheque.”
“Why? Did he give any reason?”
“Only that it could be traced. Later, I
took him the money in bank notes, and he
refused them.”
“Then how did you pay?”
“In gold.”
“Did you give him the gold while you were
in his house?”
“Yes—in the surgery.”
“Did he do anything with it?”
“He examined every sovereign under a
microscope.”
“For what reason?”
“To see if they were marked, I think.”
“I object,” said the prisoner’s counsel, and
the Judge upheld the objection; but the
prisoner knew that no objection could re-
move the answer, and felt with a new sensa-
tion of terror that the foreman of the jury
knew him—knew his ways and methods, and
had stripped his soul naked before them all.
“Just another question,” said Henry
Strange. “Did he give you a receipt?”
“No, sir.”
“That will do. Your Honor, the jury
would like the prisoner’s bankers examined,
to see what gold was paid into his account
that day.”
It was the hint oblique to the State Prose-
cutor, and he took it, despite the defending
counsel’s objection, and the statement that
all the banks in the world would prove
nothing either for or against the prisoner.
Yet, when on the second day that evidence
came, and £9O in gold were shown to have
been paid in, the - jury read it along with
the fact of two other admissions to Antio-
chus’s hospital on the day of the admission
of Myrtle Downes, and read both facts
against the prisoner. For a jury, having
little law, has much room for the deductions
of common-sense; and hangs men often be-

extended to the arms, finally spreading all over the
back. These blisters w’ould burst and then form
into the shape of ringworms. The irritation nearly
drove me mad, and I felt I could tear myself to
pieces. It was torture indescribable, for I could
not obtain a moment’s rest, and I became run-dow'n
in health, and felt depressed and despondent. I

adding to my misery.

intended. It is invaluable for both children and'adults.
